Selling the Ma'dhun Slave's Body for Debt: A Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute on attaching debt to slave's body

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on selling the ma'dhun's body for debt, explain the opposing view of Zufar and al-Shafi'i, and apply the principle of daf' al-ḍarar to justify the Hanafi ruling.

Core Idea
The slave's debt attaches to his body, not just his earnings – a ruling based on the principle of repelling harm.

This lesson explores a key dispute in Hanafi fiqh: Can a slave who is permitted to trade (ma'dhun) be sold to pay off his debts? While Zufar and al-Shafi'i limit recovery to the slave's earnings, Abu Hanifa rules that the debt attaches to the slave's body itself. The rational basis is the maxim of repelling harm (daf' al-ḍarar), which protects creditors and encourages trade.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what Abu Hanifa rules regarding the slave's body and earnings for debt repayment. List the types of debts mentioned: hire, deposit, usurpation, etc.
All these obligations attach to the slave's body (raqaba) and earnings (kasb).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Zufar (a Hanafi student) and al-Shafi'i disagree. State their ruling: only earnings can be sold, not the body. Explain their reasoning: the master's aim is to acquire wealth, not lose what he has.
Opponent's dalil: the master's presumed intention (gharaḍ) is profit, not loss of property.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the opposing argument carefully: selling the body defeats the master's purpose of permitting trade, which is to gain profit without risking the slave's capital value.
This argument is based on a literal reading of the master's interest.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is based on istihsan: the debt appears in the right of the master, so it attaches to the body to repel harm from creditors. This encourages trade and ultimately benefits the master.
The 'illa is 'daf' al-ḍarar 'an al-nās' (repelling harm from people).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi law, the slave can be sold and the price distributed among creditors; under the opposing view, only the earnings are used, and the slave remains unsold.
This difference affects the master's risk and creditors' recovery.

Source Text

[٢]قوله : وبكسبه؛ أي يتعلق دين المأذون بكسبه، ويقسم أيضاً بين الغرماء بالحصص، وقد اكتفى عن ذكرِهِ بذكرِ تقسيم ثمن العبد. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي) [٣] قوله : دفعاً للضّرر عن الناس؛ وهذا لأنَّ سببه التجارة، وهي داخلة تحت الإذن، وتعلّق الدين برقبته استيفاء حامل على المعاملة. فمن هذا الوجه صلح غرضاً للمولى، وينعدم الضرر في حقه بدخول المبيع في
Hire and renting, and compensation for a deposit, and usurpation, and an entrusted item that was denied, and the dowry of intercourse with a purchased female slave after the claim of ownership—these attach to his body, so he is sold for it, and its price is divided according to shares, and by his earnings whether acquired before the debt or after, and by what is given to him as a gift. This is our position. Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: He is not sold for the debt, but his earnings are sold, because the master's aim is to acquire wealth that was not there, not to lose what he had. Our proof: The debt appears in the right of the master, so it attaches to his body to repel harm from people. The sold item is validly owned, and the price perishes in his hand. The form of debt by hire is that he takes the advance payment, then the lessee dies before the end of the term, so the lessee gets back what he gave; this is a debt that befalls him due to the hire contract. And he mentioned 'trust' after 'deposit' because trust is more general than deposit. Thus in Hāshiyat al-Jalabī, quoting al-Nihāyah. [1] His saying 'and a trust': He mentioned trust after deposit because trust is more general than deposit. Among the types of trusts are the capital of muḍārabah, ʿāriyah, bidāʿah, and partnership property. These things, when denied, turn into usurpation, and the liability incurred by these things is liability for usurpation because the trustee becomes a usurper of the trust by denial. Thus in al-Kifāyah. [2] His saying 'and by his earnings': i.e., the debt of the permitted slave attaches to his earnings, and it is also divided among the creditors according to shares, and he sufficed with mentioning the division of the slave's price. Thus in Hāshiyat al-Jalabī. [3] His saying 'to repel harm from people': This is because its cause is trade, which falls under the permission, and the attachment of the debt to his body is a stimulus for trade. From this aspect, the master's interest is served, and harm is removed from him by the entry of the sold item into
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute within Hanafi fiqh. The text begins by listing various debts that attach to the slave's body (raqabah) and earnings (kasb) according to Abu Hanifa. Zufar and al-Shafi'i disagree, arguing that only the earnings can be sold, not the body itself, since the master's goal is profit, not loss. The Hanafi response is that the debt, having arisen in the master's right, must attach to the body to protect creditors from harm—this is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on the principle of repelling harm (dafʿ al-ḍarar). The passage then clarifies that 'trust' (amānah) is broader than 'deposit' (wadīʿah), and denial turns the trustee into a usurper. The attachment of debt to earnings means creditors share proportionally. The harm-based reasoning explains why the body can be sold: it encourages trade and benefits both master and creditors.

Key Terms
رقبة (raqaba): The physical body of the slave, distinct from his earnings or property.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who is permitted by his master to engage in trade.
غرماء (ghurama): Creditors to whom the slave owes debts from trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on selling a ma'dhun slave's body (raqaba) to pay off his trade debts?
A) It is not permitted; only the earnings are sold.
B) It is permitted because the debt attaches to the body to protect creditors.
C) It is only permitted if the master agrees.
D) It is prohibited because the slave is property.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits selling the ma'dhun slave's body to satisfy trade debts, based on the principle of repelling harm.

Master's Right to Take Levy from Indebted Ma'dhun Slave
Ruling on master's levy despite slave's debt with istihsan proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the master's right to take a customary levy (ghullah) from a ma'dhun slave with debts, explain the istihsan reasoning that overrides qiyas, and identify the conditions for the levy.

Core Idea
The master may take a customary levy despite slave's debts, by istihsan, to preserve trade permission and benefit creditors.

When a ma'dhun slave trades with the master's permission and incurs debts, a conflict arises: creditors have priority over earnings, yet the master expects some income. Hanafi fiqh resolves this by istihsan (juristic preference), allowing the master to take a customary monthly levy. This lesson unpacks the qiyas vs. istihsan reasoning and the practical conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and apparent conflict
The act is the master taking a periodic levy (ghullah) from the slave's earnings while the slave has debts. The conflict: creditors' right to priority over earnings vs. master's right to some income.
2
State the qiyas ruling
By qiyas (analogy), taking the levy is not allowed because the debt takes priority over the master's right in earnings.
Qiyas would prohibit the levy.
3
State the istihsan ruling and its basis
By istihsan, the master may take the levy. The basis: allowing the levy benefits creditors by keeping the slave's trade permission active, ensuring continued earnings. If the master cannot take anything, he might revoke permission, spoiling the door of earning.
Istihsan overrides qiyas here.
4
Identify conditions for the levy
The levy must be customary (like a monthly tax) and not excessive. Any extra beyond the levy goes to creditors.
5
Note the practical consequence
The master continues to take the levy; creditors get the remaining earnings. If earnings are insufficient, the master may sell the slave's body (after emancipation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المأذون أحكامه لا بما أخذه سيده منه قبل الدين، وطولب بما بقي بعد عنقه، وللسيد أخذ غلةِ مثلِهِ مع وجود دين، وما زاد للغرماء ( لا بما أخذه سيده منه قبل الدين، وطولب بما بقي بعد عتقه) : أي إذا قضى دينَهُ من ثمن رقبته إذا بيعت، ومن كسبه، فإن بقي شيء من الدِّينِ طولاً بـ إذا عتق. [2] (وللسيد" أخذ غلة" مثله مع وجودِ دين، وما زاد للغرماء ملكه، وتعلقه بالكسب لا يُنافي تعلقه بالرقبة، فيتعلّق بهما، غير أنه يبدأ بالكسب في الاستيفاء إيفاء لحقِّ الغرماء، وإبقاء لمقصود المولى، وعند انعدامه يستوفى من الرقبة. كذا في «الهداية». [1] قوله : لا بما أخذه سيده...الخ ; إشارة إلى الفرق بين الكسب الذي يبدأ به بأداء المأذون، وهو الذي عبر عنه المصنف له بقوله : وبكسبه حصل...الخ، وبين الكسب الذي لا يصرفُ إلى أداء دينه، أصلاً، وهو الذي قبضه مولاه قبل لحوق الدين به. دين كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» [٢] (٢) قوله : وللسيد ... الخ ; أي للمولى أن يأخذ الغلة وهي الضريبة التي يضرب المولى على العبد كل شهر مع قيام الدين عليه استحساناً، والقياس أن لا يجوز؛ لأنَّ الدين مقدم على حق المولى في الكسب. وجه الاستحسان : إن في ذلك نفع الغرماء؛ لأنَّ حقهم يتعلق بمكاسبه، ولا تحصل المكاسب إلا ببقاء الإذن في التجارة، ولو منعناه عن أخذ الغلة يحجر عليه، فيفسد باب الاكتساب. كذا في «الكفاية». (٣) قوله : غلة ; الغلة : كلّ ما يحصل من ريع الأرض أو كرائها أو أجرة غلام أو نحو ذلك. كذا في «العناية». [٤]قوله : للغرماء ; لعدم الضرورة فيها، وتقدّم حقهم.
Book of the Permitted Slave: Its Rulings. Not including what his master took from him before the debt, and he is held liable for what remains after his emancipation. The master may take the levy of his like despite the existence of debt, and what is extra is for the creditors. (Not including what his master took from him before the debt, and he is held liable for what remains after his emancipation): i.e., when he pays his debt from the price of his body if it is sold, and from his earnings, and if anything of the debt remains, he is held liable for it upon emancipation. [2] (And the master may take the levy of his like despite the existence of debt, and what is extra is for the creditors. The attachment of debt to earnings does not negate its attachment to the body, so it attaches to both, but he begins with earnings in collection to fulfill the creditors' right and preserve the master's objective; when earnings are absent, it is collected from the body. Thus in al-Hidāyah. [1] His saying: 'Not including what his master took...' etc.; it is a reference to the difference between the earnings with which the permitted slave begins payment—which the author expressed by his saying 'and by his earnings obtained...' etc.—and the earnings that are not used at all to pay his debt, which is what his master took before the debt attached to him. Thus in Ḥāshiyat al-Jalabī. [2] His saying: 'And the master...' etc.; i.e., the master may take the levy, which is the periodic tax the master imposes on the slave every month, even while the debt exists, by istiḥsān (juristic preference). By qiyās (analogy), it is not permissible because the debt takes priority over the master's right in earnings. The basis of istiḥsān is that it benefits the creditors because their right attaches to his earnings, and earnings are not obtained except by the continuation of the permission to trade; if we prevent him from taking the levy, it would be a restriction on the slave, and the door of earning would be spoiled. Thus in al-Kifāyah. (3) His saying: 'levy'; levy is everything obtained from the yield of land, its rent, or the wages of a servant, or the like. Thus in al-ʿInāyah. [4] His saying: 'for the creditors'; because there is no necessity for it, and their right takes precedence.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the master's right to collect a periodic levy (ghullah) from a ma'dhun slave who has debts. The text first clarifies that anything the master took from the slave before the debt arose is not used to pay creditors; the slave starts with his current earnings. Then, the core ruling: the master may take a monthly levy even while debts exist—this is istihsan (juristic preference). The opposing qiyas would forbid it because the creditors' right to earnings should have priority. The istihsan reasoning is that allowing the master to take the levy keeps the slave motivated and able to trade, which ultimately benefits creditors by generating more earnings. If the master were prevented from taking anything, he might revoke the trade permission, ending all earnings. The definition of ghullah is provided: all income from land, rent, or labor. Finally, any increase beyond the customary levy goes to the creditors.

Key Terms
غلة (ghullah): A periodic tax or levy the master imposes on the slave, typically monthly, from the slave's earnings.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas for a stronger reason, here preservation of earning capacity.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning that would forbid taking the levy because debt takes priority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the master's right is absolute over the slave's earnings.
B) Because by istihsan, taking the levy encourages the master to keep the trade permission, ultimately benefiting creditors.
C) Because the debt is not yet due.
D) Because the slave's earnings are not attached to the debt.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi istihsan permits the master to take a customary levy from an indebted ma'dhun slave to preserve the trade permission, benefiting creditors in the long run.

Revocation of an Authorized Slave's Permission by Absconding
Inter-madhhab dispute on absconding slave's authorization

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on revocation of an authorized slave's permission upon absconding, explain the Shafi'i counter-argument, and apply the Hanafi concept of 'dalalah al-hajar' to determine when implied restriction applies.

Core Idea
Absconding implies the master's displeasure, so the slave's authorization is revoked — unless the master explicitly consents otherwise.

This passage addresses a specific scenario in sales (buyu‘): what happens to a slave's trading authorization if the slave runs away. The Hanafi school holds that the authorization is automatically revoked due to an implied restriction, while Imam al-Shafi‘i disagrees. Understanding this dispute illuminates the Hanafi method of deriving rulings from circumstantial evidence and the limits of implied meanings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a slave absconding; the Hanafi ruling is that his authorization to trade is revoked (inhijar).
2
Locate the Shafi‘i objection
Shafi‘i argues absconding does not negate the permission itself, because the two are separate.
3
Trace the Hanafi dalil
The master's displeasure is inferred from the rebellion; this is 'dalalah al-hajar' (implied restriction).
The key concept is that the law infers restriction from circumstances.
4
Note the exception
If the master explicitly authorized the slave after absconding, the implied restriction is overridden.
Explicit permission shows the master's satisfaction.
5
Subsidiary issue: return does not revive authorization
Even if the slave returns, the authorization does not automatically revive. The correct Hanafi view is it remains revoked.
This is mentioned in al-‘Inaya as the sound position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينحجر إن أبق، هذا عندنا، وعند الشافعي: لا ينحجر؛ لأن الإباق لا ينافي الإذن، فإنه يصح إذن الآبق، ولنا: إن دلالة الحجر قائمة؛ لأن المولى لا يرضى بإسقاط حقه حال تمرده، أما إذا أذنه صريحاً فهو يفوت دلالة الحجر. وإن عاد العبد من الإباق، هل يعود الإذن؟ لم يذكره محمد، والصحيح أنه لا يعود. كذا في «العناية».
And [the authorized slave] becomes restricted if he absconds. This is with us [Hanafis], and with al-Shafi‘i: he does not become restricted because absconding does not negate authorization, for the authorization of an absconding slave is valid. And for us: the indication of restriction exists because the master is not pleased to drop his right during his rebellion. But if he explicitly authorized him, that nullifies the indication of restriction. And if the slave returns from absconding, does the authorization return? Muhammad did not mention it, and the correct [view] is that it does not return. Thus in al-‘Inaya.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with an inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi position is that an authorized slave’s permission to trade is revoked if he absconds. Imam al-Shafi‘i disagrees, arguing that absconding does not contradict the permission itself, and an absconding slave can still be validly authorized. The Hanafi response relies on the concept of 'dalalah al-hajar' (implied restriction): the master, in his normal displeasure with the slave's rebellion, would not consent to the slave continuing to trade. However, if the master explicitly authorizes the absconding slave (i.e., gives permission after absconding), the express permission overrides the implied restriction because the master's explicit consent shows his satisfaction. The text also clarifies a subsidiary issue: if the slave returns after absconding, the authorization does not automatically revive; this is the correct position in the school, as reported in al-‘Inaya.

Key Terms
إباق (ibaq): Absconding of a slave from his master without permission.
دلالة الحجر (dalalah al-hajar): Implied legal restriction inferred from circumstances, even without explicit wording.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The permission remains valid because absconding does not affect it.
B) The permission is revoked because of implied restriction (dalalah al-hajar).
C) The permission is revoked only if the master explicitly says so.
D) The permission is suspended until the slave returns.

Key Takeaway
Absconding triggers an implied revocation of a slave's authorization to trade in the Hanafi school, unless the master explicitly permits it.

Revocation of Slave's Authorization upon Master's Incapacity
Ruling with rational proof (qiyas) on automatic revocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three cases that automatically revoke a slave's authorization and explain the legal reasoning based on the non-binding nature of authorization and qiyas.

Core Idea
Authorization is non-binding; when the master loses capacity, permission ends automatically.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi ruling on the authorized slave (al-madhun). The master's permission to trade is not a binding contract—it can be withdrawn anytime. However, in three specific cases—death, continuous insanity, and apostasy by joining enemy territory—the authorization ends automatically because the master no longer has the legal capacity to continue it. The passage uses qiyas to equate apostasy with constructive death (mawt hukmi).

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases
List the three events that automatically revoke the slave's authorization: death, continuous insanity, and joining enemy territory as an apostate.
Continuous insanity means a permanent, uninterrupted loss of reason.
2
Understand the principle
The authorization (idhn) is non-binding (ghayr lazim). Its continuation depends on the master's capacity (ahliyyah).
Non-binding means it can be revoked anytime without penalty.
3
Analyze why death and insanity end capacity
Death eliminates legal personality entirely. Continuous insanity removes the ability to make sound decisions, so capacity is lost.
Temporary insanity does not automatically end permission.
4
Apply qiyas to apostasy
Joining enemy territory as an apostate is treated as constructive death (mawt hukmi). Just as death ends capacity, so does this act. Evidence: the master's property is divided among heirs.
The property division rule supports treating it as death.
5
State the legal effect
In all three cases, the slave becomes restricted (mahjur), meaning he can no longer trade without a new permission.
Any trades after the event are invalid (batil).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو مات سيده، أو جن مطبقاً، أو لحق بدار الحرب مرتداً. أي يحجر في هذه الصور الثلاث العبد المأذون؛ لأن الإذن غير لازم وما لا يكون لازماً من التصرف يعطى لدوامه حكم الابتداء، هذا هو الأصل. فلا بد من قيام أهلية الإذن في حالة البقاء، والأهلية تنعدم بالموت والجنون المطبق، وكذا بلحوقه بدار الحرب؛ لأنه موت حكمي، ألا ترى أن ماله يقسم باللحوق بدار الحرب بين ورثته. كذا في «الهداية».
Or if his master dies, or becomes insane (continuous insanity), or joins enemy territory as an apostate. That is, the authorized slave becomes restricted in these three cases; because authorization is not binding, and what is not binding from a disposition is given the ruling of initiation for its continuation; this is the principle. So the capacity for authorization must exist in the state of continuance, and capacity ceases with death and continuous insanity, and similarly with joining enemy territory because it is a constructive death; do you not see that his property is divided upon joining enemy territory among his heirs? Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with rational proof. The author explains that an authorized slave's permission is revoked if the master dies, becomes continuously insane, or joins enemy territory as an apostate. The underlying principle is that the authorization (idhn) is not a binding contract; it can be revoked at any time. For the authorization to remain valid, the master must have legal capacity (ahliyyah) to continue granting it. Death eliminates capacity entirely; continuous insanity eliminates the ability to make sound transactions. Joining enemy territory as an apostate is treated as a constructive death (mawt hukmi) because the master severs ties with the Muslim state, and his property is divided among his heirs as if he died. Therefore, in all three cases, the slave's authorization ends automatically.

Key Terms
موت حكمي (mawt hukmi): Constructive death: joining enemy territory as an apostate legally considered death for inheritance and dissolution of marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the authorized slave's permission automatically end when the master joins enemy territory as an apostate?
A) Because the slave must follow his master to enemy territory.
B) Because the master becomes a non-Muslim and cannot own a Muslim slave.
C) Because joining enemy territory is considered a constructive death (mawt hukmi), ending the master's capacity.
D) Because the slave’s permission is only valid within Islamic lands.

Key Takeaway
The slave's authorization ends automatically (without explicit revocation) when the master dies, becomes continuously insane, or joins enemy territory as an apostate, because the master's legal capacity ceases in these cases.

Revocation of Female Slave's Authorization by Childbirth (Istilad)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of istilad on authorization

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the implied restriction (dalalah al-hajar) of an umm walad's authorization and distinguish it from Zufar's view, applying the concept to determine when explicit permission is needed.

Core Idea
The umm walad's authorization is revoked by implied restriction unless the master explicitly allows trade.

This lesson examines the effect of istilad (master begetting a child from his female slave) on her authorization to engage in trade. The Hanafi school holds that the slave becomes subject to an implied restriction (dalalah al-hajar) unless the master gives explicit permission, while Imam Zufar maintains that her authorization remains valid. Understanding this dispute clarifies the role of custom (urf) in Hanafi jurisprudence and the interplay between explicit and implied legal effects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
Recognize what an umm walad is and that the master has previously authorized her to trade. The master then begets a child from her (istilad).
The scenario is set in the context of classical Islamic commerce where slaves could be authorized to trade.
2
State the majority Hanafi position
According to Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, istilad creates an implied restriction (dalalah al-hajar) that revokes her authorization unless the master explicitly permits her.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Contrast with Zufar's position
Zufar argues that since the master can authorize her after istilad, the prior authorization remains valid; begetting a child does not automatically restrict her.
Zufar's reasoning is based on the possibility of permission ab initio.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds that the custom (urf) shows masters are not pleased for their umm walad to go out and deal with people, implying a restriction. The explicit permission overrides this implication.
The proof is based on customary practice, not direct text.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under the majority view, the umm walad should not trade without explicit permission after istilad; under Zufar's view, she may continue trading until explicitly revoked.
This affects the validity of her contracts in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأمة إن استولدها: أي تنحجر الأمة إن استولدها عندنا. وعند زفر: لا تنحجر؛ لأنه يجوز إذن المستولدة، قلنا: فيه دلالة الحجر، إذ الظاهر أنه لا يرضى أن تخرج وتعامل مع الناس، لكن إذا أذنها صريحاً فالصريح يفوت دلالة الحجر.
And the female slave if he begets a child from her: that is, the female slave becomes restricted if he begets a child from her according to us. And according to Zufar: she does not become restricted, because authorization of a female slave who has borne a child is valid. We said: there is implied restriction (dalalah al-hajar) in it, for it is apparent that he is not pleased for her to go out and deal with people, but if he explicitly authorized her, the explicit [permission] nullifies the implied restriction.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa (and the majority of Hanafis) and Imam Zufar regarding the status of a female slave who has borne her master's child (umm walad). According to the Hanafi school (the position of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad), merely begetting a child from her does not automatically revoke her authorization if she was previously authorized; rather, it creates an implied restriction (dalalah al-hajar) because the master would normally not wish her to go out and trade. However, if the master explicitly gives her permission to trade, that express permission overrides the implication. Imam Zufar argues that begetting a child does not prevent authorization, so she remains authorized until explicitly revoked. The Hanafi response uses the concept of implied restriction based on customary practice.

Key Terms
استيلاد (istilad): The act of a master begetting a child from his female slave, making her an umm walad.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, and is thus entitled to freedom upon his death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn), what is the effect of begetting a child from an authorized female slave?
A) Her authorization is automatically and permanently revoked.
B) Her authorization remains valid unless the master explicitly revokes it.
C) There is an implied restriction (dalalah al-hajar) that revokes her authorization unless the master explicitly permits her.
D) Her authorization becomes suspended until the child is weaned.

Key Takeaway
The female slave's authorization is revoked by implied restriction (dalalah al-hajar) upon istilad unless the master explicitly permits her to trade.

Conditions for Valid Revocation of Authorization
Ruling on prerequisites for effective revocation of slave's authorization

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for valid revocation of a slave's authorization and apply them to scenarios of public vs. private authorization.

Core Idea
Revocation must reach those who knew the authorization, to prevent deception.

This lesson examines the ruling on revoking a slave's commercial authorization. The master may restrict the slave, but the revocation is only effective if certain knowledge conditions are met. The key principle is preventing deception (ghurur) of people who rely on the slave's apparent authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the master's revocation (hajar) of the slave's authorization. The ruling is that it is obligatory (wajib) to ensure the slave and, if applicable, the public know.
This is a condition for the revocation to be valid.
2
Determine the conditions for valid revocation
Two conditions: (1) the slave himself knows of the revocation, (2) if the authorization was public, most market people must know.
Publicity is required only when the original authorization was public.
3
Distinguish between public and private authorization
If the authorization was known only to the slave (private), informing the slave alone suffices because no third party is harmed. If it was public, the revocation must be public too.
The rationale is preventing deception (ghurur) of those who deal with the slave.
4
Understand the rationale (talil)
The condition of publicity is based on daf' al-ghurur (preventing deception) and the principle of sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking means to harm).
This is a rule of equity to protect innocent third parties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو حجر عليه بشرط أن يعلم هو وأكثر أهل سوقه، دفعاً للغرور عن الناس. وإنما يشترط الشيوع في الحجر إذا كان الإذن شائعاً، أما إذا لم يعلم به إلا العبد، فبالحجر عليه بعلم منه ينحجر؛ لأنه لا ضرر فيه. كذا في «الهداية».
Or he placed a restriction upon him with the condition that he himself and most of the people of his market know, to prevent deception from the people. And publicity is only required in the restriction if the authorization was public; but if only the slave knew about it, then by placing the restriction upon him, with knowledge from him, he becomes restricted, because there is no harm in it. Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation of its rationale. It outlines the conditions for the effective revocation of an authorized slave's permission. The master can revoke (hajar) the authorization, but for the revocation to be valid, two conditions are required: (1) the slave himself must know, and (2) most of the people in the market (i.e., the public who deal with him) must know. This requirement prevents deception (ghurur) of people who might continue dealing with the slave unaware of the revocation. However, the requirement of publicity depends on the nature of the original authorization. If the authorization was public (i.e., known to the public), then the revocation must be public too. If the authorization was only known to the slave (e.g., a private permission), then informing only the slave is sufficient for revocation because there is no third-party harm. This is based on the principle in the Hidayah.

Key Terms
غرور (ghurur): Deception or fraud that would occur if people deal with the slave unaware of the revocation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is it sufficient for the master to inform only the slave about the revocation of his authorization?
A) When the authorization was originally public and known to the market.
B) When the authorization was only known to the slave himself and not to the public.
C) When the slave has absconded.
D) When the master is the slave's father.

Key Takeaway
The validity of revoking a slave's authorization hinges on ensuring that all parties who were aware of the authorization are also informed of its revocation, to prevent deception.

Conditions for the Revocation of the Authorized Slave
Ruling with explanation of condition for hajr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for a valid revocation (hajr) of an authorized slave and explain why public knowledge is required.

Core Idea
A secret revocation does not bind — only public knowledge protects creditors.

When a master revokes his slave's permission to trade, simply intending it is not enough. The law requires that the slave himself and most of the people in his market know about the revocation. This lesson explores the wisdom behind this condition and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The act is the revocation of permission (hajr) of an authorized slave (ma'dhun). The ruling concerns when this revocation becomes effective.
2
State the condition
The revocation is only effective if both the slave and most of the people in his market know about it. This is to prevent harm to creditors.
The text says: 'بشرط أن يعلم هو وأكثر أهل سوقه'
3
Understand the rationale
If revocation could be secret, creditors would continue dealing with the slave on the assumption that his permission is still valid, and their debts might become uncollectible.
The commentator mentions that the slave's debt is not attached to his person or earnings, so creditors rely on the permission.
4
Apply the condition
If the permission was widely known, the revocation must be similarly public. If the permission was only known to the slave, then personal knowledge suffices because no third parties are harmed.
The text distinguishes between 'إذا كان الإذن شائعا' and 'إذا لم يعلم به إلا العبد'.
5
Note the exception
If the master revokes in private but most of the market witnesses (e.g. in his house), it is still valid because the knowledge spreads.
The text gives the example of revocation in the master's house in the presence of most of the market.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱] أو حجر عليه بشرط أن يعلم هو وأكثر أهل سوقه، والأمة إن استولدها أو حجر عليه بشرط أن يعلم هو وأكثر أهل سوقه)، دفعاً للغرور عن الناس. (والأمة إن استولدها) : أي تنحجر الأمة إن استولدها عندنا. وعند زفره : لا تنحجرُ ؛ لأنه يجوز إذن المستولدة، قلنا : فيه دلالة الحجر، إذ الظاهر أنَّهُ لا يرضى أن تخرج وتعامل مع الناس، لكن إذا أذنها صريحاً فالصريح يفوت دلالة الحجر. [١] قوله : أو حجر عليه...الخ ؛ أي ينحجرُ المأذون لو حجر عليه ... الخ ؛ لأنه لو انحجر بغير هذا الشرط لتضرّر الناس به ؛ لتأخّر حقهم إلى ما بعد العتق ، لما لم يتعلّق برقبته وكسبه، وقد بايعوه على رجاء ذلك، ويشترط علم أكثر أهل سوقه، حتى لو حجر في السوق وليس فيه رجل أو رجلان ينحجر، ولو بايعوه جاز، وإن باعه الذي علم بحجره، ولو حجر عليه في بيته بمحضر من أكثر أهل سوقه ينحجر. الحاصل المعتبر شيوع الحجر واشتهاره ، فيقام ذلك مقام الظهور عند الكل، كما في تبليغ الرسالة من الرسل، ويبقى العبد مأذوناً إلى أن يعلم بالحجر كالوكيل ؛ ولذا قال : شرط أن يعلم هو ؛ وهذا لأنه يتضرر به، حيث يلزمُهُ قضاء الدين من خالص ماله بعد عتقه، وما رضي به. وإنما يشترط الشيوع في الحجرِ إذا كان الإذن شائعاً ، أما إذا لم يعلم به إلا العبد ، عليه بعلم منه ينحجر ؛ لأنه لا ضرر فيه. كذا في «الهداية»(۱). [٢] قوله : لكن إذا أذنها ... الخ ؛ هذا جواب عن استدلال زفر ، وهو يقول : الاستيلاد ليس بحجر عليها اعتباراً بالابتداء، فإنّ المولى إذا استولد أمته قبل أن يأذن لها، ثمّ أذنها جاز ، فكذا إذا استولدها بعد الإذن وهو القياس، ومذهبنا هـو الاستحسان، بناءً على أنّ العادة في الظاهر على أنّ الإنسانَ يُحْصِنُ أمّ ولده كما بينه الشارح (۱) (الهداية» (٤ : ٧). (۲) ينظر: «المبسوط»(٢٥ : ٣٦).
[1] Or if he is placed under interdiction on condition that he knows and most of the people of his market know, and the female slave if she becomes a mother of his child, or if he is placed under interdiction on condition that he knows and most of the people of his market know, to prevent deception of the people. (And the female slave if she becomes a mother of his child): meaning she becomes interdicted if she becomes a mother of his child according to us. According to Zufar: she does not become interdicted because it is permissible to authorize the umm walad. We say: there is an indication of interdiction in it, because the apparent situation is that he is not pleased that she goes out and deals with people. But if he explicitly authorizes her, then the explicit overrides the indication of interdiction. [1] His saying: 'or if he is placed under interdiction...' etc.; meaning the authorized [slave] becomes interdicted if he is placed under interdiction... etc.; because if he became interdicted without this condition, it would harm the people due to the delay of their right until after manumission, since it is not attached to his person or earnings, and they dealt with him on hope of that. And it is conditional on the knowledge of most of the people of his market, so that if he placed interdiction in the market and there are not one or two men, he becomes interdicted. And if they dealt with him, it is permissible, even if the one who knows his interdiction sold to him. And if he placed interdiction on him in his house in the presence of most of the people of his market, he becomes interdicted. The conclusion: what is considered is the spread and notoriety of the interdiction, so it takes the place of appearance to all, like the delivery of the message by messengers. And the slave remains authorized until he knows of the interdiction, like the agent. Hence he said: condition that he knows. And this is because he is harmed by it, as he is obliged to repay the debt from his pure wealth after his emancipation, and he is not pleased with that. And notoriety is only required in interdiction if the permission was notorious. But if only the slave knew about it, then upon his knowledge he becomes interdicted because there is no harm in it. Thus in al-Hidayah (4:7). [2] His saying: 'But if he explicitly authorizes her...' etc.; this is a response to the argument of Zufar, who says: making her umm walad is not an interdiction on her, considering the beginning, for if the master makes his female slave umm walad before authorizing her, then authorizes her, it is permissible. So too if he makes her umm walad after authorization, and this is qiyas. Our madhhab is istihsan, based on the fact that the apparent custom is that a man confines his umm walad, as explained by the commentator. (1) al-Hidayah (4:7). (2) See: al-Mabsut (25:36).
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter of the authorized slave (al-ma'dhun) discussing the conditions under which the master's permission (idhn) for the slave to trade can be revoked (hajr). The commentary expands on the matn of al-Wiqayah. The first part states the condition: the revocation is effective only if the slave himself and most of the people in his market know about it. This is to prevent harm to creditors who dealt with the slave on the presumption that his permission was still valid. The commentator explains that if the master tries to revoke secretly, it does not take effect until knowledge spreads, similar to the way a message from messengers is only binding when delivered. If the permission was not widely known but only the slave knew, then personal knowledge suffices because no third parties are harmed. The second part deals with the case of a female slave who becomes an umm walad (mother of her master's child). According to the Hanafi school, such a slave is automatically revoked (tunhajir) because the custom is that masters do not allow their umm walad to go out and trade. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference), overriding qiyas. Zufar argues that making her umm walad should not revoke her, just as it is valid to authorize her initially after becoming umm walad. The Hanafi response is that the custom of confinement provides an implicit indication (dalala) of revocation, but if the master explicitly authorizes her, the explicit permission overrides the implicit indication.

Key Terms
حجر (hajr): Legal interdiction or revocation of the slave's permission to trade, preventing him from contracting debts or engaging in transactions.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave (or sometimes a free person) who is permitted by the master to engage in trade, with the capacity to contract debts that are binding on the master's assets or the slave's future earnings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is a master's revocation of his slave's permission to trade effective?
A) As soon as the master declares it, regardless of knowledge.
B) Only when the slave knows, even if the market is unaware.
C) Only when both the slave and most of the people of his market know.
D) Only when the market knows, even if the slave is unaware.

Key Takeaway
A revocation (hajr) of an authorized slave is only effective if the slave and most of his market know, to prevent harm to creditors.

Implicit Revocation of the Umm Walad
Ruling with proof: umm walad's implicit revocation and dispute with Zufar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that an umm walad is implicitly revoked from trade and evaluate the counter-argument of Imam Zufar.

Core Idea
Custom overrides analogy: the umm walad's confinement signals revocation stronger than any logical parallel.

When a master fathers a child with his female slave, she becomes an umm walad with special status. The Hanafi school rules that her permission to trade is automatically revoked, even without explicit statement. This lesson unpacks the istihsan reasoning and contrasts it with the qiyas of Imam Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
According to Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, an umm walad automatically becomes interdicted (tunhajir) when she becomes umm walad, meaning her permission to trade is revoked.
The text states: 'تنحجر الأمة إن استولدها عندنا'.
2
State the default exception
If the master explicitly authorizes her after she becomes umm walad, the explicit permission overrides the implicit revocation. The implicit indication yields to the explicit statement.
The text says: 'لكن إذا أذنها صريحاً فالصريح يفوت دلالة الحجر'.
3
Understand the Hanafi proof
The proof is istihsan based on custom (urf). The apparent custom is that masters confine their umm walad and do not allow them to go out and trade. This provides an implicit indication (dalala) of revocation.
The commentator says: 'العادة في الظاهر على أنّ الإنسانَ يُحْصِنُ أمّ ولده'.
4
Contrast with Zufar's qiyas
Imam Zufar argues by qiyas: If a master makes her umm walad before authorizing her, he can still authorize her later. So making her umm walad after authorization should not retroactively revoke the permission.
The text presents Zufar's argument: 'الاستيلاد ليس بحجر عليها اعتباراً بالابتداء'.
5
Apply the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds that istihsan overrides qiyas because the custom of confinement is stronger. The master's actions indicate he does not want her to trade, so the permission is implicitly revoked.
The response is: 'مذهبنا هو الاستحسان' based on the apparent custom.

Source Text

(والأمة إن استولدها) : أي تنحجر الأمة إن استولدها عندنا. وعند زفره : لا تنحجرُ ؛ لأنه يجوز إذن المستولدة، قلنا : فيه دلالة الحجر، إذ الظاهر أنَّهُ لا يرضى أن تخرج وتعامل مع الناس، لكن إذا أذنها صريحاً فالصريح يفوت دلالة الحجر. [١] قوله : أو حجر عليه...الخ ؛ أي ينحجرُ المأذون لو حجر عليه ... الخ ؛ لأنه لو انحجر بغير هذا الشرط لتضرّر الناس به ؛ لتأخّر حقهم إلى ما بعد العتق ، لما لم يتعلّق برقبته وكسبه، وقد بايعوه على رجاء ذلك، ويشترط علم أكثر أهل سوقه، حتى لو حجر في السوق وليس فيه رجل أو رجلان ينحجر، ولو بايعوه جاز، وإن باعه الذي علم بحجره، ولو حجر عليه في بيته بمحضر من أكثر أهل سوقه ينحجر. الحاصل المعتبر شيوع الحجر واشتهاره ، فيقام ذلك مقام الظهور عند الكل، كما في تبليغ الرسالة من الرسل، ويبقى العبد مأذوناً إلى أن يعلم بالحجر كالوكيل ؛ ولذا قال : شرط أن يعلم هو ؛ وهذا لأنه يتضرر به، حيث يلزمُهُ قضاء الدين من خالص ماله بعد عتقه، وما رضي به. وإنما يشترط الشيوع في الحجرِ إذا كان الإذن شائعاً ، أما إذا لم يعلم به إلا العبد ، عليه بعلم منه ينحجر ؛ لأنه لا ضرر فيه. كذا في «الهداية»(۱). Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter of the authorized slave (al-ma'dhun) discussing the conditions under which the master's permission (idhn) for the slave to trade can be revoked (hajr). The commentary expands on the matn of al-Wiqayah. The first part states the condition: the revocation is effective only if the slave himself and most of the people in his market know about it. This is to prevent harm to creditors who dealt with the slave on the presumption that his permission was still valid. The commentator explains that if the master tries to revoke secretly, it does not take effect until knowledge spreads, similar to the way a message from messengers is only binding when delivered. If the permission was not widely known but only the slave knew, then personal knowledge suffices because no third parties are harmed. The second part deals with the case of a female slave who becomes an umm walad (mother of her master's child). According to the Hanafi school, such a slave is automatically revoked (tunhajir) because the custom is that masters do not allow their umm walad to go out and trade. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference), overriding qiyas. Zufar argues that making her umm walad should not revoke her, just as it is valid to authorize her initially after becoming umm walad. The Hanafi response is that the custom of confinement provides an implicit indication (dalala) of revocation, but if the master explicitly authorizes her, the explicit permission overrides the implicit indication.

Key Terms
استيلاد (istilād): The act of the master fathering a child on his female slave, making her an umm walad, which confers on her certain rights (e.g., freedom upon the master's death).
حجر (hajr): Legal interdiction or revocation of the slave's permission to trade, preventing him from contracting debts or engaging in transactions.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave (or sometimes a free person) who is permitted by the master to engage in trade, with the capacity to contract debts that are binding on the master's assets or the slave's future earnings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a master makes his female slave an umm walad after authorizing her, what is the ruling on her permission to trade?
A) She remains authorized as before.
B) She is revoked unless the master explicitly permits her.
C) She is only revoked if the master also places interdiction on her.
D) She is automatically revoked and cannot be reauthorized.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school relies on istihsan (custom) to hold that an umm walad is implicitly revoked from trade, overriding the qiyas argument of Imam Zufar.

Ruling on Mudabbara: Not Revoked and Compensation for Value
States that mudabbara is not revoked and explains compensation for value

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that a mudabbara slave remains authorized (not revoked) and explain why the master must compensate creditors for the slave's value when debt is present.

Core Idea
Mudabbara slave is not revoked; the master pays its value if debt encumbers it.

In the previous lesson, we saw that an umm walad becomes interdicted from trade. Here, the author clarifies that a mudabbara slave—one declared free upon the master's death—does not become interdicted. Furthermore, if a mudabbara or umm walad owes debt that covers their full value, the master must compensate the creditors. This lesson explores both rulings and their underlying reasoning based on custom and legal principle.

Guided Path
1
State the two rulings
Extract from the passage: (1) a mudabbara slave is not revoked; (2) if the slave is encumbered by debt, the master pays its value to the creditors.
2
Identify the basis for no revocation
The absence of revocation is based on custom ('urf): there is no custom of confining a mudabbara (unlike an umm walad who is confined due to the bed). For a male slave, the matter is even clearer.
The passage contrasts with the previous ruling on umm walad.
3
Examine the compensation ruling
The master must pay the slave's value because he destroyed a locus (the slave's person) to which the creditors' right was attached. Tadbir and istilad prevent sale, so creditors cannot recover their debt.
Compensation is limited to the slave's value, not more.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The objector asks why manumission (which also prevents sale) does not require compensation, while tadbir/istilad do. The response: the loss is caused by tadbir/istilad, not by manumission; after tadbir/istilad, manumission does not harm creditors further.
Manumission is not the source of loss.
5
Summarize the Hanafi position
The mudabbara remains authorized; the master's liability is limited to the slave's value when debt exists.
This is the established Hanafi view (zahir al-riwayah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا إن دبرها، وضمن قيمتهما للغريم (لا إن دبرها (١) ، وضمن قيمتهما للغريم ) : أي في صورة الاستيلادِ والتدبير إن كان على المستولدة وعلى المدبرة دين محيط ، غرم السيد قيمتهما، ولا يغرم ما زاد على القيمة ؛ لأنه لم يحبس إلا الرقبة فعليه قيمتها. ١ ] قوله : لا إن دبرها ؛ أي لا تنحجرُ الأمة إن دبَّرَها، وكذا لا ينحجرُ العبد إن دبره، أما في الأمة ؛ فلأنها لا تتعين للفراش، حتى يقال : جرت العادة على تحصين الفراش، وأما العبد فوجهه مكشوف (٢). [٢] قوله : غرم السيد قيمتهما ؛ لإتلافه محلاً تعلق به حق الغرماء، إذ بالتدبير والاستيلاد يمتنع البيع، وبه يقضي حقهم. فإن قيل : إنّ الإعتاق أدخلَ منهما في الإتلافِ على الغرماء، فما وجه أنه لا غرامة في إعتاق أم ولده المديونة والمدبرة على السيد، وفيهما الغرامة عليه. قلنا : إنكشاف وجهه بحيث لا يشتبه على من له أدنى تمييز ؛ لأنَّ منشأ الخسران ، هو الاستيلاد والتدبير، اللذان يمنعان تعلق الدين بالرقبة، فوجود الإعتاق وعدمه بعدهما سيان في عدم إضرارهم . (۳)
Not if he makes her a mudabbara, and he guarantees their value to the creditor. (Not if he makes her a mudabbara (1), and he guarantees their value to the creditor): meaning in the case of istilad and tadbir, if there is a debt encompassing the umm walad and the mudabbara, the master pays their value, and he does not pay more than the value because he only withheld the person (raqaba) so he must pay its value. [1] His saying: 'not if he makes her a mudabbara'; meaning the female slave does not become interdicted if he makes her a mudabbara, and likewise the male slave does not become interdicted if he makes him a mudabbara. As for the female slave, because she is not designated for the bed (firash) such that it could be said: custom has been established on confining the bed. As for the male slave, his face is uncovered (i.e., no such custom). [2] His saying: 'the master pays their value'; because he destroyed a locus (mahall) to which the creditors' right was attached, since by tadbir and istilad sale is prevented, and by it their right would be fulfilled. If it is said: manumission is more severe in terms of destruction upon creditors, so why is there no compensation in manumitting his indebted umm walad and mudabbara, but there is compensation in these? We say: the difference is clear to anyone with the slightest discernment; because the source of the loss is istilad and tadbir, which prevent the debt from attaching to the person (raqaba). The existence or non-existence of manumission after them is the same in terms of not harming them.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the matn, covering two rulings. First, if the master makes his authorized slave a mudabbara (i.e., declares that the slave will be free upon his death), the slave does not become revoked (tunhajir). The reason is that unlike the umm walad (where custom indicates confinement on account of the bed/firash), there is no such custom for a mudabbara. For a female mudabbara, she is not designated for the master's bed (she may be used for service, not necessarily concubinage), so no implicit indication of revocation exists. For a male mudabbara, there is no custom at all, so he remains authorized. Second, if the master makes his slave an umm walad or mudabbara when the slave is encumbered with debt that surrounds (i.e., equals or exceeds) the slave's value, the master must pay the slave's value to the creditors. This is because by making the slave an umm walad or mudabbara, the master prevents the creditors from selling the slave to recover their debt (since such slaves cannot be sold). The master is thus liable for the value as compensation for destroying the creditors' right. An objection is raised: manumission ('itaq) also prevents sale, so why is there no compensation when the master manumits an indebted umm walad or mudabbara? The response is that in manumission, the master grants freedom directly, but the loss to creditors is caused by the prior act of istilad or tadbir (which already removed the slave from the market). Manumission after that does not add to the loss. The key distinction is that istilad and tadbir themselves are the cause of preventing debt attachment, not manumission.

Key Terms
مدبرة (mudabbara): A female slave who has been declared by her master to be free upon his death (tadbir). She cannot be sold and gains freedom upon the master's death.
غرامة (gharāmah): Compensation paid by the master to the creditors for the value of the slave when the master's act (istilad or tadbir) prevents the creditors from recovering their debt from the slave's sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because mudabbara slaves are already free upon the master's death.
B) Because there is no custom of confining a mudabbara, unlike an umm walad.
C) Because the master must explicitly revoke her.
D) Because mudabbara slaves are not allowed to trade at all.

Key Takeaway
A mudabbara slave remains authorized to trade and is not revoked; the master must compensate creditors for the slave's value if debt encumbers it.

Validity of a Slave's Acknowledgment After Revocation
States the ruling for slave's acknowledgment after revocation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for validity of a slave's acknowledgment after revocation and apply it to determine whether a specific acknowledgment binds the slave's current earnings.

Core Idea
A slave's acknowledgment after revocation is valid only from existing earnings, not from his person or future gains.

After a slave's permission to trade is revoked, his legal capacity to bind his master is restricted. However, he can still make acknowledgments that affect his own assets. The key is that such acknowledgments are immediately enforceable from whatever earnings (kasb) he has at the time. This lesson explains the ruling and its crucial condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act is a legal acknowledgment (iqrar) by a slave after his permission has been revoked (hajr). The ruling is that it is valid (sahih) and immediately enforceable.
The acknowledgment covers trust, usurpation, or debt.
2
Distinguish the scope of enforcement
Understand that enforcement is limited to the slave's current earnings (kasb), not from his person (raqaba). The debt does not defer until manumission.
This protects the master from liability.
3
Identify the condition for validity
The acknowledgment is only valid if the slave has sufficient earnings (mal) with him at the time to pay. If he has nothing, it is invalid. If partial, valid only to that extent.
The condition is that something exists; absolute invalidity if none.
4
Analyze partial situation
If the slave acknowledges a debt but has only part of the amount, the acknowledgment is valid for the part he has. The remainder does not bind him later.
This prevents the slave from incurring debt beyond his means.
5
Note the unanimity
All scholars (the 'greatest' and others) agree on this condition. There is no intra-Hanafi dispute.
The ruling is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو حجر فأقر أنَّ ما معه أمانةٌ أو غصب، أو أقر بدين عليه صح ( ولو حجر فأقر أن ما معه أمانة أو غصب، أو أقر بدين عليه" صح ) [٣] قوله : أو أقرّ بدين عليه ؛ عطف على قوله : أقر [أن] ما معه أمانة ؛ أي لو أقرّ بدين عليه صح إقراره في الحال، حتى يؤخذ به في الحال من كسبه دون رقبته، لا يتأخر إلى ما بعد العتق عند الأعظم الله الله . اعلم أن صحة إقرار الدين بعد الحجر مشروط بوجودِ الشيء معه من كسبه بمقدار ما بقي بأداء أقر به، حتى لو لم يوجد معه شيء أصلاً لم يصح إقراره قطعاً في قولهم جميعا، وإن وجد البعض فيصح في مقدار الموجود.
And if he is interdicted and then acknowledges that what he has is a trust or usurpation, or acknowledges a debt upon him, it is valid. (And if he is interdicted and then acknowledges that what he has is a trust or usurpation, or acknowledges a debt upon him, it is valid.) [3] His saying: 'or acknowledges a debt upon him'; is connected to his saying: 'acknowledges that what he has is a trust'; meaning if he acknowledges a debt upon him, his acknowledgment is valid immediately, so that he is taken for it immediately from his earnings, not from his person (raqaba), and it does not delay until after manumission according to the greatest [scholars] may Allah have mercy on them. Know that the validity of acknowledgment of debt after interdiction is conditional upon the existence of something with him from his earnings in the amount that remains for paying what he acknowledged. So if nothing exists with him at all, his acknowledgment is absolutely invalid in the opinion of all of them. And if some exists, it is valid to the extent of what exists.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on acknowledgments made by the authorized slave after his permission has been revoked (hajr). The matn states that if the slave acknowledges that the property in his hands is a trust (amana) or usurped property (ghasb), or acknowledges a debt upon himself, his acknowledgment is valid. The commentary explains that the acknowledgment takes effect immediately, meaning the creditors can claim payment from the slave's current earnings (kasb), not from his person (i.e., they cannot sell him as a slave to recover the debt). The debt does not defer until the slave's manumission (as was the case with debts incurred before revocation). However, the validity is conditional: the slave must have some earnings (mal) with him at the time of acknowledgment sufficient to cover the debt. If he has nothing, the acknowledgment is invalid according to all scholars. If he has partial assets, the acknowledgment is valid only to the extent of those assets. This distinction prevents the slave from burdening the master with debts that cannot be paid immediately.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): A legal acknowledgment by the slave of a right (debt, trust, usurpation) owed to another; it is binding on the slave's earnings.
حجر (hajr): Interdiction; the revocation of the slave's permission to trade, restricting his ability to bind the master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The master must give permission.
B) The debt must be for a necessity.
C) The slave must have sufficient earnings with him at the time to pay the debt.
D) The acknowledgment must be made in writing.

Key Takeaway
A slave's acknowledgment after revocation is valid only to the extent of his current earnings; without assets, it is void.

Mudabbara and Umm Walad in Debt: No Interdiction, Only Value Payment
Commentary on ruling that mudabbara/umm walad do not become mahjur

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mudabbara or umm walad slave when the master is in debt, and explain the rationale why the master must pay only the slave's value.

Core Idea
Mudabbara and umm walad slaves cannot be sold for debt; the master pays their value, not the full debt.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya comments on a ruling in al-Wiqaya: when a master makes a slave mudabbara (promised freedom upon his death) or a female slave becomes umm walad (mother of his child), that slave does not become interdicted (mahjur) if the master has debts. Instead, the master must pay the creditors the slave's value. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, including an important objection about manumission and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the opening phrase 'لا إن دبرها' (No, if he makes her mudabbara). This negates interdiction (hajr) for mudabbara and umm walad slaves. Understand that the slave remains capable of transactions but the master must compensate creditors.
The ruling also applies to male mudabbar slaves, as the commentary clarifies.
2
Determine the financial liability
The passage says the master must pay the slave's value (qimah) to the creditor, not more. Why? Because the master only owned the 'raqaba' (person) of the slave; by making the slave mudabbar or umm walad, the master has destroyed a property that was subject to the creditors' right, so he must compensate that loss.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The text raises an objection: manumission ('itq) seems more detrimental to creditors, yet no compensation is imposed for it. Why then is compensation required for tadbir/istilad? The response: tadbir/istilad prevent the debt from attaching to the slave's person (raqaba), whereas manumission after these events does not affect the creditors' right because by then the slave is already protected. Thus, the true cause of loss is the earlier status change, not the manumission.
This shows the importance of identifying the operative cause ('illah) in the ruling.
4
Summarize the legal principle
The master of a mudabbara or umm walad is not interdicted, but if debts exist, he must pay the slave's current value. The slave cannot be sold to satisfy the debt because the status (tadbir/istilad) makes sale impermissible. This balances the rights of the creditor and the slave's freedom prospect.
This is a unique Hanafi ruling distinguishing between different types of slave statuses.

١ ] قوله : لا إن دبرها ؛ أي لا تنحجرُ الأمة إن دبَّرَها، وكذا لا ينحجرُ العبد إن دبره، أما في الأمة ؛ فلأنها لا تتعين للفراش، حتى يقال : جرت العادة على تحصين الفراش، وأما العبد فوجهه مكشوف (٢).

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling that a slave who is made mudabbar (promised freedom upon the master's death) or umm walad (mother of the master's child) does not become mahjur (interdicted) for the master's debt, and if the master has debts that cover their value, he must pay their value to the creditors. The commentary explains that the rationale is that these slaves are not considered as properties subject to seizure because the custom is that they are designated for the master's bed (for female) or obviously not for sale (for male). The master only owns the raqaba (person) of these slaves, so he is liable for their value. An objection is raised: manumission ('itq) seems more detrimental to creditors, yet there is no liability for manumitting an indebted umm walad or mudabbara. The answer is that the true cause of loss is the istilad and tadbir themselves, which prevent the debt from attaching to the raqaba. Manumission after these events does not change that. Thus, the master must pay the value, not the full debt.

تدبير (tadbir): Making a slave free upon the master's death, a type of contract that prevents sale.
استيلاد (istilad): Bearing a child to the master, making the female slave an umm walad who becomes free upon the master's death.
حجر (hajr): Interdiction; preventing the slave from dealing in property due to debt or other reasons.
رقبة (raqaba): The physical person of the slave, considered as property.

Key Takeaway
A mudabbara or umm walad does not become interdicted for debt; the master must pay the slave's current value to the creditor, not the full debt, because the master only owned the raqaba.

Validity of a Mahjur Slave's Admission
Commentary expanding on a mahjur slave's valid admission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mahjur slave's admission of debt, trust, or ghasb, and identify the condition for its validity.

Core Idea
A mahjur slave's admission of debt is valid only to the extent of his present earnings.

This passage clarifies that a mahjur (interdicted) slave can validly admit that property in his hand is a trust or usurped, or admit a debt. However, this validity depends on the slave having some earnings at the time of admission. The ruling protects the master's rights while upholding the slave's obligation for debts arising from his own activities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a mahjur slave making an admission (iqrar) that property in his hand is amanah (trust) or ghasb (usurped), or that he owes a debt. The ruling is that such admission is valid (sahih) and takes immediate effect.
The admission is effective immediately from the slave's earnings, not from his raqaba (person value).
2
Determine the source of payment
The admission obligates payment from the slave's earnings (kasb), not from his person. This distinguishes liability for acts during slavery from the value of the slave himself.
According to Abu Hanifa, payment is not deferred until after manumission.
3
Apply the condition of existence of earnings
The validity of a debt admission is conditioned on the slave having some earnings in hand at the time of admission. If he has nothing, the admission is invalid by consensus. If he has some, it is valid up to the amount present.
This prevents the admission from being a fraudulent claim against the master.
4
Consider partial presence of earnings
If the slave has some earnings but less than the admitted debt, the admission is valid only to the extent of the available earnings. The remainder is not automatically admitted.
The admission is split: valid for the part covered by earnings, ineffective for the rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling that a mahjur (interdicted) slave's admission (iqrar) that property in his hand is amanah (trust) or ghasb (usurped), or that he owes a debt, is valid. The commentary specifies that the admission of debt takes immediate effect, and payment is taken from the slave's earnings (kasb), not from his raqaba (person), and is not deferred until after his manumission, according to Abu Hanifa. However, this validity is conditional: the slave must have some earnings (kasb) in his possession at the time of admission, at least enough to cover the admitted amount after paying what he owes from that same fund. If he has nothing, the admission is invalid by consensus. If he has some, it is valid only up to the amount present. This prevents the admission from being a fraudulent way to harm the master's rights.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Admission or confession by a person, here specifically by a slave, regarding a right against himself.
أمانة (amanah): Trust property; something deposited with the slave for safekeeping.
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation; taking property unlawfully.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for a mahjur slave's admission of debt to be valid?
A) The slave must have some earnings in hand at the time of admission.
B) The master must consent.
C) The debt must be less than the value of the slave.
D) The slave must be about to be manumitted.

Key Takeaway
A mahjur slave's admission of debt is valid immediately from his earnings, but only to the extent of earnings present at the time of admission.

Master's Ownership When Slave's Debt Covers All Assets
Intra-Hanafi dispute on master's ownership of slave's earnings when debt covers all.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn) on the master's ownership of a slave's earnings when the slave's debt covers all assets and person, explain the basis of the dispute using the concept of khilafah, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
When a slave's debt consumes all assets and person, the master does not own his earnings—ownership vests only after need is met.

This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the master's right to a slave's earnings when the slave is overwhelmed by debt. Abu Hanifa holds that the master does not own those earnings because his ownership is derivative (khilafah), like an heir's, which only activates after the slave's needs are satisfied. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that since the slave's person is the master's property, so are his earnings. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi principle that a slave's solvency conditions the master's right to acquisitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and positions
Read the opening sentence: 'If his debt covers his property and his raqaba, then his master does not own what he has with him.' This is Abu Hanifa's view. Then note that the Sahibayn disagree, arguing that because the raqaba is owned by the master, so are the earnings.
Raqaba here means the slave's person as a whole.
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof (dalil)
Abu Hanifa analogizes the master's ownership to an heir's inheritance: the heir only inherits after the deceased's needs (debts, funeral expenses) are settled. Similarly, the master's ownership (as khalifah/successor) only vests when the slave is free from need (i.e., his debts are paid).
The term 'khilafah' (succession) is key.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's proof and the response
The Sahibayn argue from direct ownership: since the slave's person is property, its fruits (earnings) are also property. Abu Hanifa's response is that this ownership is not absolute but conditional on the slave's need being satisfied.
The commentary supports Abu Hanifa with the Hidayah's statement: 'He spends from what is in his hand'.
4
Understand the practical consequence (thamarah)
According to Abu Hanifa, the slave can use his earnings to pay debts without the master's intervention. According to the Sahibayn, the master can take the earnings. The mufta bihi (operative position) is Abu Hanifa's.
This affects creditors and the slave's ability to clear his debts.
5
Apply the underlying usul principle
The dispute turns on whether ownership of the raqaba implies unconditional ownership of the kasb (earnings). Abu Hanifa restricts it by the condition of need, using qiyas (analogy) to inheritance. This illustrates how Hanafi usul applies al-amm (general) with conditions (khass) based on illah.
Here, the illah is the slave's need for solvency.

And if his debt covers his property and his raqaba, then his master does not own what he has with him. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the master owns, because the raqaba is his property, so likewise the earnings. And for him (Abu Hanifa): The master's ownership is established by succession from the slave when he is free from his need, like the ownership of an heir, and here it is occupied with need. And it is supported by the statement of the author of al-Hidayah: 'He spends from what is in his hand' connected to his statement: 'and he admits a debt upon himself'. So in al-Hidayah. 2) His statement: 'And for him: The validator...' meaning that the validator of his admission is the hand; therefore the admission of the ma'dhun (permitted slave) is not valid regarding what the master took from his hand, and the hand remains in reality, and the condition for its invalidation by interdiction juristically is its being free from his need, and his admission is evidence of its being occupied. Unlike if the master seizes it from his hand before the admission, because the master's hand is established in reality and juristically, so it is not invalidated by his admission. Likewise, his ownership is established in his raqaba, so it is not invalidated by his admission without his consent. So in al-Hidayah. 3) His statement: 'And here it is occupied with need' meaning in our case it is occupied with need, so the master does not succeed him in it. And when the existence or non-existence of ownership is known, manumission is its means. So in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ownership of a slave's earnings when his debt covers both his assets (mal) and his person (raqaba). According to Abu Hanifa, in such a case the master does not own what the slave has in hand. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the master does own it, because the slave's person is the master's property, so earnings should also be the master's. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the master's ownership of the slave's earnings is by succession (khilafatan) from the slave, similar to an heir's ownership of the deceased's property, but only when the slave is free from his own needs. Here, the slave's need (his debt) occupies his earnings, so the master does not succeed to them. This is supported by a statement in al-Hidayah: 'He spends from what is in his hand' which connects to the earlier ruling on admission of debt. The commentary then elaborates on the overall principles: the validator of a slave's admission is his 'hand' (possession), not the master's permission; the interdiction invalidates the hand only juristically, but the hand remains in reality. If the master physically takes the earnings, the slave's admission regarding those earnings is invalid because the master's hand is established. But if the earnings remain in the slave's hand, the admission is valid as long as the slave has need (debt).

Key Terms
خلافة (khilafah): Succession; the master's ownership of the slave's earnings is by succession from the slave, like an heir inherits from the deceased.
يد (yad): Hand; used to denote possession or control over property. For a slave, his 'yad' is considered valid for certain legal acts.
حجر (hajr): Interdiction; court order preventing a slave from dealing with property due to debt or other reasons.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis of Abu Hanifa's view that the master does not own the slave's earnings when debt covers all?
A) The slave's earnings are like inheritance for the master, which only passes after the slave's needs are met.
B) The master has no ownership over the slave whatsoever.
C) The slave's debt takes priority over the master's right.
D) The Sahibayn agree with Abu Hanifa.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school follows Abu Hanifa: the master's ownership of a slave's earnings is conditional on the slave being free from need, so when debt covers all assets and person, the master does not own the earnings.

Master's Ownership of Slave's Earnings When Debt Encompasses
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ownership of slave's earnings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the master's ownership of the authorized slave's earnings when the slave's debt encompasses his property and person, and identify the corrector (musahhih) that each party relies upon.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: the slave's hand (yad) remains the corrector; Sahibayn: the master's permission (idhn) ceases.

In the Hanafi school, there is an important internal dispute concerning an authorized slave whose debts exceed his assets. Does the master own the slave's earnings after the slave has been interdicted? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disagree. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their rulings
State the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on who owns the slave's earnings when debt encompasses his property and person.
Abu Hanifa: master does not own; Sahibayn: master owns.
2
Understand the concept of musahhih (corrector)
The slave's acknowledgment of debts is valid only if a corrector exists. The dispute centers on whether the corrector is the master's permission (idhn) or the slave's physical hand (yad).
Permission is granted initially; hand is the actual possession.
3
Analyze the reasoning of each party
Trace how each side identifies the corrector. For Abu Hanifa, the hand remains even after interdiction. For the Sahibayn, permission is removed by interdiction.
Abu Hanifa: yad baqiyah; Sahibayn: idhn za'il.
4
Apply the reasoning to the scenario
Determine the practical outcome for the slave's ability to acknowledge debts after interdiction according to each view.
Abu Hanifa: acknowledgment valid; Sahibayn: invalid.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
Identify which position is relied upon in the school and the resulting implication for creditors and the master.
Mufta bihi: Abu Hanifa's view per al-Hidayah.

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the ownership of an authorized slave's earnings when his debt encompasses both his property and his person (i.e., his total assets and his value as a slave). The core ruling is that according to Abu Hanifa, the master does not own what the slave has in his possession, while according to the two, the master does own it. The reasoning revolves around the 'corrector' (musahhih) of the slave's acknowledgment (iqrar). For Abu Hanifa, the corrector is the slave's physical possession (yad), which remains even after the master's interdiction (hajr). For the two, the corrector is the master's permission (idhn), which is removed by interdiction, so the acknowledgment is invalid. The commentary further explains that the master's ownership over the slave's earnings is established only after the slave's needs are fulfilled, analogous to an heir's ownership. This dispute has practical implications for the validity of the slave's transactions and debts.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): The authorized slave: a slave who is given permission by his master to engage in trade.
إذن (idhn): Permission: the master's authorization for the slave to trade.
يد (yad): Hand: physical possession, which is the basis for the slave's authority to acknowledge debts.
حجر (hajr): Interdiction: legal restriction placed on the slave's transactions when his debt exceeds his property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school resolves the dispute by upholding Abu Hanifa's view that the slave's hand remains the corrector, so the master does not own the earnings when the slave is occupied with need.
