Manumission and Sale of the Encompassed Debt Slave
Rulings on the authorized slave when debt encompasses assets and person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on manumission of a slave with encompassing debt, explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the rule for sale at value or less.

Core Idea
A slave whose debt exceeds his assets cannot be freed by his master's manumission (Abu Hanifa), but can be sold at value to his master.

When a slave's debts consume all his property and even his own market value, his master's power over him is restricted. This lesson examines two related rulings: whether the master can still manumit such a slave, and under what conditions he can sell the slave back to himself. The Hanafi school has a well-known internal disagreement on the first issue, while the second involves a subtle dispute about selling below value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two legal issues
The passage addresses two distinct questions: (1) Does manumission (itq) take effect when the slave's debt encompasses his assets and person? (2) Can the master sell this slave to himself, and at what price?
Both rulings depend on the condition of 'dayn muhit' (encompassing debt).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position on manumission
According to Abu Hanifa, the slave does NOT become free. The master's ownership of the slave's earnings is not sufficient to effect manumission when the debt covers everything.
The Sahibayn hold that the slave becomes free and the master must pay the slave's value to the creditors.
3
Understand the condition of encompassing debt
If the debt does NOT encompass (i.e., the slave's property exceeds his debts), then manumission is valid by consensus. The dispute only arises when debts cover both the slave's property and his person (value).
This condition is critical: the slave is then like a bankrupt debtor whose dealings are restricted.
4
Analyze the sale ruling
The passage states that the slave may sell to his master at the fair market value (misl al-qimah), but not for less. However, the master may sell to the slave at value or less. The reasoning: the master is a third party (ajnabi) regarding the slave's property when debt is encompassing.
There is a dispute about selling for less: some say it is only allowed by the Sahibayn, but the preferred view (mufta bihi) is that it is permissible by all.
5
Determine the relied-upon positions
For manumission, the mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view (slave not free). For sale, the correct view is that selling at value or less is permissible by all, because the master could redeem the slave's earnings at value anyway.
This tarjih is supported by al-'Inayah and Nata'ij al-Afkar.

al-Husn al-Dirayah li-Awakhir Sharh al-Wiqayah
124
Thus, the slave does not become free by his master's manumission of him, and he sells to his master for the equivalent of the value, not less, and his master (sells) to him for its equivalent or less. [1] (Thus) he does not become free, the slave he earned, by his master's manumission: i.e., according to Abu Hanifa, and according to them (the two), he becomes free and the master guarantees his value to the creditors, and (he becomes free) if his debt does not encompass his person and his earnings. (And he sells to his master for the equivalent of the value, not less, and his master (sells) to him for its equivalent or less): i.e., it is permissible for the authorized slave whose debt encompasses his property and his person to sell to his master, and it is only permissible because his master is a stranger regarding his property when he has a comprehensive debt. The authorized slave. [1] His statement: He becomes free because he is the earning of his slave, and the cause of ownership is found in his earning, which is ownership of the person. [2] His statement: Not less, etc.; it should not be said: This contradicts al-Tawfiq, where he said: If he sells to him for less, the sale is valid by agreement in slight and excessive loss, and the waiver is invalid... For we say: Here there is a dispute among scholars. Some said that the permissibility of the indebted authorized slave selling for less than the value is specific to them (the two), contrary to the Greatest (Imam), and this is the choice of the author of al-Hidayah, followed by the author and the commentator. And some said: It is by agreement of all in permissibility, and this is correct. It is supported by the statement of the author of al-‘Inayah: And their specification of this ruling is the choice of the author for it, due to the saying of some shuyukh. It was said: And the correct view is that it is the saying of all, because the master has the right to rescue the earning of his authorized slave for himself at the value without sale, so it is more appropriate that he not have that by sale. So the slave in his transaction with his master is like a sick debtor in his transaction with a stranger. So it is as if the author of al-Tawfiq indicated the preponderance of the view of those who say it is by agreement of all, since he did not mention a contrary view despite his perusal of reliable works which all contain the disagreement. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [3] His statement: When he has a comprehensive debt... and the author of Nata'ij al-Afkar said: His intent is that if the debt does not encompass his property and his person, his manumission is valid by consensus. As stated in al-Kafi and other reliable books. (1) Dhakhirat al-‘Uqba p. 550-?. (2) Nata'ij al-Afkar 9:301-302.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents rulings on the authorized slave whose debt encompasses his property and person (i.e., total debts exceed his assets including his value). The first ruling: According to Abu Hanifa, if the master manumits this slave, the slave does not become free because the master's ownership of the slave's earnings is not absolute when debt is encompassing. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the slave becomes free, and the master must pay the slave's value to the creditors. However, if the debt does not encompass, manumission is valid by consensus. The second ruling: The slave may sell to his master at the fair market value (not less), and the master may sell to the slave at the fair value or less. The permissibility stems from the master being considered a third party (ajnabi) regarding the slave's property when debt is encompassing. There is a dispute about selling for less than value: some say it is only allowed by the two companions (not Abu Hanifa), but the preferred view is that it is allowed by all, because the master could obtain the slave's earnings by paying the value anyway without a sale, so sale for less is a fortiori permissible. The slave's position is analogized to a sick debtor who is restricted in his dealings.

Key Terms
عتق (itq): Manumission: the act of freeing a slave by the master.
قيمة (qimah): Value: the fair market price of the slave or object.
دين محيط (dayn muhit): Encompassing debt: debt that covers all of the slave's property and his person (value).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (sahih) view, can the authorized slave whose debt encompasses sell to his master for less than the fair value?
A) No, never.
B) Yes, only according to the two companions.
C) Yes, by agreement of all scholars.
D) Only if the master pays the creditors first.

Key Takeaway
Manumission of a slave with encompassing debt is ineffective according to the relied-upon Hanafi position, but sale at value or less to the master is permissible by all.

Rulings on a Ma'dhun Slave's Sale and the Master's Right of Detention
Rulings for a ma'dhun slave's sale above or below value and detention rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for a ma'dhun slave selling above or below market value and apply the master's right to detain the sold item.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun slave's sale is valid, but the master retains options to protect creditors and his own price.

A ma'dhun slave can engage in trade, but his transactions affect the master and creditors. This passage from Sharh al-Wiqaya addresses two scenarios: selling an item above or below its value. In each case, the master has specific rights—either to deduct surplus, rescind, or approve with an option. Additionally, the master may detain the sold item until the price is paid. Understanding these rulings clarifies how Hanafi law balances commercial flexibility with creditor protection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
The act is a sale by a ma'dhun slave. Note the slave's limited capacity: he is permitted to trade but the master retains certain rights over his transactions and goods.
The ma'dhun slave is analogous to a modern authorized agent, but with distinct legal effects.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
The passage treats sale above value and sale below value separately. Identify the ruling for each: for above value, surplus is deducted or sale rescinded; for below value, the sale is valid with master's option (according to Sahibayn).
The ruling for above value is not disputed; the dispute is only for below value.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that a sale below value is invalid due to suspicion of collusion (tuhma). The two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold it valid with the master's khiyar (option) to remove favoritism or rescind. Understand each proof: Abu Hanifa analogizes to an heir's sale; Sahibayn focus on harm removal.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of the Sahibayn.
4
Understand the right of detention
The master may detain the sold item until the price is received. If he delivers before receiving the price, his right to the specific item is lost, and the price becomes void because the master cannot claim a debt from his own slave.
This is a procedural rule to enforce payment.
5
Apply the ruling to a new case
Given a scenario (e.g., slave sells at half price to a stranger), determine the ruling using the Sahibayn's view: valid, master may rescind or require the buyer to pay the difference.
The amount of favoritism (muḥābāt) does not affect the ruling; it applies whether large or small.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(فلو باع بالأكثر حط الفضل، أو نقض البيع ) : أي يؤمر السيد بإزالة المحاباة، أو نقض البيع.
ولاية.
وبطل ثمنه لو سلّم مبيعَهُ قبل قبضه ، وله حبس مبيعه لثمنه) : أي للسَّيِّدِ حبس المبيع لقبض الثَّمن ، فإن سلّمَ المبيع قبل قبض الشَّمنِ ، أبطل حقه في العين فلم يبق له حق إلا في الدين، والمولى لا يستوجب على عبدِهِ ديناً ، فيبطل الثمن.
ومن اكتفى بذكر قوله : بماله ولم يذكر رقبته ، فكأنه بنى الكلام على أن تعلق الدين بكسبه مقدم على تعلق الدين برقبته، فلم يحتج إلى ذكر الثاني بعد ذكر الأول. [٢]قوله : أبطل حقه ؛ لأنّ حق المولى كان ثابتاً في العين من حيث الحبس ؛ لعدم تعلق حقه بمالية العين بعد البيع، والثابت في العين من حيث الحبس سقط بالتسليم ، فحق المولى سقط به ، فلو فرض بقاءً حقه بعد سقوطه ، لكان ذلك في الدين ؛ لكونه في مقابلة العين، والمولى لا يستوجبه على عبده، حتى لو أتلف شيئاً من ماله لم يضمن. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» (۲)
If he sold for more, the surplus is deducted, or the sale is rescinded. And his price is void if he delivered his sold item before receiving its price, and he has the right to detain his sold item for its price. And according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): if he sold for less than its value, the sale is valid and the master has the option between removing the favoritism and rescinding the sale, because harm from the creditors is removed by that. And Abu Hanifa did not permit it due to suspicion, as in the case of the heir, and there is no suspicion when he favored a stranger. And his price is void if he delivered his sold item before receiving its price, and he has the right to detain his sold item for its price): meaning the master has the right to detain the sold item to receive the price. If he delivered the sold item before receiving the price, he nullifies his right in the specific item, so no right remains for him except in the debt, and the master does not impose a debt upon his slave, so the price becomes void. And whoever is satisfied by mentioning his statement: 'with his property' and did not mention his person, it is as if he built the speech on the principle that the attachment of debt to his earnings takes precedence over the attachment of debt to his person, so he did not need to mention the second after mentioning the first. [1] His statement: 'he is ordered'; meaning the sale is valid, but the master has the choice between the two matters, as in the case of the slave, whether the favoritism is much or little, because the increase is attached to the right of the creditors. He said in al-Nihayah: This is according to the choice of the author of al-Mabsut. As for according to the narration of the author of al-Kashshaf: this sale is not permissible according to the greatest Imam (Abu Hanifa) as mentioned in the case of the slave. My brother Jalabi said: This is understood from al-Inayah. [2] His statement: 'he nullified his right'; because the master's right was established in the specific item in terms of detention, due to the lack of attachment of his right to the value of the specific item after the sale, and what was established in the specific item in terms of detention fell by delivery, so the master's right fell by it. If his right were supposed to remain after its falling, it would be in the debt, because of its being in exchange for the specific item, and the master does not impose it upon his slave, such that if he destroys something of his property, he does not guarantee it. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi (2).
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Wiqaya addresses two situations involving a ma'dhun slave: selling for more than value, and selling for less. When the slave sells for more, the master either deducts the excess or rescinds the sale. This protects creditors. When selling for less, the two Sahibayn hold the sale valid with the master's option to eliminate the favoritism or rescind, because creditors' harm is removed. Abu Hanifa does not permit it due to suspicion (tuhma), like an heir's sale. However, if the buyer is a stranger, no tuhma. The passage also states that if the master delivers the sold item before receiving the price, his right to the specific item is lost, and the price becomes void because the master cannot claim a debt from his own slave. The commentary further explains that the master's option exists irrespective of the amount of favoritism. The relied-upon position is that of the Sahibayn.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade.
محاباة (muḥābāt): Selling an item below its market value, showing favoritism to the buyer.
تهمة (tuhma): Suspicion of collusion between the slave and the buyer to defraud creditors.
خيار (khiyar): Option or right to choose: here the master may choose to remove favoritism or rescind the sale.
غرماء (ghuramā'): Creditors, those to whom the slave or master owes debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (Sahibayn), what is the ruling if a ma'dhun slave sells an item for less than its market value?
A) The sale is invalid and the item must be returned.
B) The sale is valid and the master has no recourse.
C) The sale is valid and the master may remove the favoritism or rescind.
D) The sale is suspended until the master approves.

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun slave's sale below value is valid with the master's option to correct or rescind, while sale above value triggers deducting the surplus or rescinding; the master may also detain the item until paid.

Sale of a Slave with Overwhelming Debt and Buyer Concealment
Ruling on slave sale with overwhelming debt and buyer concealment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a slave with debts exceeding his value is sold and the buyer conceals him, and identify the creditors' option under two scenarios.

Core Idea
Sale is valid but creditors can void if they can reach the slave, or validate if they cannot.

This passage from Sharh al-Wiqaya introduces a nuanced case in sales: a slave whose debts exceed his value is sold without creditor permission, and the buyer hides the slave. The ruling hinges on whether creditors can physically reach the slave. Understanding this khiyar (option) is essential for grasping how Hanafi law balances contractual freedom with creditor rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual elements
Note the key conditions: the slave has debt encompassing his value (dayn muhit), the sale price does not cover the debt, and the sale is without creditor permission. Additionally, the buyer conceals the slave (ghayyaba).
The term 'muhit' means the debt exceeds the slave's value.
2
Parse the ruling into two scenarios
The commentary distinguishes based on creditor access: if creditors can reach the slave, they have the right to nullify the sale unless the master pays the debts. If they cannot reach the slave (due to concealment), they have an option to validate or void the sale.
The ruling is incomplete in the excerpt; the creditors' option in the second scenario is explicitly 'if they wish they may validate'.
3
Determine the hukm for each scenario
In both cases, the sale is not automatically void. It is valid subject to the creditors' right to rescind (khiyar al-ghurmi). The creditors' ability to reach the slave determines whether they can exercise the option unilaterally or only confirm.
The sale is sahih bi-shart – valid with a suspensive condition (creditor approval).
4
Summarize the legal effect
The key takeaway: the sale is effective but the creditors have a khiyar. If they can reach the slave, they can void the sale. If not, they can only choose to validate (they cannot void because the slave is inaccessible).
This protects creditors while allowing the sale to proceed if they consent.

This passage from Sharh al-Wiqaya introduces the case where a slave with debt exceeding his value is sold and the buyer hides him. If the creditors can access the slave, they can nullify the sale unless the master pays the debt. If the buyer hid the slave, preventing creditors from reaching him, the creditors have the option to validate the sale or void it. The ruling is incomplete in this excerpt but establishes a khiyar (option) for creditors based on their ability to reach the slave.

Freeing and Selling a Debtor Ma'dhun Slave
Ruling with proof on emancipation and sale of debtor ma'dhun

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for emancipating a debtor ma'dhun slave, apply the guarantee principle (the master pays the lesser of debt or value), and explain the options of creditors when the slave is sold without their permission.

Core Idea
Emancipation of a debtor slave is valid, but the master must guarantee the lesser of debt or value.

This passage addresses a common trade scenario: a slave permitted to trade (ma'dhun) incurs debts. Can the master free him or sell him without the creditors' consent? The Hanafi school affirms the master's right to do so, but protects creditors by imposing a guarantee obligation. You will learn the precise rules for emancipation and sale, and the creditors' legal options when the master acts without permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage covers (1) freeing a debtor ma'dhun and (2) selling him without creditors' permission. Note that both actions are valid but trigger compensation obligations.
2
Understand the guarantee principle
When freeing, the master guarantees the lesser of the slave's debt and his market value. This principle protects creditors' rights based on whether their right is attached to the debt (if debt ≤ value) or to the slave's person (if value < debt).
3
Analyse the sale without permission
If the master sells the debtor slave without informing the creditors, and the buyer hides him, the creditors have three options: confirm the sale and take the price, claim the value from the buyer, or claim the value from the seller (master).
If the seller pays the value and the slave is returned due to a defect, the seller recovers the value from the creditors.
4
Distinguish the informed sale
If the master informs the buyer of the debt, the creditors still have the same options. The key point is that the master's ownership is not void, but creditors' rights are preserved via the guarantee or option.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وصح إعتاقه مديوناً) : أي صح إعتاق المولى العبد المأذون حال كونه مديوناً سواء كانَ الدِّينُ محيطاً أو لم يكن ، لأن ملكه فيه باق. وضمن السيدُ الأقل من دينِهِ، وقيمته أي إذا كان الدِّينُ أقل من القيمة يضمن الدين، إذ لا حق للغرماء إلا في الدِّين، وإن كانت القيمة أقل من الدِّينِ يضمن القيمة ؛ لأنه تعلّق حقهم بالرقبة، وهو أتلفها ، (وهو فضل دينه معتقاً) : أي ضَمِنَ المأذونُ الذي عتق فضل دينه على القيمة. (فإن بيع عبد ذو دين محيط برقبته وغيبه المشتري) : معناه باعه بثمن لا يفي بديونهم بدون إذن الغرماء والدين حال. وقوله: وغيَّبه؛ لأنَّ الغرماء إذا قدروا على العبد كان لهم أن يبطلوا البيع إن لم يقض المولى ديونهم ، فإذا لم يقدروا على العبد إن شاء أجاز الغريم بيعه وله ثمنه، أو ضَمَّنَ المشتري أو البائع قيمته ، فإن ضمنه (أي البائع) ورد عليه بعيب رجع البائع على الغريم بقيمته، وعاد حقه في العبد. (فإن باعَهُ سَيِّدُهُ مُعْلِماً بدينه) : أي أعلم البائع المشتري بأن هذا العبد مديون، فحينئذ للغريم الخيار: إما أن يجيز البيع وله الثمن، أو يضمن المشتري أو البائع القيمة. فإن ضمن المشتري رجع بالثمن على البائع. فإن ضمن البائع ورد عليه بعيب رجع البائع على الغريم بالقيمة.
(His emancipation while in debt is valid): That is, it is valid for the master to emancipate the permitted slave while he is in debt, whether the debt completely covers his value or not, because his ownership remains in him. And the master guarantees the lesser of his debt and his value: that is, if the debt is less than the value, he guarantees the debt, since the creditors have no right except in the debt; and if the value is less than the debt, he guarantees the value, because their right is attached to the slave's person (al-raqabah), and he has destroyed it. (And it is his surplus debt after emancipation): That is, the permitted slave who becomes free guarantees the surplus of his debt over the value. (If a slave whose debt completely covers his value is sold and the buyer hides him): Its meaning: he sold him for a price that does not satisfy the debts without the creditors' permission while the debt is current. And his saying: 'and he hid him' because if the creditors are able to access the slave, they have the right to annul the sale if the master does not pay their debts. If they cannot access the slave, then if they wish, the creditor may confirm his sale and take its price, or he may claim the value from the buyer or the seller. If he claims it (i.e., from the seller) and it is returned to him due to a defect, the seller recovers the value from the creditor, and the creditor's right in the slave returns. (If his master sells him informing [of his debt]): That is, the seller (the master) informed the buyer that this slave is in debt. In this case, the creditor has the option: either to confirm the sale and take the price, or to claim the value from the buyer or the seller. If the buyer is made to guarantee it, he recovers the price from the seller. If the seller is made to guarantee it and it is returned to him due to a defect, the seller recovers the value from the creditor.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains two linked rulings about a ma'dhun (permitted slave) who is in debt. First, the master's emancipation of such a slave is valid, regardless of whether the debt equals or exceeds the slave's value. The reason given is that the master still owns the slave. However, because the creditors have rights attached either to the debt itself or to the slave's person, the master must compensate them. The principle is simple: the master guarantees the lesser of the debt amount and the slave's market value. If the debt is less, he pays the debt; if the value is less, he pays the value. If the debt exceeds the value and the slave is freed, the slave remains liable for the surplus after emancipation – this is the 'فضل دينه معتقاً' (surplus debt after freedom). Second, the passage discusses a scenario where the master sells this debtor slave without the creditors' permission. If the buyer hides the slave, the creditors cannot annul the sale due to inability to access the slave. Instead, they have three options: (1) confirm the sale and take the sale price, (2) claim the slave's value from the buyer, or (3) claim the value from the seller. If the seller (master) pays the value and the buyer later returns the slave due to a defect, the seller can recover that value from the creditors. If the master informs the buyer about the debt before sale, the creditors still have these options. This demonstrates the Hanafi principle that rights of creditors are protected but do not invalidate the master's ownership acts; rather, they create a guarantee obligation.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade; also called 'abd ma'dhun.
غرماء (ghurama'): Creditors of the slave (or master) whose debts arise from the slave's trading activities.
قيمة (qimah): Market value of the slave at the time of emancipation or sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a ma'dhun slave has a debt of 200 dirhams and his value is 150 dirhams, how much must the master guarantee when freeing him?
A) 200 dirhams
B) 150 dirhams
C) 50 dirhams
D) 350 dirhams

Key Takeaway
The master's ownership overrides creditors' rights but requires compensation; emancipation is valid but the master guarantees the lesser of debt or value.

Creditor's Options on Sale of Ma'dhun Slave
Ruling with proof using qiyas khafi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three options of the creditor when a ma'dhun slave is sold without permission and apply the rule that subsequent ratification is like prior permission.

Core Idea
Ratifying a sale after the fact is like giving prior permission — the buyer is not liable.

When the master of a ma'dhun slave sells the slave without the creditor's permission, the creditor is not left helpless. He has three distinct options to protect his claim. This passage explains those options and clarifies the legal effect of ratification, backed by a qiyas (analogy) to suretyship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the creditor's three options
List the three choices the creditor has when the ma'dhun slave is sold without his permission: (1) ratify the sale and take the price, (2) hold the buyer liable for the slave's value, or (3) hold the seller (master) liable for the value.
2
Understand the effect of ratification
If the creditor ratifies the sale, subsequent ratification is treated like prior permission. Therefore, the buyer is not liable, and the creditor only takes the price.
Contrast with the case where the creditor does not ratify.
3
Analyze the qiyas (analogy) to kafala
The text addresses an objection: in suretyship, if the principal ratifies after the surety acts without permission, the surety cannot recover. Why is the sale case different? Because the creditor's permission is necessary for the sale's binding; in kafala, the surety's act did not need permission from the principal. Thus ratification in the sale case validates, while in kafala it does not create recourse.
The illa (effective cause) is the necessity of permission for validity.
4
Trace the consequences of holding the buyer liable
If the creditor holds the buyer liable, the buyer pays the value and then recovers the price from the seller (master). This is because the buyer's payment of value is like returning the slave itself to the creditor.
The buyer's right to recourse against the seller is a key detail.
5
Apply the scenario of return due to defect
If the master (seller) is held liable and later the slave is returned to him due to a defect (by judicial decree), the master recovers the value he paid from the creditor, and the creditor's right in the slave is restored.
The return rescinds the sale retroactively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجاز الغريم بيعَهُ وله ثمنه، أو ضَمَّنَ المشتري أو البائع قيمته، فإن ضمنه ورد عليه بعيب رجع البائع على الغريم بقيمته، وعاد حقه في العبد

قوله : أجاز الغريم بيعه؛ أي إن شاء أجاز الغريم بيعه وله ثمنه، وليس له التضمين حينئذ؛ لأنّ الإجازة اللاحقة كالإذن السابق، فلو كان البيع بإذنه لم يكن هناك ضمان، فكذا إذا أجاز. فإن قيل : يشكل هذا بما إذا كفل رجلٌ غيره عن غيره بغير إذنه ثمّ أجازه المكفول له لا يرجع الكفيل عليه. قلنا : لأنه لا يحتاج المأذون، فلا يؤثر الإذن منها، وكذلك هاهنا؛ فإنّ البيع يتوقف لزومه على إجازة الغرماء. كذا في «الكفاية».

قوله : أو ضمن المشتري؛ أي إن شاء الغريم ضمن المشتري، ثمّ إن ضمن المشتري رجع المشتري بالثمن على البائع؛ لأنّ استرداد القيمة منه كاسترداد العبد لو ظفروا به، وإن ضمَّنَ البائع قيمته، تم البيع الذي جرى بين البائع والمشتري لزوال المانع. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي».

قوله : وردّ عليه؛ أي البائع وهو المولى. قوله : رجع؛ أي البائع؛ قيل : معناه إذا قبله بقضاء؛ لأنّ القاضي إذا ردّه فقد فسخ العقد بينهما، فعاد إلى الحال الأوّل وهو ظاهر. كذا في «العناية».
The creditor may ratify his sale and take its price, or hold the buyer or the seller liable for its value. Then if he (the master) is held liable and the (slave) is returned to him due to a defect, the seller (master) recovers its value from the creditor, and his right in the slave returns. Commentary: He said, 'The creditor may ratify his sale,' i.e., if he wishes, the creditor may ratify the sale and take its price, and he has no right of holding liable then, because subsequent ratification is like prior permission. For if the sale had been with his permission, there would be no liability, so likewise if he ratifies. If it is asked: This is problematic because if a man stands surety for another without his permission, then the one for whom he stood surety ratifies it, the surety does not have recourse against him. We say: Because the permitted one does not need permission, so permission from him has no effect. Likewise here, the sale's binding depends on the creditors' ratification. So it is in al-Kifaya. He said, 'Or hold the buyer liable,' i.e., if the creditor wishes, he may hold the buyer liable. Then if the buyer is held liable, the buyer recovers the price from the seller, because recovering the value from him is like recovering the slave if they had seized him. And if he holds the seller liable for its value, the sale that took place between the seller and buyer is finalized due to the removal of the impediment. So it is in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. He said, 'And is returned to him,' i.e., the seller, who is the master. He said, 'He recovers,' i.e., the seller. It is said: Its meaning is if he accepts it (the return) by judicial decree, because when the judge returns it, he has rescinded the contract between them, so the situation reverts to the original, and this is apparent. So it is in al-Inaya.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Kitab al-Ma'dhun. It explains the options available to a creditor when the master of a ma'dhun slave (who owes the debt) sells the slave without the creditor's prior permission. Three options are given: (1) The creditor may ratify the sale and take the price from the buyer; (2) He may hold the buyer liable for the value of the slave; (3) He may hold the seller (master) liable for the value. If the master is held liable and then the slave is returned to him due to a defect, the master can recover the value he paid from the creditor, and the creditor's right to collect from the slave reverts. The commentary clarifies that ratification after the fact is equivalent to prior permission, so the buyer incurs no liability. It addresses an objection based on kafala (suretyship), distinguishing the scenarios: in kafala, ratification by the principal does not give the surety recourse because the surety did not need permission, but in this case, the sale's validity depends on the creditor's ratification. If the creditor holds the buyer liable, the buyer can recover the price from the seller because the buyer is entitled to recourse. If the creditor holds the seller liable, the sale is finalized as the impediment is removed. The return of the slave due to defect with judicial decree rescinds the contract, restoring the creditor's original right.

Key Terms
الغريم (al-gharīm): The creditor to whom the ma'dhun slave owes a debt.
المأذون (al-ma'dhūn): A slave whom the master has permitted to engage in trade, with earnings used to pay debts before the master's claim.
الإجازة (al-ijāzah): Ratification or approval of a contract after it has been executed by an unauthorized party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the effect of the creditor ratifying the sale of the ma'dhun slave?
A) The buyer becomes liable for the value of the slave.
B) The master is free of any liability to the creditor.
C) The creditor can take the price from the buyer and has no further claim.
D) The sale is rescinded.

Key Takeaway
The creditor of a ma'dhun slave has a choice to ratify the unauthorised sale or seek compensation from the buyer or seller, but ratification ends further options.

Master Selling a Ma'dhun Slave with Disclosure of Debt – Creditor's Right to Void
Ruling on creditor's right to void sale of ma'dhun slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a creditor can void the sale of a ma'dhun slave and evaluate the Hanafi distinction between debt and crime liability.

Core Idea
A creditor can void the sale only if the price hasn't reached them; once paid at fair value, the right is lost.

This passage from al-Hidayah (via al-Wiqaya and its commentaries) examines the creditor's right to rescind a sale when a master sells his ma'dhun slave while disclosing the debt. The ruling depends on whether the sale price has reached the creditors and whether the sale was at market value. The commentary also addresses an objection comparing this to the case of a slave who committed a crime, clarifying the Hanafi position that debt liability rests on the slave, not the master.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the parties
Recognize the act: master sells ma'dhun slave, informing the buyer of the slave's debt. The parties include master, slave, buyer, and creditors.
The slave is ma'dhun (permitted to trade), so he has debts from business transactions.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
First scenario: sale price has not yet reached the creditors → they can void the sale. Second scenario: price has reached them and the sale is at fair value (no favour) → they cannot void.
The phrase 'وإن وصل ولا محاباة في البيع لا' is the key condition.
3
Understand the reasoning for creditors' right
Creditors have authority to void because their right is attached both to the slave's earnings (istis'a') and to his person (raqaba). This is cited from al-Inaya.
The benefit of voiding is that creditors can seek the slave's earnings or collect from his sale proceeds.
4
Analyze the objection and Hanafi response
Some analogize to a slave who committed a crime: master can choose to pay ransom. Why not here? Hanafi answer: crime liability is on the master, debt is on the slave's liability. Master's sale is like a non-binding promise to pay.
This is a qiyas khafi (subtle analogy) that fails due to difference in liability.
5
Apply the procedural rule
If the buyer denies the debt and the seller (master) is absent, the creditor cannot sue the buyer alone. This is a procedural safeguard.
The text ends with 'إن غاب' discussing absence of buyer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن باعَهُ سَيِّدُهُ مُعْلِمًا بدينه، فللغريم ردُّ بيعِهِ إن لم يصل ثمنه إليه، وإن وصل ولا محاباة في البيع لا، ولا يخاصم المشتري منكراً دينَهُ إن غاب بائعه

قوله : يكون للغرماء ولاية أردّا البيع؛ لتعلّق حقهم وهو الاستسعاء من العبد، والاستيفاء من رقبته وفي كلّ واحدٍ منهما فائدة. كذا في «العناية». فإن قيل : إذا باع المولى عبده الجاني بعد العلم بالجناية كان مختاراً للفداء، فما بال هذا أن لا يكون مختارا لقضاء الديون من ماله. أجيب : بأن موجب الجناية دفع على المولى، فإذا تعذر عليه بالبيع طولب به لبقاء الواجب عليه، وأما الدين فهو واجب في ذمّة العبد، بحيث لا يسقط عنه بالبيع والإعتاق، حتى يؤاخذ بـه بعد العتق، فلما كان كذلك كان البيع من المولى بمنزلة أن يقول : أنا أقضي دينه، وذلك عدة منه بالتبرع، فلا يلزمه. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي».

قوله : فلا؛ أي فليس للغرماء حق نقض البيع إذا وصل إليهم الثمن، والبيع بمثل القيمة وإن لم يكن في الثمن وفاء بديونهم. كذا في «الكفاية».

قوله : إن غاب؛ أما إذا غاب المشتري والبائع حاضر فلا خصومة بينهم وبين
If his master sells him while informing of his debt, then the creditor has the right to void his sale if its price has not reached him; and if it has reached and there is no favour (overvaluation) in the sale, then no. And the creditor does not litigate with the buyer who denies his debt if his seller is absent. Commentary: He said: The creditors have the authority to void the sale because their right is attached: they have the right to seek earnings from the slave and to collect from his person, and in each of them there is a benefit. So it is in al-Inaya. If it is said: When the master sells his slave who committed a crime after knowledge of the crime, he is given the choice to pay the ransom. Why then is he not given the choice here to pay the debts from his wealth? The answer: The liability for the crime is upon the master to pay off; if it becomes difficult for him through sale, he is called to account for the remaining obligation. As for debt, it is an obligation on the slave's liability such that it is not removed by sale or emancipation; the slave is even held accountable after emancipation. Therefore, the master's sale is like his saying, 'I will pay his debt,' and that is a promise of donation, so it is not binding. So it is in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. He said: 'No': meaning the creditors have no right to rescind the sale if the price has reached them, and the sale is at the fair value, even if the price does not cover the debts. So it is in al-Kifaya. He said: 'If absent': as for when the buyer is absent and the seller is present, there is no litigation between them.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage deals with the scenario where the master sells the ma'dhun slave and explicitly informs the buyer of the slave's debt. The ruling has several conditions. First, if the price of the sale has not yet been paid to the creditors, they have the right to void the sale. However, if the price has been paid to them and the sale was at market value without any favour (i.e., no undervaluation), they cannot void it. If there is favour (the slave was sold below market value), the creditors can either demand the difference or rescind the sale. Additionally, the creditors cannot sue the buyer regarding the debt if the buyer denies it and the seller (master) is absent. The commentary explains the basis for the creditors' right: they have a claim on the slave's earnings (istis'a') and on his person (raqaba). It addresses an objection comparing this to the case of a slave who commits a crime: in that case, the master can choose to pay the ransom or surrender the slave, but here the master cannot unilaterally decide to pay the debt because the debt is on the slave's liability, not the master's. Therefore, the master's sale is like a non-binding promise to pay. The commentary also clarifies that if the price reaches the creditors and the sale is at fair value, the creditors lose the right to void, even if the price does not fully cover the debt. Finally, the absence of the buyer from the creditor's litigation relates to procedure: if the seller is absent, the creditor cannot sue the buyer alone.

Key Terms
الردّ (al-radd): The creditor's right to rescind or void the sale, returning the slave to the master's ownership for debt collection.
المحاباة (al-muḥābāh): Selling at a price below market value (undervaluation), which gives creditors a right to demand the difference or rescind.
خصومة (khuṣūmah): Litigation or legal dispute between the creditor and the buyer regarding the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the master sells the ma'dhun slave at market value and the price reaches the creditors, can the creditors void the sale?
A) Yes, because the debt is still unpaid.
B) No, because the price has reached them and there is no favour.
C) Yes, because the master cannot dispose of the slave.
D) No, because the buyer is honest.

Key Takeaway
The creditor's right to void a sale of a ma'dhun slave is conditioned on non-receipt of price or presence of favour, based on the distinction that debt remains on the slave's liability.

Creditor's Right to Rescind Sale of Debtor Slave
Ruling with conditions on creditor's right to rescind

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a creditor may rescind the sale of a debtor slave and apply them to a given scenario, distinguishing between price received and undervalue.

Core Idea
Creditors can rescind only if the price hasn't reached them or if the sale was below market value.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah addresses a nuanced rule in the sale of a slave who owes debt. The scholars balance the interests of creditors with the finality of commercial transactions. The key is understanding when a creditor can annul a completed sale and when the sale is insulated from challenge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its two conditions
The matn states: creditors may rescind if the price does not reach them. If it does reach, they may rescind only if there was abuse (muhabah). Identify these two branches clearly.
The condition 'informing the buyer of the debt' is mentioned but does not affect the creditors' right.
2
Understand why the matn mentions 'informing of the debt'
The commentator explains that one might think informing makes the sale irrevocable. But the creditors still retain authority to rescind if the price is not received, to protect their right to istis'ā' or seizure.
This highlights that creditors' rights are independent of the contracting parties' consent.
3
Analyse the objection from the criminal slave case
An objector asks: if selling a criminal slave makes the master liable for ransom, why doesn't selling a debtor slave make the master liable for the debt? The response distinguishes the obligor: crime liability is on the master, debt liability is on the slave.
This is a qiyas rebuttal showing that analogy fails due to different loci of obligation.
4
Confirm the scenario where creditors have no right
The commentator clarifies: if the price reaches the creditors and the sale is at market value (no muhabah), they have no right to rescind even if the price does not cover all debts.
This protects the stability of transactions.
5
Apply the ruling to a hypothetical case
Given a slave with debt, sale price, and market value, determine whether creditors can rescind. Check: price reached? If yes, is there muhabah? Answer accordingly.
Practice with a concrete example solidifies the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بدينه، فللغريم ردُّ بيعه إن لم يصل ثمنه إليه، وإن وصل ولا محاباة في البيع لا. وإنما قال: معلماً بدينه؛ لأن البائع إذا أعلم المشتري أن على العبد الدين، والمشتري رضي بذلك، تُوهم أن ينفذ البيع برضا البائع والمشتري، فنقول: إن مع هذا يكون للغرماء ولاية رد البيع إذا لم يصل الثمن إليهم وإن وصل، فإن لم يكن في البيع محاباة فلا، وإن كانت فإما أن ترفع المحاباة أو ينقض البيع. [11] قوله: يكون للغرماء ولاية رد البيع؛ لتعلق حقهم وهو الاستسعاء من العبد، والاستيفاء من رقبته وفي كل واحد منهما فائدة. كذا في «العناية». فإن قيل: إذا باع المولى عبده الجاني بعد العلم بالجناية كان مختاراً للفداء، فما بال هذا أن لا يكون مختاراً لقضاء الديون من ماله. أجيب: بأن موجب الجناية دفع على المولى، فإذا تعذر عليه بالبيع طولب به لبقاء الواجب عليه، وأما الدين فهو واجب في ذمة العبد، بحيث لا يسقط عنه بالبيع والإعتاق، حتى يؤاخذ به بعد العتق، فلما كان كذلك كان البيع من المولى بمنزلة أن يقول: أنا أقضي دينه، وذلك عدة منه بالتبرع، فلا يلزمه. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي». [2] قوله: فلا؛ أي فليس للغرماء حق نقض البيع إذا وصل إليهم الثمن، والبيع بمثل القيمة وإن لم يكن في الثمن وفاء بديونهم. كذا في «الكفاية».
Due to the debt, the creditor has the right to rescind his sale if its price does not reach him, and if it does reach and there is no abuse in the sale, then no (right). And he only said: 'informing of the debt' because when the seller informs the buyer that the slave has a debt, and the buyer is pleased with that, it might be imagined that the sale is executed by the consent of the seller and the buyer. So we say: Even with this, the creditors have the authority to rescind the sale if the price does not reach them, and if it does reach, then if there is no abuse in the sale, then no (right), and if there is abuse, then either the abuse is removed or the sale is annulled. [11] His saying: 'The creditors have authority to rescind the sale' because their right is attached, which is the earning of the slave and the collection from his person, and in each one there is benefit. As in al-'Inayah. If it is said: When the master sells his slave who committed a crime after knowledge of the crime, he is choosing to pay the ransom, so why is he not here choosing to pay the debts from his wealth? The answer: The liability of the crime is an obligation on the master, so if it becomes impossible for him due to the sale, he is demanded for it because the obligation remains on him. As for the debt, it is an obligation on the slave's conscience such that it is not dropped by sale or emancipation, so that he is held accountable for it after emancipation. So when this is the case, the sale from the master is like him saying: I will pay his debt, and that is a promise of charity, so it is not binding. As in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [2] His saying: 'no' meaning there is no right for the creditors to annul the sale when the price reaches them, and the sale is at like value even if there is no fulfillment of their debt in the price. As in al-Kifayah.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn with its commentary. It addresses the situation where a master sells a slave who has a debt, and the seller informs the buyer of the debt. The initial ruling states that the creditors of the slave have the right to rescind the sale if the sale price has not reached them. If the price has reached them, but the sale was at a fair price (no abuse), then they have no right to rescind. The commentary clarifies that the reason the matn specifies 'informing of the debt' is to prevent the false impression that the sale is valid simply because both parties consented. The creditors' authority to rescind stems from their right to have the slave work to pay the debt or to collect from his value. The passage then addresses an objection: Why is the master not considered to be paying off the debt by selling the slave, similar to the case of a criminal slave where the master is bound to pay the ransom? The distinction is that in the criminal case, the liability is on the master, whereas the debt is on the slave and remains even after sale. The master's sale is merely a charitable promise unless the price actually reaches the creditors. Finally, the commentator clarifies that if the price reaches the creditors and the sale is at market value, they have no right to annul even if it does not fully cover the debt. This lesson demonstrates the nuanced rules regarding creditors' rights in transactions involving debtor slaves.

Key Terms
الغرماء (al-ghuramā'): Creditors of the slave who have a right to his earnings or price
المحاباة (al-muḥābāh): Selling below market value, i.e., favoritism in price
الاستسعاء (al-istis'ā'): The process of having the slave work to earn money to pay off debts

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What gives the creditors the right to rescind the sale of a debtor slave?
A) The sale was below market value and the price has reached them
B) The price has not reached them, or if it has, the sale was below market value
C) The buyer was unaware of the debt
D) The master gave a charitable promise

Key Takeaway
The creditor's right to rescind is conditional upon the price not reaching them or the sale being below market value.

Creditor's Litigation Against Buyer When Seller is Absent
Ruling on whether the buyer can litigate when seller is absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi positions on whether a creditor can sue the buyer when the seller is absent and state the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
A creditor cannot sue the buyer when the seller is absent because that would judge an absent defendant.

This lesson examines a specific issue in Hanafi sales law: if a seller has a debt on the item sold and the buyer denies it, can the creditor litigate with the buyer? The Hanafi imams disagreed, and their reasoning reveals important principles about litigation and absent parties.

Guided Path
1
State the majority Hanafi position
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the creditor cannot litigate with the buyer when the seller is absent.
2
Identify the opposing position
Abu Yusuf holds that the creditor can litigate with the buyer because he claims ownership of the property.
3
Follow the proof for each position
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that the lawsuit would imply rescinding the contract, which is a judgment against the absent seller. Abu Yusuf argues that the creditor is a khasm (opponent) because he claims a right in the property.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The majority responds that the creditor is not a direct party to the contract, so he cannot be a khasm against the buyer.
5
Determine the practical consequence and tarjih
Under the majority view, the creditor must pursue the seller, not the buyer. The mufta bihi is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
This is confirmed by the commentary in al-Kifayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يخاصم المشتري منكراً دينه إن غاب بائعه: أي إذا كان البائع غائباً، الذي أبيعك مديون، وفائدة هذا الإعلام سقوط خيار المشتري في الرد بعيب الدين، حتى يقع البيع لازماً فيما بين البائع والمشتري، وإن لم يكن لازماً في حق الغرماء. كذا في «الكفاية». [3] قوله: إن غاب; أما إذا غاب المشتري والبائع حاضر فلا خصومة بينهم وبين المشتري منكراً للدين، فالدائن لا يخاصمه عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد; لأنه ليس خصماً له، وعند أبي يوسف له هو خصمه، ويقضي للغريم بدينه، لأنه يدعي الملك لنفسه فيكون خصماً لكل من ينازعه، ولهما: أن الدعوى يتضمن فسخ العقد، وفي الفسخ قضاء على الغائب.
And the buyer does not litigate (against someone) denying his debt if his seller is absent: meaning when the seller is absent, 'the one I sold you is in debt', and the benefit of this information is the dropping of the buyer's option of return due to the defect of debt, so that the sale becomes binding between the seller and the buyer, even if it is not binding with respect to the creditors. As in al-Kifayah. [3] His saying: 'if he is absent'; As for when the buyer is absent and the seller is present, there is no litigation between them and the buyer denying the debt, so the creditor does not litigate with him according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, because he is not an opponent for him. And according to Abu Yusuf, he is his opponent, and he rules for the creditor to (obtain) his debt, because he claims ownership for himself, so he is an opponent for anyone who disputes him. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) that the lawsuit entails the rescission of the contract, and in rescission there is judgment against the absent party.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The matn states a rule: if the seller is absent and the buyer denies the debt, the buyer does not litigate (i.e., the creditor cannot sue the buyer). The phrase 'if his seller is absent' is clarified: it means the seller is absent, so the buyer has no opponent. The benefit of the seller informing the buyer of the debt is that the buyer loses the option to return the slave due to the defect of debt; the sale becomes binding between the parties, though it is not binding regarding the creditors (who can still rescind). The disagreement among the Hanafi imams is about whether the creditor can litigate with the buyer when the seller is absent. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the creditor cannot litigate with the buyer because he is not a direct opponent; the lawsuit would involve rescission, which would be a judgment against the absent seller. According to Abu Yusuf, the creditor is an opponent because he claims ownership of the slave, and thus can sue anyone who disputes his right. The mufta bihi is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as the commentary shows.

Key Terms
خصم (khaṣm): Opponent or litigant in a lawsuit
فسخ العقد (faskh al-'aqd): Rescission of the contract, which would be a judgment against the absent seller

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, why cannot the creditor litigate with the buyer when the seller is absent?
A) The buyer is not liable for the debt
B) The lawsuit would involve rescinding the contract, which is a judgment against the absent seller
C) The creditor does not have a claim against the buyer
D) The seller must be present for any transaction

Key Takeaway
A creditor cannot directly sue the buyer when the seller is absent because that would require a judgment against the absent seller.

Slave Claiming Authorization: Istihsan Over Qiyas
Ruling on a slave claiming to be authorized

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a slave who claims to be authorized in trade and explain the istihsan reasoning that overrides qiyas.

Core Idea
A slave's claim to be authorized is accepted by istihsan to facilitate trade, even though qiyas would reject it.

In this passage from the chapter on the authorized slave (al-ma'dhun), the Hanafi school addresses a practical problem: a slave arrives in a city claiming his master allowed him to trade. Strict analogy (qiyas) would dismiss his claim because it involves an admission against his master. However, the school adopts istihsan (juristic preference) to accept his report, recognizing that commerce requires trust in such situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the Hanafi ruling
A slave appears in a city, claims to be authorized for trade, and buys/sells. According to the Hanafi school, he is considered authorized (ma'dhun) and his transactions are valid.
The ruling is from the text: 'فهو مأذون' (so he is authorized).
2
Understand the qiyas objection
Qiyas would not accept the slave's claim because he is reporting two things: his own slavery (admission against himself, valid) and the master's permission (admission against the master, not valid). The analogy is drawn from the general principle that a slave's admission against his master is not acceptable.
The text states: 'والقياس أن لا يقبل قوله' (qiyas is that his word is not accepted).
3
Analyze the istihsan reasoning
Istihsan overrides qiyas here. The reason: the slave's report falls under transactions, where reports of a single person are accepted for public benefit. People need to accept such claims because masters commonly send slaves to trade in distant lands. The analogy is to someone claiming to be an agent or mudarib.
The text compares to 'وكيل' (agent) and 'مضارب' (mudarib) – single reports accepted.
4
Note the practical legal effect
Once the slave is considered authorized, his transactions are valid. His person is not immediately liable to creditors until the buyer appears, but creditors can claim the value from the seller if the slave has disposed of the goods.
The passage discusses creditors' rights after the sale.
5
Recognize the methodological difference
This ruling is specific to Hanafi fiqh, which prioritizes istihsan over qiyas in such cases. Other schools may not accept the slave's claim without proof, reflecting a difference in usul al-fiqh.
Mukhtalaf fih between Hanafi and others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المأذون أحكامه
ولو اشترى عبد، وباع ساكتاً عن إذنِهِ وحجره فهو مأذون. عبد قدم مصراً، وقال: أنا عبد فلان مأذون في التجارة، ويبيع ويشتري فهو مأذون. البائع في رقبة العبد بلا خلاف، حتى يحضر المشتري; لأن الملك واليد للمشتري، وإبطال ذلك بدون حضوره لا يمكن فيه، فما لم يبطل ملك المشتري لا تكون الرقبة محلاً لحق الغرماء، إلا أن لهم أن يضمنوا البائع قيمته; لأن البيع والتسليم صار مفوّتاً محل حقهم. كذا في «الكفاية». [1] قوله: والمشتري منكراً... الخ; إنما قيد بالإنكار; لأن المشتري إذا أقر بدينهم وصدقهم في الدعوى كان لهم أن يردوا البيع بلا خلاف. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي». [2] قوله: قضاء على الغائب; ولأن العقد قائم بكل من العاقدين، وفسخه حكم عليهما. [3] قوله: فهو مأذون; وهو استحسان، والقياس أن لا يقبل قوله; لأنه خبر عن شيئين أحدهما أخبر أنه مملوك، وهذا إقرار منه على نفسه، والثاني أخبر أنه مأذون في التجارة، وهو إقرار على المولى، وإقراره عليه ليس بحجة. كذا في «العناية». ووجه الاستحسان: إن هذا خبر في المعاملات، وخبر الواحد فيها مقبول، ألا ترى أن واحداً إذا قال: أنا وكيل فلان أو مضاربه، وليس ثمة أحد يكذبه يقبل قوله; لأن الظاهر أنه يصدق ولا يكذب، وكذا هاهنا; لأن في التجارة منفعة لمولاه، فالظاهر أنه مأذون ما لم يتبين الحجر; ولأن للناس حاجة إلى قبول قول الآحاد في هذا الباب; لأن الإنسان يبعث عبده في الآفاق لينجر ويضارب.
Chapter of the Authorized Slave: Its Rulings. And if a slave buys and sells while silent about his permission and interdiction, then he is authorized. A slave came to Egypt and said: I am the slave of so-and-so, authorized in trade, and he buys and sells, so he is authorized. The seller has (right) over the slave's person without dispute, until the buyer appears; because ownership and possession belong to the buyer, and nullifying that without his presence is not possible. So as long as the buyer's ownership is not nullified, the slave's person is not a locus for the creditors' right, except that they can claim the seller's value; because the sale and delivery have extinguished their right. As in al-Kifayah. [1] His saying: 'and the buyer denying... etc.'; He only restricted it to denial because when the buyer acknowledges their debt and confirms their claim, they have the right to rescind the sale without dispute. As in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [2] His saying: 'judgment against the absent'; because the contract is binding on both contractors, and its rescission is a ruling against them. [3] His saying: 'so he is authorized'; and this is istihsan, and the qiyas is that his word is not accepted; because he is reporting two things: one that he is owned, which is an admission against himself, and the second that he is authorized in trade, which is an admission against the master, and his admission is not valid against him. As in al-'Inayah. And the face of istihsan: This is a report in transactions, and the report of a single person therein is accepted. Do you not see that when someone says: I am the agent or mudarib of so-and-so, and there is no one to deny him, his word is accepted; because the apparent is that he is truthful and does not lie. Similarly here; because in trade there is benefit for his master, so the apparent is that he is authorized as long as hindrance is not evident; and because the people have need to accept the word of individuals in this chapter; because a person sends his slave to faraway places to trade and do mudaraba.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is from the beginning of the chapter on the authorized slave (al-ma'dhun). It begins with a ruling: if a slave buys and sells while his master remains silent without explicitly granting permission or prohibiting him, the slave is considered authorized. The commentary provides an example: a slave comes to Egypt and claims to be authorized, and he is accepted as such. The discussion then addresses a related point about creditors' rights when the buyer has already taken possession. The key part is the istihsan ruling: qiyas would require the slave's claim not to be accepted because it involves admission against the master, but istihsan allows it on the basis of public need and the principle that a single person's report in transactions is accepted. This is comparable to accepting someone's claim of being an agent. The Hanafi school gives weight to practical necessity over strict analogy in this case.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave who has been granted permission by his master to engage in trade
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that departs from strict qiyas for public benefit or necessity
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning; comparing a new case to an established case based on a common effective cause

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a slave's claim to be authorized in trade accepted despite qiyas?
A) Because qiyas requires it
B) Because istihsan overrides qiyas for public need
C) Because the master's silence implies permission
D) Because the slave is free

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a slave's claim to be authorized in trade is accepted by istihsan, overriding qiyas, to facilitate commerce.

The Authorized Slave and the Creditor's Lawsuit: Hanafi Dispute
Intra-madhhab dispute and ruling on slave's trading status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that a slave trading in silence is considered authorized, and explain why Abu Hanifa and Muhammad deny the creditor the right to sue the buyer directly while Abu Yusuf allows it.

Core Idea
A silent slave is treated as authorized by istihsan, but the creditor cannot sue the buyer who denies the debt—per Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a practical scenario in classical commerce: a slave buys and sells property without stating whether his master has permitted trade. The Hanafi school uses istihsan to deem such a slave authorized, facilitating trade and benefiting the master. However, the imams disagree on the creditor's procedural right: can the creditor sue the buyer when the buyer denies the debt? Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say no, because the lawsuit implies rescinding the contract, which would affect the absent master. Abu Yusuf says yes, because the buyer claims ownership for himself, making him a direct opponent. Understanding this ikhtilaf clarifies the limits of procedural litigation in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling on the Slave's Status
Note the primary ruling: a slave who buys and sells while silent about authorization and interdiction is considered authorized (ma'dhun). This is based on istihsan, not strict qiyas.
The slave's actions are taken as evidence of authorization.
2
State the Dispute on the Creditor's Lawsuit
Two opinions: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the creditor cannot sue the buyer if the buyer denies the debt; Abu Yusuf holds that the creditor can sue the buyer directly.
The dispute hinges on who is considered a proper litigant.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's Dalil
They argue that suing for the debt would require rescinding the contract between the slave and buyer, which is a ruling against the absent master (since the master is the real party in interest).
Rescission is a judgment against the missing party.
4
Analyze Abu Yusuf's Dalil
Abu Yusuf argues that the buyer claims ownership for himself, so he is a direct opponent to the creditor; therefore, the creditor can sue him.
The buyer's claim of ownership makes him a proper party.
5
Understand the Istihsan Basis for the Slave's Authorization
The slave's claim of being authorized is accepted as a solitary report in transactions, analogized to an agent or mudarib's claim, because of public need and the benefit to the master.
Istihsan overrides qiyas here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اشترى عبد، وباع ساكتاً عن إذنه وحجره فهو مأذون والمشتري منكراً للدين ، فالدَّائنُ لا يخاصمه عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد ؛ لأنه ليس خصماً له، وعند أبي يوسف له هو خصمه ، ويقضي للغريم بدينه ، لأنه يدعي الملك لنفسه فيكون خصماً لكلِّ مَن ينازعه، ولهما : أن الدعوى يتضمَّنُ فسخ العقد، وفي الفسخ قضاء على الغائب (ولو اشترى عبد، وباع ساكتاً عن إذنِهِ وحجره فهو مأذون)، عبد قدم مصراً ، وقال : أنا عبد فلان مأذون في التجارة، ويبيع ويشتري فهو مأذون البائع في رقبة العبد بلا خلاف، حتى يحضر المشتري ؛ لأن الملك واليد للمشتري ، وإبطال ذلك بدون حضوره لا يمكن فيه ، فما لم يبطل ملك المشتري لا تكون الرقبة محلاً لحق الغرماء، إلا أنّ لهم أن يضمنوا البائع قيمته ؛ لأنّ البيع والتسليم صار مفوّتاً محل حقهم. كذا في «الكفاية» [1] قوله : والمشتري منكراً... الخ ؛ إنما قيد بالإنكار ؛ لأن المشتري إذا أقر بدينهم وصدقهم في الدعوى كان لهم أن يردوا البيع بلا خلاف. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي». [۲] قوله : قضاء على الغائب ; ولأنَّ العقدَ قائم بكل من العاقدين، وفسخه حكم عليهما. [٣]قوله : فهو مأذون ; وهو استحسان، والقياس أن لا يقبل قوله ; لأنه خبر عن شيئين أحدهما أخبر أنه مملوك، وهذا إقرار منه على نفسه، والثاني أخبر أنه مأذون في التجارة، وهو إقرار على المولى، وإقراره عليه ليس بحجة. كذا في «العناية». ووجه الاستحسان : إنّ هذا خبر في المعاملات، وخبر الواحد فيها مقبول، ألا ترى أن واحداً إذا قال: أنا وكيل فلان أو مضاربه، وليس ثمة أحد يكذبه يقبل قوله ; لأن الظاهر أنه يصدق ولا يكذب، وكذا هاهنا ; لأنَّ في التجارة منفعة لمولاه، فالظاهر أنه مأذون ما لم يتبين الحجر ; ولأن للناس حاجة إلى قبول قول الآحاد في هذا الباب ; لأن الإنسان يبعث عبده في الآفاق لينجر ويضارب. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» ناقلا عن «البيانية». وكذا إن سكت عن الإذن والحجر فإنَّ تصرُّفَهُ دليل على إذنه. قوله : دليل على إذنه ; والقياس أنه لا يجوز بيعه وشراؤه ; لأنه يحتمل أن يكون مأذوناً أو محجوراً، فلا يثبت الإذن بالشك، وما في يده مال لمولاه، فلا يباع في دينه بالشك. وفي الاستحسان: يجوز أن يطالب العبد بديون الغرماء ; لأنّ إقدامه على المبايعة مع الناس دليل على إقراره أنه مأذون ; لأنه عاقل، دين، فالظاهر أنه لا يقدم على تصرفات باطلة. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» ناقلا عن «البيانية».
And if a slave buys and sells while silent about his authorization and restriction, he is authorized. And the buyer denies the debt, so the creditor does not litigate against him according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, because he is not a litigant against him, and according to Abu Yusuf he is his litigant, and he adjudicates for the creditor his debt, because he claims ownership for himself, so he is a litigant against anyone who disputes him. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): the lawsuit entails rescission of the contract, and in rescission there is a ruling against the absent party. (And if a slave buys and sells while silent about his authorization and restriction, he is authorized). A slave came to Egypt and said: 'I am the slave of so-and-so, authorized in trade, and he buys and sells, so he is authorized.' The seller regarding the corpus of the slave without dispute, until the buyer appears, because ownership and possession are with the buyer, and nullifying that without his presence is not possible. So as long as the buyer's ownership is not nullified, the corpus is not a subject for the creditors' right, except that they can guarantee the seller its value, because the sale and delivery have caused the subject of their right to be lost. Thus in al-Kifaya. He said: 'The buyer denies... etc.' The restriction to denial is because if the buyer acknowledges their debt and confirms them in the claim, then they have the right to rescind the sale without dispute. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. He said: 'A ruling against the absent party.' Because the contract is binding on both contractors, and its rescission is a ruling on both of them. He said: 'He is authorized.' This is istihsan, and the qiyas is that his statement is not accepted, because it is a report about two things: one, he reports that he is a slave, this is an acknowledgment against himself; second, he reports that he is authorized in trade, which is an acknowledgment against the master, and his acknowledgment against him is not a proof. Thus in al-Inaya. The basis of istihsan: This is a report in transactions, and the solitary report is accepted in them. Do you not see that if someone says: 'I am the agent of so-and-so or his mudarib,' and no one is there to deny him, his statement is accepted? Because the apparent is that he tells the truth and does not lie. Likewise here, because in trade there is benefit for his master, so the apparent is that he is authorized as long as restriction is not evident. And because people have a need to accept such solitary reports in this chapter, because a man sends his slave to the horizons to trade and enter into mudaraba. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi, quoting from al-Bayaniyya. And likewise, if he is silent about authorization and restriction, his transaction is evidence of his authorization. He said: 'Evidence of his authorization.' The qiyas is that his sale and purchase are not permissible, because it is possible he is authorized or restricted, so authorization is not established by doubt, and what is in his hand is his master's property, so it cannot be sold for his debt based on doubt. And in istihsan: It is permissible to demand the slave for the debts of the creditors, because his initiating transactions with people is evidence of his acknowledgment that he is authorized. He is sane and indebted, so the apparent is that he does not engage in invalid transactions. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi, quoting from al-Bayaniyya.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage deals with the legal status of a slave who engages in buying and selling while being silent about whether he has his master's permission to trade (idhn) or is under interdiction (hajr). The author first states the ruling: such a slave is considered to be 'ma'dhun' (authorized) by virtue of his actions. However, a dispute arises between the founders of the Hanafi school regarding the standing of the creditor. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, if the buyer denies the debt, the creditor cannot sue the buyer directly because the buyer is not the slave's adversary. According to Abu Yusuf, the creditor can sue the buyer because the buyer claims ownership for himself, making him an opponent. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that suing for the debt implies rescinding the contract between the buyer and the slave, which would be a ruling against the absent buyer (the slave's master). The commentary elaborates using istihsan (juristic preference) to accept the slave's own statement that he is authorized, analogizing it to the acceptance of an agent's or mudarib's claim. The slave's transactions are considered evidence of his authorization, and this is extended even to a scenario where the slave is silent about his status. The passage also discusses the qiyas (analogy) opposed to istihsan, which would reject the slave's claim due to the possibility of restriction, but istihsan prevails due to public need and the benefit of trade.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave authorized by his master to engage in trade.
حجر (hajr): Interdiction; a legal restriction preventing the slave from transacting.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; departing from strict qiyas in favor of a more suitable ruling due to necessity or public interest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the buyer is not a litigant against the creditor.
B) Because the lawsuit implies rescission of the contract, which is a ruling against the absent master.
C) Because the slave is not authorized.
D) Because the buyer has rights over the property.

Key Takeaway
A slave's silence about authorization does not invalidate his trades, but the majority Hanafi view bars the creditor from suing the buyer directly to avoid ruling against the absent master.

Master's Acknowledgment Required for Sale of Slave's Property
Presents a ruling and its justification regarding the burden of proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for selling a slave's property for debt and explain why the master's acknowledgment is required based on Hanafi legal reasoning.

Core Idea
A slave's property cannot be sold for debt without the master's acknowledgment.

In Hanafi fiqh, a slave's legal capacity is limited. This lesson explains that selling a slave's property to satisfy his debts is contingent upon the master's clear acknowledgment that he authorized the slave to trade. The ruling balances the creditors' reliance on appearances with the master's right to deny unauthorized dealings. The burden of proof rests on the creditors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the condition: the slave's property is not sold for his debt unless the master acknowledges that he granted authorization.
2
Examine the proof
If the master denies authorization, the debt does not become apparent against him. The creditors relied on appearances, but the master did not deceive them.
The proof is rational (dalil aqli), not textual.
3
Address the objection
One might argue that the slave's word should be accepted to ease transactions. However, accepting it without proof would force the slave to constantly produce witnesses, causing hardship.
The principle of istihsan (juristic preference) is not applied here.
4
Understand the burden of proof
When the master claims the slave is restricted (mahjur), his word is accepted because it aligns with the original state (no authorization). Creditors must provide evidence (bayyinah).
The slave's claim of authorization is analogous to claims of emancipation or kitaba, requiring proof.
5
Apply the analogy
Just as emancipation claims need evidence, so does a claim of authorization against the master's denial.
This reinforces the rule that the master's acknowledgment is conclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يباع لدينه إلا إذا أقر سيده بإذنه ; لأنَّ المولى إذا لم يقرّ بالإذن، فالدين لا يظهر في حقه، والمعاملون إنما تضرروا لأنهم اعتمدوا على ظاهر الحال ، والمولى لم يغرَّهم. قلنا : إن قولهم لا يقبل لضاق الأمر على النّاس ; لأنه حينئذ يحتاج العبد إلى أن يستصحب مولاه آناء الليل وأطراف النهار، أو شاهدين عادلين يشهدان على الإذن والمضاربة، وما ضاق أمراه إذ اتّسع فيه. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» ناقلا عن «البيانية». [٢] قوله : إلا إذا أقرّ سيده ; أي يقول المولى : إنّه مأذون ; فإنه يباع حينئذ فإنّ حكم المأذون أن يباع في الدين، وأما إن قال إنّه محجورٌ فالقول قوله ; لأنه متمسك بالأصل، وعلى الغرماء البيِّنة ; لأنَّ دعوى العبدِ الإذن كدعوى الإعتاق والكتابة فلا يقبل قوله عند جحود المولى إلا بالبينة. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي». [٣]قوله : والمولى لم يغرهم ; أي لم يجعلهم مغروراً.
And he is not sold for his debt unless his master acknowledges his authorization, because if the master does not acknowledge authorization, the debt does not appear in his right, and the transactors only suffer harm because they relied on the apparent situation, and the master did not deceive them. We said: If their statement were not accepted, it would be difficult for people, because then the slave would need to bring his master at all times of night and day, or two just witnesses to testify to authorization and mudaraba, and what is difficult for him is expanded. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi, quoting from al-Bayaniyya. [2] He said: 'Unless his master acknowledges.' That is, the master says he is authorized; then he is sold, because the ruling for the authorized slave is that he is sold for debt. But if he says he is restricted, then his word is accepted, because he is adhering to the original state, and the burden of proof is on the creditors, because the slave's claim of authorization is like a claim of emancipation or kitaba, so his word is not accepted in the face of the master's denial except with evidence. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [3] He said: 'And the master did not deceive them.' That is, he did not make them deceived.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage explains the condition for selling a slave's property to satisfy his debts. The ruling is that the slave's property cannot be sold for his debt unless the master affirmatively acknowledges that he had authorized the slave to trade. The rationale is that the debt does not become apparent against the master if he denies authorization. The creditors relied on appearances, but the master did not cause them to be deceived. The commentary then addresses a potential objection: if the slave's claim of authorization were accepted without proof, it would make matters easier for people, but the master's denial is given weight because he is upholding the original state (no authorization). The burden of proof falls on the creditors because the slave's claim of authorization is analogous to claims of emancipation or kitaba, which require evidence. The passage also clarifies the meaning of 'the master did not deceive them' – he did not present them with false appearances.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment or admission; here, the master's affirmation that the slave is authorized.
بيّنة (bayyinah): Proof or evidence; the burden of proof lies on the creditors to show authorization.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the slave cannot be held liable.
D) Because the creditors have no rights.

Key Takeaway
The master's acknowledgment is essential before the slave's property can be sold for debt.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
