Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Dividing Shuf'ah Among Partners
Inter-madhhab dispute over division of shuf'ah proceeds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on dividing shuf'ah among multiple partners, state the Shafi'i counter-argument, and evaluate the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between the two views.

Core Idea
When multiple partners claim shuf'ah, Hanafi divides equally; Shafi'i divides proportionally — the dispute hinges on the scope of shuf'ah texts.

This lesson examines a classical dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding how a purchased share should be divided among multiple partners who all have the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah). The Hanafi position, based on general ahadith, is that the partners divide the purchased share equally regardless of their original ownership percentages. Al-Shafi'i, relying on analogical reasoning and a specific hadith, argues for proportional division. Understanding this ikhtilaf is essential for correctly applying shuf'ah in multi-partner properties.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: Hanafi holds that when multiple partners exercise shuf'ah on a sold share, they divide it equally among themselves, irrespective of their original shares in the property.
The text explicitly says: 'and according to us: they divide in all cases into halves.'
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that shuf'ah should be divided proportionally to each partner's existing ownership stake. The text gives three examples with shares 1/2, 1/3, 1/6.
Shafi'i's view is described as 'biqadr al-milk' (in proportion to ownership).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Al-Shafi'i relies on the hadith 'al-shuf'ah fima lam yuqsam' (shuf'ah is in what has not been divided) and the argument that shuf'ah is contrary to qiyas and thus cannot be extended beyond its explicit scope.
The burden of division is on the original property, not on the partners' shares.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafi rebuts by citing the hadith 'al-jar ahaqqu bi-shuf'atihi' (the neighbor has more right to his shuf'ah) which is general and covers even divided properties if they share a path. They also argue that shuf'ah is a right established by Law, not subject to analogy.
Equality is presumed when the Law does not specify proportion.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: In the example of shares 1/2, 1/3, 1/6, when the half-share is sold, Hanafi gives each of the other two partners 1/4 of the total property each, while Shafi'i gives 1/6 to the third-share partner and 1/12 to the sixth-share partner.
This difference applies in all cases of shuf'ah among partners.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند الشافعي (٢) لا تثبت الشفعة وقال الشافعي له : يجب بقدر الملك، حتى لو كانت الدار بين ثلاثة لأحد نصفُها، وللآخر ثلثها، وللثالث سدسها ، فباعَ صاحب النصف نصيبه فالشريكان لو أخذاه بالشفعة يقسمانه أثلاثاً ، ثلث لصاحب السدس، وثلثان لصاحب الثلث. ولو باع صاحب الثلث ثلثه، يقسمه للشريكان الباقيان أرباعاً، ربع لصاحب السدس، وثلاثة الأرباع لصاحب النصف، ولو باع صاحب السدس سدسه، اقتسمه الشريكان الباقيان أخماساً، خمساه لصاحب الثلث، وثلاثة أخماسه لصاحب النصف عنده، وعندنا : يقتسمان في الكلّ ،نصفين، هذا ما في «البناية شرح الهداية». [١) قوله : ثم ؛ لفظ : ثمّ ؛  ُيفيدُ ثبوت حق الشفعة لكل واحد من هؤلاء، وأيضاً يفيد الترتيب. كذا في «الهداية)) [٢]قوله : للجار الملاصق ؛ سواء كان مكاتباً أو مأذوناً أو ذميّاً عملاً بإطلاق الحديث، وهو قوله : «الشفعة لشريك لم يقاسم»). [٣]قوله : وعند الشافعي لا تثبت الشفعة ... الخ ؛ وله قوله : «الشفعة فيما لم يقسم، فإذا وقعت الحدود وصرفت الطرق فلا شفعة» (۱) ؛ ولأنّ حق الشفعة خلافٌ للقياس لما فيه من تملك المال على الغير من غير رضاه، وقد ورد الشرع به فيما لم يقسم، وهذا ليس في معناه ؛ لأنّ مؤنة القسمة تلزمه في الأصل دون الفرع. ولنا : ما أخرجه أصحاب السنن الأربع، وهو قوله : «الجار أحق بشفعته ، ينتظر بها وإن كان غائباً إذا كان طريقهما واحداً (۲) ، هذا ما في «الهداية»(۳)، وحواشيه.
And according to al-Shafi'i, shuf'ah is not established. Al-Shafi'i said: it is obligatory in proportion to ownership, such that if the house is among three, one has half, another a third, and a third a sixth; then if the owner of the half sells his share, the two partners, if they take it by shuf'ah, divide it into three: a third for the owner of the sixth, and two thirds for the owner of the third. And if the owner of the third sells his third, he divides it between the remaining two partners into fourths: a quarter for the owner of the sixth, and three quarters for the owner of the half. And if the owner of the sixth sells his sixth, the remaining two partners divide it into fifths: two fifths for the owner of the third, and three fifths for the owner of the half. That is according to him; and according to us: they divide in all cases into halves. This is what is in al-Binaya Sharh al-Hidayah. [1) He said: 'Thumma' the word 'thumma' indicates the establishment of the right of shuf'ah for each of these, and also indicates order. Thus in al-Hidayah. [2] He said: For the adjacent neighbor, whether he is a mukatib, or a ma'dhun, or a dhimmi, acting on the general application of the hadith, which is his saying: 'Shuf'ah is for a partner who has not divided.' [3] He said: And according to al-Shafi'i, shuf'ah is not established... etc. He has his saying: 'Shuf'ah is in what has not been divided, so if boundaries are set and roads demarcated, there is no shuf'ah.' And because the right of shuf'ah is contrary to qiyas as it involves taking ownership of property from another without his consent, and the Law has brought it regarding what has not been divided, and this [neighbor situation] is not in its meaning; because the burden of division falls on the original [property] not the branch. And our proof: what the authors of the four Sunan narrated, which is his saying: 'The neighbor has more right to his shuf'ah, he waits for it even if he is absent when their path is one.' This is in al-Hidayah and its glosses.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i concerning the distribution of shuf'ah rights when multiple partners exercise pre-emption. According to the Hanafi school, when several partners in a property wish to claim shuf'ah upon the sale of one partner's share, they divide the taken property equally among themselves, regardless of their original ownership shares. In contrast, al-Shafi'i holds that shuf'ah is exercised proportionally to each partner's existing share in the property. The text provides three detailed examples: if the shares are half, third, and sixth, then upon the sale of the half, the remaining two divide the purchased share in thirds according to Shafi'i, but in halves according to Hanafi. Similarly for other sales. The commentators then cite al-Shafi'i's proof: the hadith 'shuf'ah is in what has not been divided' implies that once boundaries are set, shuf'ah ceases. Moreover, shuf'ah is an exception to qiyas (analogy) and is restricted to undivided properties. The Hanafi response relies on the hadith 'the neighbor has more right to his shuf'ah', which is general and includes cases where boundaries are set, especially if they share a common path. The Hanafi view is preferred and is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
بقدر الملك (biqadr al-milk): In proportion to ownership share; al-Shafi'i's basis for dividing shuf'ah according to each partner's prior share.
خلاف للقياس (khilaf li-l-qiyas): Contrary to analogy; used here to argue that shuf'ah is an exception to normal property rules and thus cannot be extended beyond its explicit texts.
الجار أحق بشفعته (al-jar ahaqqu bi-shuf'atihi): The neighbor has more right to his shuf'ah; a hadith used by Hanafis to support equal division among partners.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

The First Demand of Preemption: Immediacy and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
States the ruling on the first demand and discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute about silence in the assembly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the requirements of the first demand of preemption (talab al-muwathaba) and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding silence in the assembly, identifying the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Preemption is for the one who leaps — immediate demand is wajib, silence voids it.

In Hanafi fiqh, preemption (shuf'a) is a right that must be claimed promptly. The first demand, called talab al-muwathaba, is an obligatory step that requires immediate action upon learning of the sale. This lesson examines the ruling, its proof, and a key intra-Hanafi disagreement: whether a moment's silence in the assembly nullifies the right or whether the preemptor has the option of the assembly (khiyar al-majlis). Understanding this teaches the importance of textual authority in resolving ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage defines the first demand (talab al-muwathaba) as an obligatory (wajib) act: the preemptor must demand preemption in the same assembly where he learns of the sale, using clear words.
The ruling is that failure to demand immediately voids the right.
2
Locate the dalil
The passage cites the hadith 'al-shuf'a liman wathabaha' (preemption is for the one who leaps to it). This hadith is narrated by Shurayh via 'Abd al-Razzaq. The term 'wathabaha' implies haste and promptness.
The hadith is zanni in thubut but provides the basis for the requirement of immediate action.
3
Understand the meaning of muwathaba
Muwathaba derives from 'wathb' (leaping), symbolizing extreme promptness. The passage explains that the first demand is named to emphasize the need for speed, as opposed to mere presence.
The three demands are muwathaba, ishhad (witnessing), and tamlik (ownership/litigation).
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
The passage presents a dispute: al-Karkhi and some Bukharan mashayikh hold that the preemptor has khiyar al-majlis (option of the assembly), so silence does not void. The original narration (zahir al-riwaya) states that even slight silence voids. Most mashayikh of Balkh and Bukhara follow the original narration.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not a cross-madhhab one.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The passage, via Ibn 'Abidin, declares that the original narration (immediate nullification) is the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) and thus the fatwa is upon it. The opposing view, though chosen in some matn works, is overridden because it contradicts the stronger transmission.
Authority weight: zahir_riwayah; tarjih by Ibn 'Abidin in Radd al-Muhtar.

Chapter of demanding preemption. The preemptor demands it in the assembly of his knowledge of the sale with a wording that makes his demand understood, like 'I demanded preemption' and similar. And it is a demand of immediate action (muwathaba). [1] For the chapter of demanding preemption: (And the preemptor demands it in the assembly of his knowledge of the sale with a wording that makes his demand understood, like 'I demanded preemption' and similar, such as 'I am a demander of preemption' or 'I demand it.' Al-Jalabi said it. (3) (1) They differed regarding the choice of the two positions. What al-Karkhi chose and some mashayikh of Bukhara adhered to in the matn is that it is like the assembly of the mukhayyara for reflection, and this is the most correct as in al-Durar (2:209). And the original narration (rivayat al-asl) is that it is conditional upon his immediate knowledge of the sale so that if he remains silent for an hour it is void; and the mashayikh of Balkh and most mashayikh of Bukhara inclined to this, and the fatwa is upon it as in al-Jawahir. Ibn 'Abidin said in Radd al-Muhtar (5:143): This is an explicit preference while it is the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), so it is given precedence over the preference of the matn which adheres to the contrary, because it is implicit. (2) Al-Zayla'i said in Nasb al-Raya (4:176): It is gharib (strange), and 'Abd al-Razzaq narrated it in his Musannaf (8:83) from the saying of Shurayh. The end. Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a hashya from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya on the chapter of preemption (shuf'a) from Sharh al-Wiqaya. It opens by stating the ruling for the first demand (talab al-muwathaba): the preemptor must make his demand immediately upon learning of the sale, in the same assembly (majlis al-'ilm), using clear words like 'I demanded preemption' or 'I am a demander of preemption.' The author then explains that there are three demands—muwathaba (immediate action), ishhad (witnessing), and tamlik (ownership/litigation). The term 'muwathaba' derives from wathb (leaping), emphasizing extreme promptness, as supported by the hadith 'al-shuf'a liman wathabaha' (preemption belongs to the one who leaps to it). The core legal discussion is the intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of silence in the assembly. Al-Karkhi and some Bukharan scholars hold that the preemptor has an 'option of the assembly' (khiyar al-majlis), similar to the option of reflection (mukhayyara), so silence does not immediately void his right. The majority of Balkh and most Bukharan scholars, however, adhere to the 'original narration' (zahir al-riwaya) that requires immediate action: even a moment's silence voids preemption. The fatwa is given on this latter view, as stated in al-Jawahir, and Ibn 'Abidin affirms that this is the explicit preference of the apparent narration, thus overriding the choice of the matn authors who relied on al-Karkhi's view. The passage thus shows the interplay between textual authority and scholarly preference in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'a): Preemption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a property sold to a third party by matching the price.
طلب المواثبة (talab al-muwathaba): The first demand of preemption, made immediately upon learning of the sale, characterized by haste and promptness.
خيار المجلس (khiyar al-majlis): Option of the assembly: the right of the preemptor to reflect for the duration of the gathering before finalizing his demand.
مجلس العلم (majlis al-'ilm): The assembly where the preemptor learns of the sale; the time and place for making the first demand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) His right of preemption is voided.
B) He retains the right as long as he is in the same assembly.
C) He must repeat the demand in a later assembly.
D) He must pay a penalty.

Key Takeaway
The first demand of preemption (talab al-muwathaba) must be made immediately upon learning of the sale; any delay, even silence in the assembly, voids the right according to the mufta bihi.

The Second Demand of Preemption: Talab al-Ishhad
Explains the second demand where the preemptor bears witnesses at the property or before the seller/buyer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the procedure of the second demand of preemption (talab al-ishhad) and identify against whom the preemptor must witness depending on possession of the property.

Core Idea
The second demand of preemption secures your right via formal witnesses; you must witness against the current possessor.

In the previous lesson, you learned the first demand (muwathaba), where the preemptor immediately declares intent upon learning of the sale. The second demand is the formal bearing of witnesses. This lesson explains how and against whom to make this demand, including a key nuance: if the property hasn't been delivered, witness against the seller; if delivered, against the buyer. The text also discusses a combined scenario where the first demand with witnesses covers both.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second demand and its terminology
Read the Arabic text and note that after the first demand, the preemptor 'bears witness at the property or against whoever is with him from seller or buyer'. This is called talab al-ishhad.
The term 'ishhad' means making witnesses present.
2
Determine the object of witnessing
The preemptor can witness at the property itself (against the property) OR against a person who is present with the property – either the seller or the buyer.
The pronoun in 'on whoever is with him' refers to the person who has the property.
3
Distinguish based on possession
If the seller has not yet handed over the property, he is the possessor (sahib al-yad) and must be witnessed against. If the buyer has taken delivery, the buyer is the owner and litigant, so witnessing against the buyer is valid.
This is clarified in the footnote: 'if the house is in his hand... otherwise, against the buyer'.
4
Recognize the combined scenario
If the preemptor hears the sale in the presence of the buyer, seller, or the house itself, making the first demand and simultaneously witnessing suffices for both demands.
Shaykh al-Islam emphasizes that this context provides sufficient knowledge.

Commentary
This passage continues the hashya on the chapter of preemption, now explaining the second demand (talab al-ishhad: the demand of witness). The preemptor, after making the immediate first demand (muwathaba), must proceed to the property or to the seller or buyer who is present (literally 'who is with the property') and publicly declare: 'So-and-so bought this house, I am the preemptor, I already demanded and now demand again—bear witness.' This formal witness establishes his claim for subsequent litigation. The hashya clarifies the term 'yathibu' (leaps) from al-wathba, reinforcing the idea of prompt action. It then discusses the precise application: the preemptor should witness against the seller if the property is still in the seller's possession (i.e., not yet handed over to the buyer), because the seller is the one with possession and ability to defend; if the property has been handed over, he should witness against the buyer, as he is now the owner and the litigant. Shaykh al-Islam (likely a Hanafi authority) adds an important nuance: if the preemptor learns of the sale in the presence of the buyer, seller, or the house itself, making the first demand (muwathaba) and simultaneously witnessing suffices for both demands, as the context indicates knowledge. Otherwise, he must make the first demand immediately and then follow with the second demand of witness separately. The footnotes cite Durar al-Hukkam and Dhakhirat al-'Uqba for further details.

Key Terms
طلب الإشهاد (talab al-ishhad): The second demand of preemption, where the preemptor bears witness to his claim at the property or before the seller/buyer.
الوثبة (al-wathba): Leap or jump; used metaphorically to denote prompt action in the first demand.
صاحب اليد (sahib al-yad): The possessor of the property; the person who currently holds the property (either seller or buyer).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Both

Key Takeaway
The second demand of preemption (talab al-ishhad) must be directed at the current possessor of the property – the seller if not delivered, the buyer if delivered – and can be combined with the first demand if the preemptor learns of the sale in the presence of a party.

Three Demands in Shuf'ah and the Dispute on Delay
Intra-Hanafi dispute on delaying talab al-khusumah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf vs Muhammad regarding delaying talab al-khusumah and apply the reasoning (talil) to evaluate which view governs in a given scenario.

Core Idea
A right once fixed is not lost by mere delay—but excessive delay may harm others, leading to a Hanafi internal split.

In the previous discussion, we learned about the three demands required to establish the right of preemption (shuf'ah): immediate demand (talab al-muwathabah), demand by witnesses (talab al-ishhad), and demand before a judge (talab al-khusumah). The first two must be performed promptly or the right is lost. But what if the preemptor delays the third demand—going to court? The Hanafi school splits on this question. Let us unpack the reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three demands
List the three types of talab in shuf'ah and their respective purposes. The first is verbal, the second formalises with witnesses, the third initiates litigation.
Remember that the first two are time-sensitive; the third is contested.
2
Locate the dispute
The passage states that delaying talab al-khusumah does not nullify the right according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that a one-month delay without excuse nullifies it.
The dispute is recorded in zahir al-riwayah, but later fatwa practice varies.
3
Analyze each side's reasoning (talil)
Examine the 'illa for each position: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that a fixed right cannot lapse without explicit waiver; Muhammad argues that indefinite delay harms the buyer, so a limit of one month is set.
Note that both sides use qiyas: Abu Hanifa analogises to other fixed rights; Muhammad analogises to the harm principle.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical consequence: under Muhammad's view, the buyer can act freely after a month; under Abu Hanifa's, the buyer remains at risk until the preemptor acts.
The buyer can petition the judge to compel action, but this is difficult if the preemptor is absent.
5
Apply the fatwa hierarchy
Understand that zahir al-riwayah supports Abu Hanifa, but many later authorities (e.g., in al-Hidayah, al-Durr al-Mukhtar) give fatwa on Muhammad's view to prevent harm. Some also distinguish between excuses and non-excuses.
Know that the mufta bihi varies by context; always check the local practice.

Source Text

Then he demands before a judge saying: 'So-and-so bought such a house, and I am its preemptor by such a house of mine, so order him to surrender it to me.' This is a demand for ownership and litigation. By delaying it, the preemption is not invalidated. Know that this demand is only obligatory when able to have witnesses at the house and before the possessor, so if he is able and does not have witnesses, his preemption is invalidated. Thus in al-Fawa'id al-'Arifiyyah. The word 'fa-murhu' is a command form with an object pronoun. This is the third demand, and the preemption is not nullified by delaying the demand of litigation according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: If he leaves it for a month after the witness-demand, his preemption is nullified. The reasoning for Muhammad's statement: If it were never nullified by delaying litigation, the buyer would suffer harm, as he could not dispose of the property for fear of the preemptor invalidating the sale, so we measured the harm by a month because it is a delay; anything less is immediate. The reasoning for Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: A right once established and settled does not lapse except by explicit waiver, just like other rights. Thus in al-Fawa'id al-'Arifiyyah. In al-'Ayni: The fatwa is on the statement of Imam; from the second (Abu Yusuf) there is a report that if he leaves the lawsuit in a single session of the judge without excuse, his preemption is nullified. Muhammad (may Allah be pleased with him) said: If he delays up to a month without excuse, his preemption is nullified, due to changing circumstances of people in intending harm to others. The place of dispute is when he delays without excuse; if there is an excuse like illness, imprisonment and unable to appoint a deputy, or a judge who does not recognize preemption by adjacency in his town, it is not nullified by consensus even if the period lengthens, because he is unable to litigate in his own district. The reasoning for Imam's statement: His right is established, so it does not lapse by delay after that; and the harm mentioned can be prevented by the buyer raising the matter to the judge, who will order the preemptor to take or leave. However, this is problematic if the preemptor is absent, where it is not nullified by delay; if material harm is considered, it would lapse, since there is no difference in harm whether present or absent. In al-Kafi: If there is no judge in the town, it is not nullified by delay by consensus. Thus in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (2:148). Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Wiqayah discusses the three levels of demand (talab) required for establishing shuf'ah (preemption). The first demand is talab al-muwathabah (immediate demand upon learning of the sale), which is a spontaneous verbal expression of claiming the right. The second is talab al-ishhad (demand by witnesses), where the preemptor must formally testify before witnesses at the property or before the buyer. If he is able to do so and neglects, his shuf'ah is lost. However, if he is on a journey and cannot, he may appoint a deputy, send a letter, etc. The third demand is talab al-khusumah (demand before a judge), which is a legal claim in court. The passage then focuses on the effect of delaying this third demand. According to zahir al-riwayah (the well-known position transmitted from the founding imams), delaying talab al-khusumah does not nullify the right of shuf'ah. This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Their reasoning is that once a right is established, it does not lapse merely by delay; it can only be forfeited by explicit renunciation, analogous to other property rights. The potential harm to the buyer can be addressed by his petitioning the judge to compel the preemptor to either take the property or release it. Muhammad al-Shaybani, however, argued that if the preemptor delays talab al-khusumah for a full month without a valid excuse, his right is forfeited. His reasoning is based on preventing harm to the buyer, who would otherwise be unable to dispose of the property indefinitely. Muhammad fixed the limit at one month as a practical measure. The later authorities are divided: some give fatwa on Muhammad's view, others on the Imam's view. The passage also notes that the dispute only applies when the delay is without excuse; if there is a valid excuse (illness, imprisonment, absence of a judge, etc.), the right is not lost by consensus. The discussion highlights the interplay between established legal principles and the need to prevent harm in transactions.

Key Terms
طلب المواثبة (talab al-muwathabah): The immediate demand made by the preemptor upon learning of the sale, without any formalities.
طلب الإشهاد (talab al-ishhad): The demand made by having witnesses attest to the preemptor's claim at the property or in the presence of the buyer.
طلب الخصومة (talab al-khusumah): The formal demand before a judge, initiating litigation to claim the preempted property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, after how long does delaying talab al-khusumah without excuse nullify the shuf'ah?
A) Immediately upon delay
B) One month
C) One year
D) It never nullifies

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school internally disagrees on whether delaying a lawsuit in shuf'ah forfeits the right; the majority of later practice follows Muhammad's view to prevent harm, but zahir al-riwayah supports Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Delay in Shuf'a: When Does the Right Lapse?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on delay in shuf'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three positions (Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) on the effect of delay on shuf'a, identify the mufta bihi view, and apply the exception for excuses.

Core Idea
Shuf'a lapses after one month without excuse by Muhammad's view, which is the mufta bihi.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: does the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) become invalid if the holder delays claiming it? The three eponymous imams differ, and the later Hanafi tradition has settled on the view of Imam Muhammad. Understanding this dispute teaches us how the school weighs the established right against the potential harm to the buyer and how a valid excuse preserves the right.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
State the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding the effect of delay on shuf'a. Note the agreed and disputed points.
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the right does not lapse by mere delay; Muhammad says it lapses after one month without excuse.
2
Understand the proofs (dalil)
For each position, identify the reasoning: Abu Hanifa argues the right is established (taqarrara) and cannot be lost; the buyer can go to court. Muhammad argues that changed social conditions necessitate a time limit to prevent harm.
Muhammad relies on istihsan (equity) to override the established right.
3
Note the exception for excuses
Identify the excusing conditions mentioned: illness, imprisonment, absence of a qadi who recognizes shuf'a by adjacency, or inability to appoint an agent. In these cases, by consensus, the right does not lapse even if the delay is long.
The dispute only applies when there is no excuse.
4
Recognize the mufta bihi (operative view)
Identify that the relied-upon position for issuing fatwa is Muhammad's view, as supported by many later authorities (e.g., al-Sharnublaliya, Ibn Abidin).
The text says 'وبه يفتى' (and with it fatwa is given) and elaborates in the footnote.

وقال محمد: إذا أخره شهراً بطلت، وبه يفتى(1). وهو طلب الأخذ بعدما استقرت شفعته بالإشهاد، وهذا قول الإمام وأبي يوسف في ظاهر الرواية. وفي العيني: الفتوى على قول الإمام، وعن الثاني إذا ترك المخاصمة في مجلس من مجالس القاضي من غير عذر بطلت شفعته، وقال محمد: وإن أخر إلى شهر بغير عذر بطلت شفعته لتغير أحوال الناس في قصد الإضرار بالغير. ومحل الخلاف إذا أخر بغير عذر، ولو كان بعذر من مرض أو حبس ولم يمكنه التوكيل، أو قاض لا يرى الشفعة بالجوار في بلدته، لا تسقط بالإجماع، وإن طالت المدة؛ لكونه لا يتمكن من الخصومة في مصره. وجه قول الإمام: إن حقه قد تقرر فلا يسقط بالتأخير بعد ذلك، وما ذكره من الضرر يمكن دفعه بأن يرفع المشتري الأمر إلى الحاكم، فيؤمر الشفيع بالأخذ أو الترك. على أنه مشكل فيما إذا كان الشفيع غائباً، حيث لا يسقط بالتأخير، ولو كان ضرورةً تراعى سقطت إذ لا فرق في الضرر بين أن يكون حاضراً أو غائباً. وفي «الكافي»: لو لم يكن في البلدة قاض لا تبطل بالتأخير بالإجماع. كذا في «البحر الرائق». And Muhammad said: If he delays it a month, it becomes invalid, and this is the mufta bih. And it is the demand for taking after his shuf'a has been established by witness testimony, and this is the statement of the Imam and Abu Yusuf in the apparent narration. And in al-'Ayni: The fatwa is on the statement of the Imam, and from the second [Abu Yusuf?] if he abandons litigation in a session of the qadi's court without excuse, his shuf'a becomes invalid, and Muhammad said: And if he delays to a month without excuse, his shuf'a becomes invalid due to the change of people's circumstances in intending to harm others. And the place of disagreement is when he delays without an excuse, and if there is an excuse of illness or imprisonment and he cannot appoint an agent, or a qadi who does not recognize shuf'a by adjacency in his town, it does not lapse by consensus, even if the period lengthens, because he is unable to litigate in his own town. The proof of the Imam's statement: His right has been established, so it does not lapse by delay after that. And the harm he mentioned can be repelled by the buyer raising the matter to the judge, who then orders the shafi' to take or leave. However, it is problematic when the shafi' is absent, as it does not lapse by delay, and if necessity were considered, it would lapse, as there is no difference in harm between being present or absent. And in al-Kafi: If there is no qadi in the town, it does not become invalid by delay by consensus. This is in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of delay (ta'khir) on the right of shuf'a (pre-emption). The text begins by stating the position of Imam Muhammad: if the shafi' delays claiming his right for a month without excuse, his shuf'a becomes invalid (batalat), and this is the relied-upon position for issuing fatwa. The phrase 'هو طلب الأخذ بعدما استقرت شفعته بالإشهاد' clarifies that the discussion pertains to the demand for taking possession after the shuf'a has already been established by testimony (ishhad). This is the view of Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf in the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah). Then the commentary mentions al-'Ayni's statement that the fatwa is on the Imam's view, and from the second (presumably Muhammad) that if the shafi' abandons litigation in a single session of the qadi without excuse, the right lapses; but Muhammad's well-known view is that a month delay without excuse causes lapse, due to changed social circumstances where people may intentionally harm others by delaying. The text then limits the area of disagreement: it is only when the delay is without excuse. If there is a valid excuse—such as illness, imprisonment preventing appointment of an agent, or the qadi does not recognize shuf'a by adjacency—then by consensus the right does not lapse even if the period is long, because the shafi' is unable to litigate in his town. The proof of Imam Abu Hanifa's view is that the right has been established and does not lapse by mere delay. The harm to the buyer can be prevented by the buyer raising the issue to the judge, who will order the shafi' to take or leave. However, a problem arises when the shafi' is absent: his right does not lapse by delay even if necessity were considered, but the text notes that if necessity were considered consistently, it should lapse, as there is no difference in harm whether present or absent. Finally, al-Kafi states that if there is no qadi in the town, the right does not lapse by delay by consensus. The footnote lists authorities who support Muhammad's view as the basis for fatwa.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): The right of pre-emption: a co-owner or adjoining owner can purchase the sold property by paying its price.
تأخير (ta'khir): Delay in claiming the right of shuf'a after it has been established.
إشهاد (ishhad): Making witnesses testify to establish the shuf'a right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in this text, what happens if the shafi' delays taking action for a month without an excuse?
A) His right remains intact.
B) His shuf'a becomes invalid.
C) He must pay compensation to the buyer.
D) The sale is nullified.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the right of shuf'a is lost if the holder delays claiming it for one month without a valid excuse.

Procedure of Proving Shuf'ah and Bringing the Price
Commentary on judicial procedure in shuf'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi judicial procedure for proving ownership and the sale in a pre-emption claim, and explain the ruling regarding bringing the price and the cross-madhhab difference on returning the oath.

Core Idea
In Hanafi shuf'ah, the price need not be brought at claim; later fatwa voids the right if unpaid in three days; the oath is never returned to the claimant.

This lesson unpacks a passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya on 'Sharh al-Wiqaya' detailing the judge's procedure in a shuf'ah claim. We will examine two crucial aspects: the sequence of questioning and oaths to prove the pre-emptor's ownership and the sale, and the intra-Hanafi dispute over whether the pre-emptor must present the price at the time of the claim. The commentator also contrasts the Hanafi rule on oaths with the Shafi'i view, grounding it in the well-known hadith on burden of proof.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two procedural phases
Identify that the passage covers (1) the process of proving the pre-emptor's entitlement through ownership and sale, and (2) the requirement (or lack thereof) to bring the price at the time of claim.
The two phases are distinct and have different rulings.
2
Trace the judge's questioning sequence
Follow the order: judge asks the buyer about pre-emptor's ownership, then about the sale. Note that the pre-emptor may provide evidence; if not, the buyer is sworn on knowledge.
Possession alone is insufficient; evidence or oath is required.
3
Analyze the oath distinction
Understand the two types of oath: 'ala al-sabab (on the cause) and 'ala al-hasil (on the result). The hadith 'al-bayyina 'ala al-muddai' is cited to justify that the oath is never returned to the claimant.
This contrasts with Shafi'i who allows returning the oath.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on the price
Identify the zahir al-riwaya (price not required at claim) versus Imam Muhammad's view (judgment requires price), and the later fatwa according to al-Hawi that non-payment within three days voids the right.
The mufta bihi is the voiding position.
5
Evaluate the cross-madhhab difference
Note the Shafi'i position that if the defendant refuses to swear, the oath is returned to the claimant. The Hanafis consider this an innovation, citing the hadith and historical practice.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf, not a methodological flaw.

سأل القاضي الخصم عنها : أي عن مالكيَّةِ الشَّفيع الدَّارَ المشفوع بها ، فإن أقر بملك ما يشفع به، أو نكل عن الحلف على العلم بأنه مالك كذا، أو برهن الشفيع سأله عن الشراء، فإن أقرّ به أو نكل عن الحلف على الحاصل أو السبب الشفيع : يعني الملك : أي يشفع به فيها وإلا كلّفه بإقامة البينة ؛ لأن اليد ظاهر محتمل ، فلا تكفي اليد لإثبات الاستحقاق. ثم يسأل القاضي المدعي قبل أن يعترف المشتري بملكه الذي يشفع به، وكلّف القاضي الشفيع إقامة البينة... الخ، عن موضع الدار وحدودها ؛ لأن الشفيع ادعى حقا في تلك الدار، وصار كما ادعى رقبتها : يعني ذات دار حق، وملك من باست، فإذا شفعته ؛ لاختلاف أسبابها ، فإنّها تحتمل أن يكون السبب يد ملك، ويحتمل أن يكون عارية أو إجارة وغير ذلك فإن قال : أنا شفيعها بدار تلاصقها بين ذلك سأله . الآن، ثم دعواه. عن سبب وإذا عجز عن البينة استحلف القاضي المشتري : بالله لا يعلم أنّ الشفيع مالك للذي ذكره مما يشفع به؛ لأنه ادعى على المشتري ، فلو أقر المشتري به لزمه، وهذا الاستحلاف إنمـا هـو علـى ما في يد غيره، فيحلف المشتري على العلم، فإنّ نكل المشتري أو قامت البينة للشفيع ثبت ملكه الدار التي بها يشفع وثبت الجوار، وبعد ذلك سأل القاضي المدعى عليه، هل ابتاع أم لا ؟ يعني هل مشتري أم لا ؟ فإن أنكر الابتياع قيل للشفيع : أقم البينة ؛ لأنَّ الشفعة لا تجب إلا بعد ثبوت البيع، وثبوته بالحجة، فإن عجز عن البينة استحلف القاضي المشتري : بالله ما ابتاع أو بالله استحق الشفيع في هذا الدار شفعة من الوجه الذي ذكره ، فهذا على الحاصل، والأوّل أعني ما ابتاع على السبب. كذا في «الفوائد العارفية». قوله : سأل القاضي الخصم ؛ قيل : مما يجب عليه أن يسأل بعده عن سبب شفعته ؛ لاختلاف أسبابها ، فإنّها على مراتب كما عرفت فيما سبق، فلا بد من بيان السبب ؛ ليعلم هل هو محجور بغيره أو لا، وربّما ظنّ مما ليس بسبب كالجار المقابل سبباً ، فإنه سبب عند شريح له إذا كان أقرب باباً. كذا في «الأكملية» قاله الجلبي قوله : وقد سبق في كتاب الدعوى» ؛ عبارة: «كتاب الدعوى» هكذا : ولا يرد اليمين على المدعي، وإن نكل خصمه فيه خلاف الشافعي ، فإنّ عنده إذا نكل الخصم يردُّ اليمين على المدعي. وعندنا هذا بدعة، وأوّل من قضى به به معاویه ، وهو مخالف للحديث المشهور. انتهى. والحديث المشهور هو قوله : «البينة على المدعي واليمينُ على من أنكر ). قوله : وإن لم يحضر الثمن.... الخ ؛ أي لا يلزم الشفيع إحضار الثمن وقت الدعوى، بل يجوز له المنازعة، وإن لم يحضر الثمن إلى مجلس القاضي، فإن قضى له بشفعة يأمره بإحضار الثمن، وهو ظاهر الرواية. وعن محمد : إنه لا يقضي له بالشفعة حتى يحضر الثمن احترازاً عن طلب الشفيع الشفعة وراجعة إلى القاضي، والقاضي يؤجله ثلاثة أيام لنقد الثمن فإن جاء به إلى هذه المدة فيها، وإلا أبطل شفعته. وفي فتاوى أبي الليث»: الشفيع إذا طلب الشفعة، قال المشتري : هاتِ الدراهم وخذ شفعتك، فإن أمكن إحضار الدراهم في ثلاثةِ أيام صحت الدعوى، وإلا بطلت شفعته، قال الصدر الشهيد الله : والمختارُ أنها لا تبطل وفي «الحاوي»: إنّها تبطل، وفي «جامع الفتاوى»: الفتوى اليوم على قول «الحاوي».
The judge asked the opponent about it: i.e., about the ownership of the pre-emptor of the property subject to pre-emption. If he admitted ownership of what he pre-empts with, or he refrained from taking an oath upon knowledge that he is the owner of such, or the pre-emptor provided evidence, then he asked him about the purchase. If he admitted it, or refrained from taking an oath upon the result or the cause. The pre-emptor: i.e., the property: meaning he pre-empts with it. Otherwise, he requires him to produce evidence; because possession is a probable indication, so possession is not sufficient to prove entitlement. Then the judge asks the claimant before the buyer acknowledges his ownership with which he pre-empts, and the judge requires the pre-emptor to produce evidence... etc., about the location of the property and its boundaries; because the pre-emptor claimed a right in that property, and it became as if he claimed its corpus: meaning the same property right, and ownership from [؟], so if he pre-empts it; due to the difference of its causes, for it may be that the cause is ownership by possession, and it may be a loan or lease or otherwise. If he says: I am its pre-emptor by an adjacent property, then he asks him about that. Now, then his claim. About the cause. And if he is unable to produce evidence, the judge makes the buyer take an oath: By Allah, he does not know that the pre-emptor owns what he mentioned by which he pre-empts; because he claimed against the buyer, so if the buyer admitted it, it would be binding on him. This oath is only about what is in another's hand, so the buyer takes an oath upon knowledge. If the buyer refrains from taking the oath or evidence is produced for the pre-emptor, his ownership of the property with which he pre-empts is established, and adjacency is established. After that, the judge asked the defendant: Did you buy or not? Meaning: Are you a buyer or not? If he denies the purchase, it is said to the pre-emptor: Produce evidence; because pre-emption is not obligatory except after establishing the sale, and its establishment is by proof. If he is unable to produce evidence, the judge makes the buyer take an oath: By Allah, I did not buy, or By Allah, the pre-emptor has no right of pre-emption in this property from the aspect he mentioned. This is upon the result, and the first, i.e., 'I did not buy', is upon the cause. Thus in 'al-Fawa'id al-'Arifiyyah'. His statement: 'The judge asked the opponent'; it is said: He must also ask him after that about the cause of his pre-emption; due to the difference of its causes, for they are in levels as you have learned previously, so it is necessary to explain the cause; to know whether he is prevented by another or not, and perhaps he may think that what is not a cause, like the opposite neighbor, is a cause, for it is a cause according to Shuraih if he is nearer in door. Thus in 'al-Akmaliyyah', said by al-Jalabi. His statement: 'And it has preceded in the Book of Claims'; the expression of the 'Book of Claims' is as follows: The oath is not returned to the claimant, even if his opponent refrains, contrary to al-Shafi'i, for according to him, if the opponent refrains, the oath is returned to the claimant. And according to us, this is an innovation, and the first to judge by it was Mu'awiyah, and it contradicts the well-known hadith. End. And the well-known hadith is his statement: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies.' His statement: 'And if the pre-emptor did not bring the price...' etc.; i.e., it is not required of the pre-emptor to bring the price at the time of the claim, rather he is allowed to litigate even if he did not bring the price to the judge's court. If judgment is given for him for pre-emption, he orders him to bring the price, and this is the apparent narration. And from Muhammad: He does not give judgment for pre-emption until he brings the price, as a precaution against the pre-emptor seeking pre-emption and returning to the judge, and the judge gives him three days to pay the price. If he brings it within this period, it is valid; otherwise, his pre-emption is void. And in the 'Fatawa of Abu al-Layth': The pre-emptor, when he requests pre-emption, the buyer says: Bring the dirhams and take your pre-emption. If it is possible to bring the dirhams within three days, the claim is valid; otherwise, his pre-emption is void. Al-Sadr al-Shahid said: The chosen view is that it is not void. And in 'al-Hawi': It is void. And in 'Jami' al-Fatawa': The fatwa today is according to the statement of 'al-Hawi'.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from 'Umda al-Ri'aya on 'Sharh al-Wiqaya', explaining the judicial procedure in a pre-emption (shuf'ah) claim. The commentary expands on two main rulings: (1) the judge's questioning of the buyer to verify the pre-emptor's ownership and the sale, and (2) whether the pre-emptor must bring the price at the time of claim. The author begins by explaining that the judge asks the buyer about the pre-emptor's ownership of the property that gives the right of pre-emption (the 'property through which he pre-empts'). If the buyer admits it or refuses to swear that he does not know, or if the pre-emptor provides evidence, the judge then asks about the purchase. The pre-emptor must prove his ownership and the sale; possession alone is insufficient because it is ambiguous. If the pre-emptor cannot produce evidence, the judge administers an oath to the buyer. The commentary distinguishes between oaths on 'the result' (al-hasil) and 'the cause' (al-sabab). The well-known hadith 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies' is cited, and the Hanafi position that the oath is not returned to the claimant even if the defendant refuses is affirmed, contrary to al-Shafi'i. This is stated to be an innovation. Regarding the price, the commentary presents the majority view (zahir al-riwaya) that the pre-emptor need not bring the price at the time of claim; the judge orders it after judgment. Imam Muhammad holds that judgment is not given until the price is brought, and the judge allows three days. Later fatwa collections vary: al-Sadr al-Shahid prefers non-voiding, but 'al-Hawi' and 'Jami' al-Fatawa' state the fatwa is according to 'al-Hawi' that the pre-emption is void if the price is not brought within three days.

Key Terms
الشُّفْعَة (al-shuf'ah): The right of a co-owner or neighbor to acquire property that has been sold to a third party, by paying the price.
شَفِيع (shafi'): The person who claims the right of pre-emption.
اليمين على الحاصل (al-yamin 'ala al-hasil): An oath that denies the result or the claim itself, e.g., 'By Allah, the pre-emptor has no right of pre-emption.'
الثَّمَن (al-thaman): The price payable by the pre-emptor to acquire the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens if the pre-emptor (shafi') cannot bring the price at the time of claiming pre-emption?
A) The claim is dismissed immediately.
B) The claim is valid, and the judge orders payment after judgment.
C) The claim is postponed indefinitely.
D) The pre-emptor must pay a fine.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi shuf'ah procedure does not require the price at the time of claim, but later fatwa voids the right if not paid within three days; the oath is never returned to the claimant, based on the hadith 'al-bayyina 'ala al-muddai wa al-yamin 'ala man ankara'.

When the Pre-emptor Must Bring the Price
Ruling on price requirement and oath in pre-emption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether the pre-emptor must bring the price at the time of suit, distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and explain the correct form of oath for the defendant depending on the basis of pre-emption.

Core Idea
The pre-emptor need not bring the price to court when suing — only after judgment does payment become due.

One of the practical questions in pre-emption (shuf'ah) is whether the pre-emptor must produce the purchase price at the moment he files his claim. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the Hanafi position: he may litigate without the price, but after the judge rules in his favor, he must pay and the buyer may detain the property until paid. The text also addresses a related issue: how the defendant (buyer) should swear when the basis of the pre-emption is agreed or disputed, contrasting the Hanafi rule with Shafi'i practice.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two stages of the case
Identify the act being ruled upon: the pre-emptor's suit for pre-emption. The ruling says he may sue without bringing the price; after judgment, he must bring it. This is the core legal distinction.
2
Analyze the oath issue
Determine whether the basis of pre-emption is agreed (e.g., joint ownership) or disputed (e.g., neighborhood). This affects the form of the oath the defendant must take: on the result or on the cause.
The goal is to avoid perjury if the defendant would swear according to Shafi'i school.
3
Locate the dalil for the oath rule
The text cites the hadith 'al-bayyinah 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yamin 'ala man ankara' to support that the oath is not returned to the plaintiff if the defendant refuses. This is a direct proof against the Shafi'i position.
This hadith is a cornerstone of Hanafi procedural law.
4
Map the intra-Hanafi dispute on price requirement
The zahir al-riwaya (Abu Hanifa) says no requirement at suit. Imam Muhammad says judgment only after price brought, with a three-day grace. A narration from Hasan from Abu Hanifa supports Muhammad. Later fatwa (Jami' al-Fatawa) adopts al-Hawi's view that pre-emption is void if price not brought in three days.
This shows how later practice shifted to protect the buyer's interest.
5
Determine the operative fatwa
According to the text, the fatwa today follows al-Hawi, making pre-emption void if the price is not brought within three days. However, the author notes that the chosen view (al-mukhtar) of al-Sadr al-Shahid was non-voidance.
This illustrates the importance of distinguishing between the established riwaya and later fatwa practice.

أو برهن الشفيع قضي له بها ، وإن لم يحضر الثمن اعلم أن ثبوت الشفعة إن كان متفقاً عليه يحلف على الحاصل : بالله ما استحق هذا الشفيع الشفعة علي، وإن كان مختلفاً فيه كشفعة الجوار يحلف على السبب : بالله ما اشتريت هذه الدار ؛ لأنه ربما يحلف على الحاصل بمذهب الشافعي ، وقد سبق في «كتاب الدعوى) ، (أو) برهن الشفيع قضي له بها ، وإن لم يحضر الثمن" السبب ؛ ليعلم هل هو محجور بغيره أو لا، وربما ظن مما ليس بسبب كالجار المقابل سبباً ، فإنه سبب عند شريح له إذا كان أقرب باباً. كذا في «الأكملية» قاله الجلبي [١] قوله : وقد سبق في كتاب الدعوى» ؛ عبارة: «كتاب الدعوى» هكذا : ولا يرد اليمين على المدعي، وإن نكل خصمه فيه خلاف الشافعي ، فإن عنده إذا نكل الخصم يرد اليمين على المدعي. وعندنا هذا بدعة، وأول من قضى به به معاوية ، وهو مخالف للحديث المشهور. انتهى. والحديث المشهور هو قوله : «البينة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر). (٢) قوله : وإن لم يحضر الثمن.... الخ ؛ أي لا يلزم الشفيع إحضار الثمن وقت الدعوى، بل يجوز له المنازعة، وإن لم يحضر الثمن إلى مجلس القاضي، فإن قضى له بشفعة يأمره بإحضار الثمن، وهو ظاهر الرواية. وعن محمد : إنه لا يقضي له بالشفعة حتى يحضر الثمن احترازاً عن طلب الشفيع الشفعة وراجعة إلى القاضي، والقاضي يؤجله ثلاثة أيام لنقد الثمن فإن جاء به إلى هذه المدة فيها، وإلا أبطل شفعته. وفي فتاوى أبي الليث»: الشفيع إذا طلب الشفعة، قال المشتري : هاتِ الدراهم وخذ شفعتك، فإن أمكن إحضار الدراهم في ثلاثة أيام صحت الدعوى، وإلا بطلت شفعته، قال الصدر الشهيد الله : والمختار أنها لا تبطل وفي «الحاوي»: إنها تبطل، وفي «جامع الفتاوى»: الفتوى اليوم على قول «الحاوي». وقت الدعوى وإذا قضي لزمه إحضاره ، وللمشتري حبس الدار لقبض ثمنه ، فلو قيل للشفيع : أد الثمن فأخر لا تبطل شفعته " والخصم البائع إن لم يسلم وقت الدعوى وإذا قضي لزمه إحضاره ) ، وللمشتري حبس الدار لقبض ثمنه " ، فلو قيل للشفيع : أد الثمن فأخر لا تبطل شفعته والخصم البائع إن لم يسلم " ) : أي خصم الشفيع البائع إن لم يسلم المبيع إلى المشتري. [١]قوله : لزمه إحضاره ؛ هذا ظاهر الرواية، وعن محمد له : إنه لا يقضي حتى يحضر الشفيع الثمن، وهو رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة ؛ لأن الشفيع عساه يكون مفلساً ليتوقف القضاء على إحضاره، حتى لا يهلك مال المشتري، وجه الظاهر أنه لا ثمن له عليه قبل القضاء ؛ ولهذا لا يشترط تسليمه، فكذا لا يشترط إحضاره. هذا ما في «الهداية». [٢] قوله : لقبض ثمنه ؛ وينفذ القضاء عند محمد الله أيضاً ؛ لأنه فصل مجتهد فيه ، ووجب عليه الثمن فيه، فلو أخر أداء الثمن بعدما قال له : ادفع الثمن إليه لا يبطل شفعته ؛ لأنها تأكدت بالخصومة عند القاضي. كذا في «الهداية».
Or if the pre-emptor produces proof, judgment is given for him, even if he does not bring the price. Know that if the establishment of pre-emption is agreed upon, he swears on the result: 'By Allah, this pre-emptor does not deserve pre-emption over me.' If it is disputed, such as pre-emption of neighborhood, he swears on the cause: 'By Allah, I did not buy this house,' because he might swear on the result according to the Shafi'i school, and it has preceded in the Book of Claims. (Or) if the pre-emptor produces proof, judgment is given for him, even if he does not bring the price? The cause: to know whether he is barred by another or not, and perhaps he thinks something that is not a cause, like the opposite neighbor, is a cause, as it is a cause according to Shuraih if he has a nearer door. (So in al-Akmaliyah, al-Jalabi said.) His statement: 'And it has preceded in the Book of Claims': the phrase from the Book of Claims is: 'The oath is not returned to the plaintiff, even if his adversary refuses, contrary to al-Shafi'i, for according to him if the adversary refuses, the oath is returned to the plaintiff. According to us this is an innovation, and the first to rule by it was Mu'awiyah, and it contradicts the famous hadith.' End. The famous hadith is his saying: 'The burden of proof is on the plaintiff, and the oath is on the one who denies.' (2) His statement: 'Even if he does not bring the price... etc.': i.e., the pre-emptor is not required to bring the price at the time of the suit; rather, he may dispute even if he does not bring the price to the court of the judge. Then if judgment is given for him regarding pre-emption, he orders him to bring the price, and this is the zahir al-riwaya. From Muhammad: that judgment is not given for him for pre-emption until he brings the price, as a precaution against the pre-emptor seeking pre-emption and returning to the judge, and the judge grants him three days to pay the price. If he brings it within this period, [it is valid]; otherwise his pre-emption is void. In the Fatawa of Abu al-Layth: If the pre-emptor demands pre-emption and the buyer says, 'Bring the dirhams and take your pre-emption,' if it is possible to bring the dirhams within three days, the suit is valid; otherwise his pre-emption is void. Al-Sadr al-Shahid said: The chosen view is that it is not void. In al-Hawi: it is void. In Jami' al-Fatawa: The fatwa today is according to the opinion of al-Hawi. The time of the suit: and if judgment is given, he is required to bring it, and the buyer has the right to detain the house to receive his price. So if it is said to the pre-emptor: 'Pay the price,' and he delays, his pre-emption is not void. And the adversary is the seller if he does not deliver: i.e., the pre-emptor's adversary is the seller if he has not delivered the sold property to the buyer. [1] His statement: 'He is required to bring it': this is the zahir al-riwaya. From Muhammad: that he does not give judgment until the pre-emptor brings the price, and this is a narration of Hasan from Abu Hanifa; because the pre-emptor might be insolvent, so judgment is postponed until he brings the price, so that the buyer's property is not wasted. The apparent view's reasoning is that there is no price incumbent upon him before judgment; therefore delivery is not required, so likewise bringing it is not required. This is what is in al-Hidayah. [2] His statement: 'To receive his price': and the judgment is executed according to Muhammad also; because it is a matter of ijtihad, and the price becomes obligatory upon him by it. So if he delays paying the price after he told him: 'Pay the price to him,' his pre-emption is not void; because it has been confirmed by the litigation before the judge. (So in al-Hidayah).
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (though the proof is referenced implicitly) and includes intra-Hanafi dispute. The core issue is whether the pre-emptor (shafi') must physically bring the purchase price to the court when he first files his claim. The text states the general rule: if the pre-emptor produces evidence (proof of his right), the judge rules in his favor even if he has not brought the price. Then it discusses two related sub-issues: (1) the oath to be taken by the defendant (buyer) and (2) the requirement of bringing the price. On the oath: if the basis of pre-emption is agreed upon (e.g., joint ownership), the defendant swears on the result (that the plaintiff has no right). If the basis is disputed (e.g., pre-emption by neighborhood), he swears on the cause (that he did not buy). This subtlety is to avoid perjury if the defendant would swear according to Shafi'i school that he bought. The reference to Kitab al-Da'wa clarifies that in Hanafi law, the oath is not returned to the plaintiff if the defendant refuses; that is an innovation introduced by Mu'awiya and contradicts the famous hadith 'al-bayyinah 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yamin 'ala man ankara' (The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the denier). Then the text returns to the price requirement: the apparent riwaya (zahir al-riwaya) is that the pre-emptor need not bring the price at the suit; he can litigate without it. Once judgment is given, he is ordered to bring it. This is the position of Abu Hanifa and the majority of the school. A counter-opinion from Imam Muhammad (also a narration from Hasan from Abu Hanifa) says judgment is only given after the price is brought, to avoid the pre-emptor being insolvent and wasting the buyer's money. The judge would then grant a three-day respite. The fatwa literature shows disagreement: Abu al-Layth's Fatawa says the suit is valid if the price can be brought in three days, else void. Al-Sadr al-Shahid chose non-voidance. Al-Hawi opts for voidance. Jami' al-Fatawa states the fatwa today follows al-Hawi. The text then continues with a reaffirmation of the apparent riwaya: upon judgment, the pre-emptor must bring the price, and the buyer may detain the property until paid. If the pre-emptor delays after being told to pay, his right is not void because it has been confirmed by litigation. The mention 'the adversary is the seller if he has not delivered' introduces a separate rule about who the pre-emptor sues when the seller still holds the property.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): Right of pre-emption: a property right allowing a co-owner or neighbor to purchase the share/ property sold to an outsider by compulsory sale.
الثمن (al-thaman): The purchase price that the pre-emptor must pay to the buyer to acquire the property.
إحضار الثمن (ihdar al-thaman): Physically bringing the price money to the court at the time of suit or after judgment.
الحالف على الحاصل (al-halif 'ala al-hasil): Swearing on the result: that the opponent has no right, without specifying the cause.
الحالف على السبب (al-halif 'ala al-sabab): Swearing on the cause: e.g., 'I did not buy this property,' to avoid perjury when the basis is disputed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the zahir al-riwayah of the Hanafi school, when must the pre-emptor bring the purchase price?
A) At the moment he files the pre-emption suit.
B) Within three days of filing the suit.
C) Never; he can always delay payment.
D) Only after the judge rules in his favor.

Key Takeaway
The pre-emptor may sue without bringing the price; only after judgment does payment become obligatory, and the defendant's oath depends on whether the basis of pre-emption is agreed or disputed.

The Adversary in Pre-emption: Seller vs. Buyer
Determining whom the pre-emptor sues when the seller has not delivered

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the proper adversary in a pre-emption claim based on possession of the sold property.

Core Idea
In pre-emption, the seller is the adversary if he still possesses the property.

Pre-emption (shuf'a) gives a neighbor or partner the right to purchase a sold property. But who should be sued? This passage clarifies that the answer depends on possession: if the seller has not delivered the property to the buyer, the pre-emptor's adversary is the seller. This ruling underscores the legal weight of possession (yad) in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Issue
Recognize that the passage addresses a procedural question: who is the proper adversary (khasm) in a pre-emption lawsuit?
The context is a sale where the seller has not yet handed over the property.
2
State the Ruling
The ruling: If the seller has not delivered the property to the buyer, the pre-emptor's adversary is the seller.
The Arabic phrase 'إن لم يسلم' (if he does not deliver) is the key condition.
3
Explain the Rationale
Possession (yad) is a legally significant right. The seller still holds the property, so he has the standing to be sued.
The judge will not hear evidence until the buyer is also present because the buyer has ownership.
4
Consider the Exception
If the buyer has already taken possession, the adversary becomes the buyer. The seller becomes irrelevant ('ajnabi') because he no longer has possession or ownership.
This exception shows the rule's dependence on possession.
5
Apply to a Scenario
Given a pre-emption case, determine who has possession and identify the proper adversary accordingly.
This step integrates the rule into practical legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخصم البائع إن لم يسلم " ) : أي خصم الشفيع البائع إن لم يسلم المبيع إلى المشتري. [٣]قوله : والخصم البائع إن لم يسلّم ... الخ ؛ يعني إذا حضر الشفيع [و] البائع ، والمبيع في يد البائع فللشفيع أن يخاصم البائع في الشفعة ؛ لأن اليد للبائع، واليديد مستحقة معتبرة كيد المالك، ولا يسمع القاضي البينة حتى يحضر المشتري. فيفسخ البيع بمحضر من المشتري، ويقضي بالشفعة على البائع، ويجعل العهدة على البائع ؛ لأن الملك للمشتري واليد للبائع والقاضي يقضي بهما للشفيع، فلا بد من حضور البائع والمشتري ، بخلاف ما إذا كانت الدار قد قبضها المشتري، حيث لا يعتبر حضور البائع ؛ لأنه صار أجنبياً ، إذ لا يبقى له يد، ولا ملك. كذا في «الفوائد العارفية).
And the adversary is the seller if he does not deliver: i.e., the pre-emptor's adversary is the seller if he has not delivered the sold property to the buyer. [3] His statement: 'And the adversary is the seller if he does not deliver... etc.': means when the pre-emptor and the seller are present, and the sold property is in the seller's hand, then the pre-emptor may sue the seller for pre-emption; because the hand (possession) is with the seller, and the hand is a considered right like the owner's hand. And the judge does not hear the evidence until the buyer is present. So he rescinds the sale in the presence of the buyer, and rules for pre-emption against the seller, and places the liability on the seller; because ownership is with the buyer and possession is with the seller, and the judge rules for the pre-emptor on both, so the presence of both the seller and the buyer is necessary. Contrary to when the house has been taken possession of by the buyer, where the presence of the seller is not considered; because he has become a stranger, as he no longer has possession or ownership. (So in al-Fawa'id al-'Aarifiyyah).
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that clarifies who the pre-emptor should sue in a pre-emption case. The rule is: if the seller has not yet delivered the sold property to the buyer (i.e., the seller still holds possession), then the pre-emptor's adversary (khasm) is the seller, not the buyer. The reasoning is that possession (yad) is a significant legal right, and the seller still has it. The judge will only hear evidence and proceed once the buyer is also present, because the buyer has ownership and his presence is needed for rescinding the sale. The judge rescinds the sale in the presence of the buyer, then rules for pre-emption against the seller, placing the liability (al-'uhdah) on the seller. However, if the buyer has already taken possession of the property, the seller becomes a stranger (ajnabi) with no possession or ownership, so his presence is not required; the pre-emptor sues the buyer directly. This distinction is recorded in al-Fawa'id al-'Aarifiyyah.

Key Terms
الخصم (al-khasm): The adversary or opponent in litigation; the party to be sued.
اليد (al-yad): Possession or control over property; a legally recognized right that can be the basis for lawsuits.
العهدة (al-'uhdah): Liability or guarantee; here meaning the seller bears responsibility for the pre-emption claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The buyer only
B) The seller only
C) Both seller and buyer are equally adversaries
D) Neither; the pre-emptor sues the judge

Key Takeaway
The proper adversary in pre-emption is determined by possession: if the seller has not delivered, sue the seller; if the buyer has possession, sue the buyer.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Producing the Price in Shuf'a
Explains the seller as opponent before delivery and intra-Hanafi dispute on producing the price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether the shafi' must produce the price before judgment, identify the intra-Hanafi difference, and explain the practical consequence of the dispute.

Core Idea
In shuf'a, the shafi' need not bring the price before judgment; delay does not invalidate his right.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the procedural requirement for the shafi' (pre-emptor) to produce the purchase price before the judge rules in his favor. The relied-upon view (zahir al-riwaya) does not require prior payment, while Muhammad al-Shaybani and a narration from Abu Hanifa hold that the judge should wait until the price is produced. Understanding this dispute clarifies when the right of shuf'a is confirmed and how the buyer's interests are protected.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Determine the precise point of disagreement: whether the shafi' must bring the price before the judge passes judgment.
The passage addresses this as part of the commentary on al-Wiqaya.
2
State the zahir al-riwaya position
The majority view (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) holds that no price is due before judgment, so bringing it is not required. The shafi' may delay payment without losing his right.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Identify the opposing view and its proof
Muhammad al-Shaybani (and al-Hasan's narration from Abu Hanifa) argues that the shafi' might be bankrupt, so the judge should ensure payment to protect the buyer. Thus, the judge should not rule until the price is produced.
This is a minority view within the Hanafi school.
4
Explain the Hanafi response and tarjih
The majority responds that no price is owed before the ruling, so there is no basis to require its production. The ruling itself creates the obligation; if the shafi' delays after judgment, his right remains confirmed. Al-Hidayah supports the majority view.
The practical consequence: under the majority, the judge rules first and payment comes later; under the minority, payment is a prerequisite for the ruling.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a shafi' who files a claim but does not bring the money. The judge, following the mufta bihi, issues a decree confirming the shafi's right. The shafi' then pays. If the judge followed the opposing view, he would dismiss the case until the price is produced.
This illustrates the thamarah al-khilaf.

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya addresses the procedural rules of shuf'a, focusing on who the shafi' should sue and when. The matn states that if the seller has not yet delivered the property to the buyer, the seller is the proper opponent. The shafi' may detain the house for his price, but if he is told to pay and delays, his right is not lost. The sharh first clarifies that 'it is necessary for him to bring it' refers to the price after judgment, not before. It then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: the zahir al-riwaya (from Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad) holds that the shafi' need not bring the price before judgment because no price is due until the ruling. However, a narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa says the judge should not rule until the price is brought, to protect the buyer from a bankrupt shafi'. The commentary favors the zahir al-riwaya, reasoning that without a prior debt, there is no requirement to bring the price. The passage further explains that when the property is still with the seller, the shafi' sues him because his possession is akin to ownership. The buyer must be present for the judge to hear evidence and rescind the sale, but if the buyer has already taken possession, the seller becomes a stranger and need not attend. The guarantee (uhdah) falls on the seller in this scenario.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): The right of pre-emption: a co-owner or neighbour may purchase the sold property by paying its price.
خصم (khasm): The opponent or party sued in a litigation.
عهدة (uhdah): Guarantee or responsibility for defects and claims against the sold property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The sale is automatically rescinded.
D) The buyer may cancel the sale.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi shuf'a, the shafi' does not need to bring the price before judgment; the right is confirmed first, and payment follows later.

Buyer's Presence and Guarantee Dispute in Shuf'a
Explains requirement of buyer's presence for judgment and Shafi'i dispute on who bears guarantee

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the necessity of the buyer's presence in shuf'a judgment and explain the difference between Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on the guarantee (uhdah) of the sold property.

Core Idea
Before delivery, guarantee is on seller; after delivery, it shifts to buyer—presence of buyer required for judgment when property is still with seller.

In shuf'a transactions, the presence of the buyer is required for the judge to rescind the sale because taking property from the seller's hand before delivery would cause loss akin to perishing. But what about responsibility for defects? The Hanafi school distinguishes before and after delivery, while the Shafi'i school places guarantee always on the buyer. This passage clarifies both the procedural requirement and the substantive guarantee dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two legal issues
The passage discusses two distinct but related issues: (1) when the buyer's presence is required for judgment in shuf'a, and (2) who bears the guarantee (uhdah) of the sold property.
2
State the Hanafi position on each issue
On presence: The seller and buyer must both be present when the property is still with the seller; after delivery, only the buyer need attend. On guarantee: Before delivery it is on the seller; after delivery it shifts to the buyer.
Based on the text: 'ولا يسمع البيِّنة عليه حتى يحضر المشتري... والعهدة على البائع' and the commentary clarifying after delivery.
3
Identify the Shafi'i position on guarantee
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the guarantee is on the buyer in all cases, whether the shafi' takes the property from the seller's hand or the buyer's hand, because in Shafi'i law all rights of the buyer revert to the seller (the previous owner).
Reference: 'وقال الشافعي له : العهدة على المشتري بكل حال'
4
Trace the reasoning for each school
Hanafi: The guarantee follows possession and completion of the contract; before delivery the seller has possession and the contract is incomplete. Shafi'i: The buyer is considered the owner from the moment of contract, so all rights and liabilities attach to him.
The Hanafi reasoning implies that possession (al-yadd) determines liability; Shafi'i uses the principle that ownership (al-milk) transfers at contract.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Hanafi, if a defect appears after delivery, the shafi' sues the buyer. Under Shafi'i, he sues the seller (the previous owner). This affects legal strategy and who bears the risk.
The text does not explicitly state the consequence, but it follows from the positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يسمع البيِّنة عليه حتى يحضر المشتري فيفسح بحضوره ويقضى للشفيع بالشفعة، والعهدة على البائع
[۱]
(ولا يسمع البيِّنة عليه حتى يحضر المشتري فيفسح بحضوره)، إنَّما يشترط حضور البائع والمشتري ؛ لأنَّ الملك له ، واليد للبائع ، فإذا سَلَّمَ إلى المشتري لا يشترط حضور البائع ؛ لأنَّهُ صارَ أجنبياً ، ويقضى للشفيع بالشفعة، والعهدة على البائع" (۱) ) ، حتى يجب تسليمُ الدَّارِ على البائع ، وعند الاستحقاق يكون عهدة الثّمن على البائع ، فيطلب منه.
[1] قوله : حضور البائع والمشتري ... الخ ؛ فلا بُدّ من اجتماعهما ؛ لأنَّ القضاء على الغائب لا يجوز؛ لأنَّ أخذه من يد البائع يوجب فوات المبيع قبل القبض، وفواتُه قبل القبض يوجب الفسخ ؛ لكونه قبل تمامه.

وقال الشافعي له : العهدة على المشتري بكل حال سواء يأخذها من يد البائع أو من يد المشتري ؛ لأن عنده حقوق العبد ترجع الى المالك. كذا في «الكفاية» (٤) ، قاله جلبي
And he does not hear the evidence against him until the buyer is present, then he rescinds (the sale) in his presence and passes judgment for the shafi' with shuf'a, and the guarantee (uhdah) is on the seller. [1] (And he does not hear the evidence against him until the buyer is present, then he rescinds in his presence.) The presence of the seller and buyer is required because ownership is for him and possession is for the seller. When he (the seller) has delivered to the buyer, the presence of the seller is not required because he has become a stranger. And he passes judgment for the shafi' with shuf'a, and the guarantee is on the seller, such that the delivery of the house is obligatory on the seller, and upon a claim (of defect) the guarantee of the price is on the seller, so it is demanded from him. [1] His saying: presence of the seller and buyer... etc.; there is no alternative but their assembly, because judgment against an absent person is not permissible; because taking it from the seller's hand causes loss of the sold item before delivery, and its loss before delivery necessitates rescission, as it is before completion. Just as if it perished before delivery, and rescission is not permissible against them (both) unless they are present, contrary to after delivery, where the presence of the seller is not required because the contract has ended with delivery and the seller has become a stranger to them. Likewise in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. [2] His saying: and the guarantee is on the seller... etc.; meaning before delivery of the sold item to the buyer. As for afterwards, there is no doubt that the guarantee is on the buyer, as clarified in al-Hidayah. Therefore, the author's expression is not free from some deficiency, so let one reflect. And al-Shafi'i said: The guarantee is on the buyer in all cases, whether he takes it from the seller's hand or the buyer's hand, because according to him the rights of the slave (i.e., the buyer) revert to the owner (the seller). Likewise in al-Kifaya, said by Jalabi.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on shuf'a procedure, specifically the requirement of the buyer's presence for the judge to hear evidence and pass judgment. The sharh explains that both seller and buyer must be present when the property is still with the seller, because the judge's action of taking the property from the seller's hand causes a loss before delivery, which is akin to destruction before delivery and necessitates rescission. Such rescission cannot be done in the absence of either party. However, if the buyer has already taken possession, the seller is a stranger and need not attend. The second part addresses the guarantee (uhdah): it is on the seller before delivery, and on the buyer after. The commentator notes a deficiency in the matn's wording that might imply otherwise. Finally, an inter-madhhab dispute is recorded: Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the guarantee is always on the buyer, regardless of possession, because in his school all rights of the buyer revert to the seller (i.e., the buyer is considered the owner from the contract). The Hanafi position distinguishes based on possession status.

Key Terms
عهدة (uhdah): Guarantee or warranty against defects and claims on the sold property.
قضاء على الغائب (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib): Passing judgment against an absent party without their presence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The seller.
B) The buyer.
C) The shafi'.
D) The judge.

Key Takeaway
Before delivery, guarantee is on the seller and both parties must be present; after delivery, guarantee shifts to the buyer and only the buyer need attend.
