The Pre-emptor's Options of Sight and Defect: Not Waivable by Buyer's Stipulation
Establishes shafi's options and their immunity to buyer's waiver

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the pre-emptor's options of sight and defect and explain why the buyer's stipulation of immunity does not waive them.

Core Idea
The shafi' has the same options as a buyer; the buyer cannot waive them because the shafi' is not his agent.

Pre-emption (shuf'ah) grants a co-owner the right to purchase another co-owner's share sold to a third party. A key question is whether the pre-emptor (shafi') can later cancel the pre-emption if he sees the property or discovers a defect. This passage confirms that the shafi' retains these options even if the buyer stipulated immunity. The reasoning is based on the analogy between pre-emption and an ordinary sale, and the principle that the shafi' acts independently.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the exact ruling: the shafi' has the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'ya) and option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb). These options exist even if the buyer stipulates immunity from them.
The ruling is unconditional; the buyer's stipulation has no effect on the shafi's rights.
2
Identify the analogy (qiyas)
Recognise that the text compares pre-emption to a sale: 'taking by pre-emption is akin to purchase.' The legal basis (illah) is the exchange of property for property (mubadala al-mal bi al-mal).
This illah is the same as in ordinary sales, so the same options apply.
3
Derive the options for pre-emption
Since pre-emption is an exchange, it shares the same khiyarat (options) as sale. Therefore, the shafi' can annul if he sees the property (if unseen at contract) or if a defect exists.
These options are established by qiyas jali (clear analogy).
4
Explain why the buyer's stipulation does not waive the options
The buyer is a separate party; the shafi' is not his agent (na'ib). Therefore, the buyer cannot forfeit the shafi's independent rights through a stipulation in his own contract with the seller.
This is a key principle: the shafi's rights are not derivative of the buyer's contract.
5
Note the authority
The passage cites al-Hidayah, confirming this as the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school.
Authority weight: zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللشفيع خيار الرؤيةِ والعيب ، وإن شرط المشتري البراءة عنه. قوله : وللشفيع ... الخ ; لأن الأخذ بالشفعة بمنزلة الشراء، ألا يرى أنه مبادلة المال بالمال، فيثبت فيه الخياران كما في الشراء، ولا يسقط بشرط البراءة من المشتري ، ولا برؤيته ; لأنه ليس بنائب عنه، فلا يملك إسقاطه. كذا في «الهداية».
And the pre-emptor has the option of sight and defect, even if the buyer stipulates immunity from it. His saying: 'And for the pre-emptor... etc.' because taking by pre-emption is akin to purchase. Do you not see that it is an exchange of property for property? So both options are established as in purchase, and they are not nullified by the buyer's stipulation of immunity, nor by his (the pre-emptor's) sight, because the pre-emptor is not his (the buyer's) agent, so he does not have the power to forfeit it. As in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This is a ruling statement about the rights of the pre-emptor (shafi'). The text first states that the shafi' has the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'ya) and the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb), meaning he can annul the pre-emption if he sees the property or discovers a defect, similar to a buyer. This right remains even if the buyer stipulates immunity from such options. The commentary explains the reasoning: pre-emption is essentially a purchase, being an exchange of property for property, so all options of a sale apply. The buyer cannot waive these options on behalf of the shafi' because the shafi' is not the buyer's agent. Thus the options are firmly established.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option to annul a contract (here pre-emption) after seeing the subject matter if it was unseen at contract.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option to annul a contract due to a defect present in the subject matter at the time of contract.
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): Pre-emption: the right of a co-owner to purchase the share of another co-owner when sold to a third party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because pre-emption is a gift, not a sale.
B) Because the shafi' is not the buyer's agent, so he cannot waive the option.
C) Because the buyer is the owner of the property.
D) Because the option is a special right given by the judge.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the shafi' retains the options of sight and defect irrespective of any waiver by the buyer, because pre-emption is analogous to a direct purchase.

Buyer's Word Taken in Pre-emption Price Dispute
Fixes credibility in price disputes between shafi' and buyer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on price disputes between the shafi' and buyer and explain why the buyer's word is taken with oath.

Core Idea
In a price dispute with the shafi', the buyer's word is taken with oath — he is the defendant, not a contractual counterparty.

When a pre-emptor (shafi') claims to take property at a lower price than what the buyer paid, the buyer may dispute the stated price. This passage clarifies whose statement is given legal weight. The Hanafi school, following the principle that the claimant bears the burden of proof, accepts the buyer's testimony with an oath. Let's analyse the reasoning and the alternative of mutual oaths (tahaluf).

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the dispute
Recognize that the shafi' claims the property was sold at a lower price, while the buyer insists on a higher price.
The shafi' is not a party to the original sale.
2
Determine claimant and defendant
Apply the legal maxim 'al-bayyinah 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yamin 'ala man ankara' (the claimant must prove, the defendant swears). The shafi' is the claimant because he is asserting a right to take at a lower price.
The buyer is in possession and denies the lower price.
3
Understand why mutual oaths (tahaluf) are not required
Tahaluf is for disputes between direct parties to a sale. Here, the shafi' is an outsider claiming a right, so the general rule applies: the defendant's oath is enough.
The text states 'no text (nass) here' mandating tahaluf.
4
Note the commentator's expansion
The commentator adds that the buyer is believed 'with an oath' (ma'a al-hilf), making explicit the oath requirement implied in tasdiq.
This is consistent with Hanafi procedure.
5
Extract the ruling
The final ruling: the buyer's word is accepted with an oath; the shafi' loses if he cannot prove the lower price.
This is the mufta bihi position from al-Hidayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن اختلف الشفيع والمشتري في الثَّمن صُدِّقَ المشتري. قوله: صدق المشتري؛ أي صدق المشتري مع الحلف كما قال الشارح ولا يتحالفان; لأن الشفيع إن كان يدعي عليه استحقاق الدار، فالمشتري لا يدعي عليه شبهة لتخيره من الترك والأخذ، ولا نص هاهنا فلا يتحالفان. كذا في الهداية.
And if the pre-emptor and the buyer differ over the price, the buyer is believed. His saying: 'The buyer is believed' means he is believed with an oath, as the commentator said, and they do not take mutual oaths (tahaluf). Because the pre-emptor claims entitlement to the property upon payment of the lower price, but the buyer denies that. There is no text (explicit ruling) here, so they do not take mutual oaths. As in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This is a ruling on evidence when the shafi' and buyer dispute the price. The rule is that the buyer's word is taken, but he must swear an oath. The reasoning is that the shafi' is making a claim (da'wa) that he can take the property at a lower price, while the buyer denies that. Since the buyer is the possessor and the shafi' is the claimant, the burden of proof falls on the shafi'. The buyer's oath suffices. The commentary clarifies that mutual oaths (tahaluf) are not required here because there is no explicit text mandating them; instead, standard rules of evidence apply.

Key Terms
تصديق (tasdiq): Accepting someone's statement as true, specifically the buyer's word in a price dispute with the shafi'.
تحالف (tahaluf): Mutual oaths: when both parties swear, usually in sales disputes. Not applied here.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The shafi''s word.
B) The buyer's word with oath.
C) Both swear and split the difference.
D) The judge sets the market price.

Key Takeaway
In a price dispute between the shafi' and the buyer, the buyer's word with oath is accepted; the shafi' is the claimant and must prove the lower price.

Resolving the Shafi' v Buyer Evidence Conflict: The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Resolves conflict of evidence between shafi' and buyer in pre-emption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the basis of the Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute when both shafi' and buyer present conflicting price evidence, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
When shafi' and buyer both present price evidence, two sales can be imagined, so shafi' gets the lower price.

Pre-emption gives the shafi' the right to step into the buyer's shoes. But what happens when the shafi' and buyer disagree on the price and both have witnesses? The Hanafi school has an internal difference: the majority allows the shafi' to take at the lower price by assuming two sales occurred, while Abu Yusuf gives priority to the buyer's more confirmatory evidence. This lesson unpacks the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi majority position
Identify the ruling from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani: the shafi' takes at the lower price when both parties present evidence.
2
Identify the opposing view
Abu Yusuf holds that the buyer's evidence should be preferred because it is more confirmatory (akthar ithbatan), so the higher price prevails.
3
Follow the majority's proof
The majority argue that both evidences can be truthful if we imagine two separate sales—one at each price. Since the shafi' has a strong right, this reconciliation is possible.
4
Analyze Abu Yusuf's criticism
Abu Yusuf analogizes to the seller-buyer dispute: only one sale is possible, so if evidence conflicts, the more confirmatory is preferred. For him, the same logic should apply here.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under the majority view, shafi' pays the lower price; under Abu Yusuf, he pays the higher price. The mufta bihi is the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو برهنا، فالشفيع أحق. هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد ، وحجتهما أن ما ذكرنا، وأيضاً : يمكن صدق البينتين بجريان العقدِ مرَّتين، فيأخذ الشفيع بالأقل، وعند أبي يوسف بينةُ المشتري أحقُّ لأنها أكثر إثباتاً. قوله : وأيضاً ; وهذا دليل ثان لهما، وتلخيصه ; لأنه لا تنافي بين البينتين في حق الشفيع الجواز تحقق البيع مرّة بألف وأخرى بألفين على ما شهد عليه البينتان، وفسخ أحدهما بالآخر لا يظهر في حق الشفيع، لتأكد حقه، فيجمع بينهما، فجاز أن يجعله موجودين في حقه وله أن يأخذ بأيهما شاء. وهذا بخلاف البائع والمشتري ; لأنهما لا يتوالى بينهما عقدان إلا بانفساخ الأوّل فالجمع بينهما غير ممكن، فيصار إلى أكثرهما إثباتاً ; لأنّ المصير إلى الترجيح عند تعذر التوفيق.
And if both have evidence, then the pre-emptor is more entitled. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Their proof is what we mentioned, and also: it is possible for both evidences to be true by the occurrence of the contract twice, so the pre-emptor takes at the lower price. According to Abu Yusuf, the buyer's evidence is superior because it is more confirmatory. His saying: 'And also' is a second proof for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Its summary: there is no contradiction between the two evidences with respect to the pre-emptor because it is possible that a sale occurred once for one thousand and another time for two thousand as the two evidences attest, and the cancellation of one by the other does not appear with respect to the pre-emptor due to the strength of his right. So they are combined, and it is permissible to consider both as existing for his right, and he may take whichever he wishes. This is unlike the seller and buyer because they cannot have two consecutive contracts without the first being rescinded, so combining them is not possible; thus the one that is more confirmatory is preferred, because recourse is had to preference when reconciliation is impossible.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf fi al-madhhab) about what happens when both the shafi' and the buyer present evidence (bayyinah) regarding the price. The ruling is that the shafi' is entitled to take the property at the lower price according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Their reasoning: the two conflicting pieces of evidence can both be truthful if the property was sold twice—once at the lower price and once at the higher. Since the shafi' has a strong right, he can choose the lower price. Abu Yusuf disagrees: he holds the buyer's evidence superior because it proves more (i.e., a higher price), and in case of conflict, the more confirmatory evidence should be given preference, just as in a dispute between seller and buyer where only one sale can be true. The commentary explains that the difference stems from whether multiple sales can be imagined for the shafi'. Since the shafi' is not a participant in the sale, two separate sales can be envisioned without contradiction, allowing him to take at the lower price. This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence: here refers to witnesses testifying to the price in a pre-emption case.
ترجيح (tarjih): Preferring one evidence over another when reconciliation is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of the Hanafi school, when both the shafi' and the buyer present evidence of different prices for the same property, what is the ruling?
A) The buyer's evidence is preferred because it is more confirmatory.
B) The shafi' may take the property at the lower price.
C) The judge must determine which evidence is more truthful.
D) The shafi' is not entitled to any property if evidence conflicts.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when shafi' and buyer both produce evidence of different prices, the shafi' is given the option to take at the lower price because two sales can be imagined, reconciling the evidence.

Price Credibility and Special Cases in Shuf'a
Rulings on price determination in shuf'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rules of price determination in shuf'a when the seller and buyer dispute, when the seller remits the price, and when the price is fungible, non-fungible, or deferred.

Core Idea
The shafi' pays the full price even if the seller remits it; price disputes are resolved by the seller's word before payment and the buyer's word after.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies several practical scenarios in shuf'a (pre-emption) regarding the price the shafi' must pay. Understanding who to believe in a price dispute, the effect of a price remission by the seller, and how to handle different types of prices (fungible, non-fungible, deferred) is essential for correctly applying the right of pre-emption.

Guided Path
1
Identify Each Scenario and Its Ruling
Read the Arabic text and list each distinct case: price dispute before payment, price dispute after payment, seller's full remission, purchase with fungible price, purchase with non-fungible price, exchange of land, deferred price, and silence of the shafi'.
Each scenario has a clear ruling; note the key phrases like 'f-al-qawl li-l-ba'i' and 'f-al-qawl li-l-mushtari'.
2
Analyze the Reasoning for Price Disputes
Consider why the seller's word is taken before payment (default possession of the property) and the buyer's word after payment (the buyer is now in possession and the seller has no claim).
This is based on the principle that the party in possession is credible unless evidence contradicts.
3
Explain the Effect of Price Remission
Understand that when the seller remits the entire price (hatt al-kull), the shafi' still pays the full price because the remission is a contract between seller and buyer only; the shafi' is not a party to it.
The shafi' steps into the buyer's shoes but does not inherit the remission.
4
Distinguish Between Fungible and Non-Fungible Prices
Identify that for a fungible price (thaman mithli), the shafi' must give the same type and quantity; for non-fungible, he gives the market value. For land exchange, each party gives the value of the other's land.
This ensures that the shafi' does not gain or lose due to price type differences.
5
Understand Deferred Price Options and Consequences
The shafi' may pay immediately and take the property, or request the property now and pay after the term. If he remains silent (no action), his right is lost.
Silence is considered abandonment of the right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ادعى المشتري ثمناً ، وبائعُهُ أقل منه بلا قبضه فالقول له. قوله : فالقول للبائع . ومع قبضه المشتري . قوله : فالقول للمشتري . وأخذ في حط الكلِّ بالكلِّ. وفي الشراء بثمن مثلي بمثله، وفي غيره بالقيمة، وفي عقار بعقار أُخِذَ كل بقيمة الآخر. وفي ثمن مؤجل بحال أو طَلَبَ في الحال وأخذ بعد الأجل. ولو سكت عنه، بطلت.
And if the buyer claims a price, and his seller (claims) less than it without the buyer having paid it, then the word is for him (the seller). His saying: 'The word is for the seller.' And with the buyer having paid it, (the word is for the buyer). His saying: 'The word is for the buyer.' And taking (by the shafi') in case of complete remission (of the price by the seller) is at the full price. And in a purchase for a fungible price, (the shafi' takes) with its like; in other cases, with its value; in (exchange of) land for land, each is taken at the value of the other. And in (the case of) a deferred price, (the shafi' may take) immediately, or request (the property) immediately and take after the term. And if he remains silent about it, his right is nullified.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This lesson presents several rulings related to price determination in pre-emption. The first scenario: if the buyer and seller dispute the price before the price is paid, the seller's word is accepted. After payment, the buyer's word is accepted. Next, if the seller fully remits the price (hittat al-kull), the shafi' must pay the full price because remission does not affect the shafi' (the debt is not cancelled in his favor). When the price is fungible (mithli), the shafi' gives the same type; otherwise, he gives the market value. In an exchange of land for land, each party takes the value of the other's land. If the price is deferred, the shafi' can either pay immediately and take possession, or request the property now and pay later, but if he remains silent, his right is lost. These are all applications of the principle that the shafi' steps into the buyer's shoes but with certain adjustments.

Key Terms
حط الكل (hatt al-kull): Complete remission of the price by the seller; the shafi' still pays the full price.
ثمن مثلي (thaman mithli): A price that is fungible, i.e., measured or weighed substances (like gold, silver, grains).
ثمن مؤجل (thaman mu'ajjal): Deferred price: payment due at a future date.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if the seller remits the entire price after the sale?
A) The shafi' takes the property for free.
B) The shafi' must pay the full price to the seller.
C) The shafi' pays half the price.
D) The shafi' loses his right.

Key Takeaway
The shafi' pays the price determined by dispute rules, remission does not relieve him, and he must act promptly or lose the right.

Price Disputes in Shuf'ah: Who Is Believed?
Price dispute rule with reduction scenario.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for whose statement is accepted when the buyer and seller dispute the price in a shuf'ah case, before and after receipt of the price, and apply it to a practical scenario.

Core Idea
If the seller hasn't received the price, his word prevails; after receipt, the buyer's word is taken.

The pre-emptor steps into the buyer's shoes, so the actual price paid determines his liability. But when the buyer and seller disagree on the price, which one is believed? The Hanafi school draws a clear distinction based on whether the seller has already received the payment. This lesson explains the rule and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed element
Recognize that the dispute is between the buyer and seller over the actual price paid for the property subject to pre-emption.
2
Determine receipt status
Establish whether the seller has received the price from the buyer at the time of litigation. The rule changes depending on this fact.
3
Apply the rule
If the seller has not received the price, his statement is accepted. If he has received it, the buyer's statement is accepted.
The rationale is that the seller is more knowledgeable about the price before receipt, while the buyer knows after payment.
4
Understand the reduction scenario
If the seller reduces the price for the buyer (hatt), the pre-emptor takes at the full original price, not the reduced amount, because the reduction is a separate transaction.
This is similar to the rule in murabahah: the pre-emptor takes the lesser of the two prices in certain cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من كتاب الشفعة: وإن ادعى المشتري ثمناً، وبائعه أقل منه بلا قبضه فالقول له ومع قبضه المشتري وأخذ في حط الكل بالكل وفي الشراء بثمن مثلي بمثله، وفي غيره بالقيمة، وفي عقار بعقار أخذ كل بقيمة الآخر، وفي ثمن مؤجل بحال أو طلب في الحال وأخذ بعد الأجل، ولو سكت عنه، بطلت. (وإن ادعى المشتري ثمناً، وبائعه أقل منه بلا قبضه فالقول له) : أي بلا قبض الثمن، فالقول للبائع، (ومع قبضه المشتري) : أي مع قبض الثمن، فالقول للمشتري، (وأخذ في حط الكل بالكل) ، مسألة حط البعض قد مرت في «باب المرابحة» بقوله : والشفيع يأخذ بالأقل في الفصلين. (وفي الشراء بثمن مثلي بمثله، وفي غيره بالقيمة، وفي عقار بعقار أخذ كل بقيمة الآخر) ، وفي ثمن مؤجل بحال أو طلب في الحال وأخذ بعد الأجل، هذا عندنا، وأما عند زفر والشافعي له في قوله القديم، فله أن يأخذه في الحال بالثمن المؤجل، (ولو سكت عنه، بطلت) : أي إن سكت عن الطلب. وصفبر حتى يطلب عند الأجل بطلت شفعته. وفي شراء ذمي بخمر أو خنزير، والشفيع ذمي بمثل الخمر وقيمة الخنزير، والشفيع المسلم بقيمة كل. وفي بناء المشتري وغرسه بالثمن وقيمة البناء والغرس مقلوعين، كما في الغصب، وإن شاء كلف المشتري قلعهما : أي أخذ الشفيع فيما إذا بنى المشتري أو غرس بالثمن وقيمتهما مقلوعين، أو كلف المشتري قلع البناء أو الغرس، والمراد بقيمتهما مقلوعين قيمتهما مستحقي القلع، كما مر في «الغصب»، وعن أبي يوسف له : أنه لا يكلف بالقلع، بل يخير بين أن يأخذ. (قوله: بطلت شفعته... الخ؛ وقد روى ابن أبي مالك عن أبي يوسف له إنه كان يقول أولاً كقولهما، وهو ما ذكر في الكتاب، ثم رجع وقال : له أن يأخذها عند حلول الأجل، وإن لم يطلب في الحال؛ لأن الطلب إنما هو بلا أخذ. وهو في الحال لا يتمكن منه على الوجه الذي يطلبه؛ لأنه إنما يريد الأخذ بعد حلول الأجل أو ثمن مؤجل في الحال لا يتمكن من ذلك، فلا فائدة في طلبه في الحال؛ لسكوته لعدم الفائدة في الطلب لا لإعراضه عن الأخذ. قاله جلبي. (قوله: والشفيع ذمي؛ قال في «العناية»: هذا احتراز عما إذا كان مرتداً فإنه لا شفعة له، سواء قتل على الردة أو مات أو لحق بدار الحرب، ولا ورثة له؛ لأنها لا تورث. قاله جلبي. (قوله: بقيمة كل... الخ؛ أما الخنزير فظاهر، فإنه من ذوات القيم، وكذا الخمر لامتناع التسليم والتسلم في حق المسلم، فالتحق بغير المثلي.
From the book of pre-emption: And if the buyer claims a price, and his seller claims less than it, without receipt (of the price), then the statement is (the seller's). And with the buyer's receipt (of the price), and he takes in the reduction of the whole for the whole. And in the purchase with a similar price by its like, and in other than it by value, and in real estate for real estate each is taken by the value of the other. And regarding a deferred price, (he takes) immediately or he demands immediately and takes after the term. And if he is silent about it, (the pre-emption) becomes void. (And if the buyer claims a price, and his seller less than it without receipt, then the statement is his): i.e., without receipt of the price, then the statement is the seller's. (And with the buyer's receipt): i.e., with receipt of the price, then the statement is the buyer's. (And he takes in the reduction of the whole for the whole): the issue of partial reduction has already passed in the chapter of Murabahah by his statement: the pre-emptor takes the lesser in the two cases. (And in the purchase with a similar price by its like, and in other than it by value, and in real estate for real estate each is taken by the value of the other): and regarding a deferred price, (he takes) immediately or he demands immediately and takes after the term. This is our (Hanafi) position. As for Zufar and al-Shafi'i, in his old opinion, he may take it immediately with the deferred price. (And if he is silent about it, it becomes void): i.e., if he is silent about the demand. And he waits until he demands at the term, his pre-emption becomes void. And in the purchase by a dhimmi with wine or pig, and the pre-emptor (is) a dhimmi: with the like of wine and value of the pig; and the Muslim pre-emptor: with the value of each. And regarding the buyer's building and planting: with the price and value of the building and planting as uprooted, as in usurpation; and if he wishes, the buyer is tasked with uprooting them: i.e., the pre-emptor takes in the case where the buyer built or planted with the price and their value as uprooted, or the buyer is tasked with removing the building or planting. And the meaning of their value as uprooted is their value as deserving of removal, as has passed in the chapter of usurpation. And from Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him): that he is not tasked with removal, but rather he chooses between taking. (His statement: his pre-emption becomes void... etc.; and Ibn Abi Malik related from Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) that he initially used to say like their (the Sahibayn) statement, which is what is mentioned in the book, then he retracted and said: he may take it at the time of the term, even if he did not demand immediately, because demand is only without taking. And he cannot achieve it immediately in the manner he desires, because he only intends to take after the term or a deferred price; immediately he cannot achieve that, so there is no benefit in demanding immediately; his silence is due to lack of benefit in demand, not due to abandoning the taking. Jabiri said it. (His statement: and the pre-emptor is a dhimmi; he said in al-'Inayah: this is a precaution against the case of an apostate, for he has no pre-emption, whether he is killed for apostasy, dies, or joins the war zone, and he has no heirs, because it (pre-emption) is not inherited. Jabiri said it. (His statement: with the value of each... etc.; as for the pig, it is clear, for it is from the category of valuables, and likewise wine, due to the impossibility of delivery and receipt for a Muslim, so it is annexed to non-mithli items.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings on how the pre-emptor (shafi') takes the property (mashfu') in various scenarios. The text begins by establishing a key principle: when there is a dispute between the buyer and seller regarding the actual price paid, and the pre-emptor wants to take the property, the statement of the one who has not yet received the price is trusted. Thus, if the seller has not yet taken the price, his word is taken over the buyer's; if the seller has already received the price, the buyer's word is taken. This is because the pre-emptor is essentially stepping into the buyer's shoes, and the actual price determines his liability. Next, the text addresses the case where the seller reduces (hatt) the entire price for the buyer. In such a case, the pre-emptor takes the property at the full original price, not the reduced amount, because the reduction is considered a separate transaction that does not affect the original sale. Then, the text discusses transactions where the price is a similar item (mithli), such as wheat for wheat, where the pre-emptor pays in kind; if the price is non-mithli (qimiyy), he pays its cash value. If two properties are exchanged, each pre-emptor pays the value of the other property. For a deferred price (thaman mu'ajjal), the pre-emptor has the option to either take the property immediately by paying the full deferred price in cash, or demand the pre-emption immediately but postpone payment until the term. If he remains silent without demanding, his right to pre-emption becomes void. This is the Hanafi position; Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i (in his old view) hold that the pre-emptor can take immediately without demanding, paying the deferred price at its present value. Then, the text turns to cases involving a dhimmi buyer. If a dhimmi buys property in exchange for wine or pigs, the pre-emptor, if also a dhimmi, takes the property by paying the like of wine (i.e., an equivalent amount of wine) and the value of the pig (since pigs are not quantified by like); if the pre-emptor is a Muslim, he must pay the cash value for both because Muslims cannot own or transact in wine or pigs. Finally, the text addresses improvements made by the buyer, such as buildings or plants. The pre-emptor may either pay for these improvements at their value as uprooted (i.e., after removal) and take the property, or force the buyer to remove them at his own expense. This is similar to the rules in usurpation (ghasb). A later narration from Abu Yusuf indicates that he retracted his earlier view and held that the buyer should not be forced to remove improvements; instead, the pre-emptor chooses between taking with compensation or leaving the improvements. Additionally, the commentary notes that a non-Muslim pre-emptor cannot be an apostate, as an apostate has no right of pre-emption, nor can he inherit it. The text also clarifies why wine is treated like pig: for a Muslim, delivery and receipt of wine are impossible, so it falls under the category of valuables (qimiyyat) rather than similar (mithliyat).

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of a pre-emptor to take a property sold to another by paying the price and compensating for improvements.
حط (hatt): Reduction of the price by the seller for the buyer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a shuf'ah case, if the seller and buyer dispute the price and the seller has not yet received the payment, whose statement is accepted?
A) The buyer's statement
B) The seller's statement
C) The pre-emptor's statement
D) A neutral appraiser

Key Takeaway
The accepted statement in a price dispute depends on whether the seller has received payment: before receipt, seller's; after receipt, buyer's.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Payment Methods in Shuf'ah: Mithli, Qimah, Real Estate, and Deferred Price
Rulings on how the pre-emptor pays based on price type and deferred payment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the appropriate payment method in shuf'ah (mithli, qimah, real estate exchange, and deferred price) and explain the Hanafi rule regarding immediate vs deferred payment, including the ikhtilaf with other schools.

Core Idea
The pre-emptor pays in kind for mithli, cash value for non-mithli, and for deferred price he must pay cash immediately or demand and wait.

The pre-emptor's obligation is to pay the price, but the form of payment varies based on what was exchanged in the original sale. If the price was a fungible (mithli), he pays in kind; if non-fungible, he pays market value. When two properties are exchanged, each pre-emptor pays the value of the other. For a deferred price, the Hanafi school requires immediate cash or early demand, unlike Zufar and al-Shafi'i (old opinion) who allow immediate taking on credit.

Guided Path
1
Classify the price in the original sale
Determine whether the price is mithli (fungible, measurable by weight/volume/count) or qimiyy (non-fungible).
2
Determine payment method for mithli
If the price was mithli, the pre-emptor pays the same kind and quantity.
E.g., if house was bought for 100 kg of wheat, he pays 100 kg of wheat.
3
Determine payment method for non-mithli
If the price was qimiyy (e.g., pig, wine, or unique item), the pre-emptor pays its market value (qimah).
4
Handle real estate exchange
If the sale was an exchange of two properties, each pre-emptor pays the value of the other property.
This applies when both parties have pre-emption rights.
5
Apply the rule for deferred price
If the price was deferred (credit sale), the pre-emptor has two options: pay the full deferred price in cash immediately, or demand the pre-emption now but pay only after the term. Silence without demand voids the right.
This is the Hanafi position; Zufar and al-Shafi'i (old view) allow immediate taking with the deferred price.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من كتاب الشفعة: وإن ادعى المشتري ثمناً، وبائعه أقل منه بلا قبضه فالقول له ومع قبضه المشتري وأخذ في حط الكل بالكل وفي الشراء بثمن مثلي بمثله، وفي غيره بالقيمة، وفي عقار بعقار أخذ كل بقيمة الآخر، وفي ثمن مؤجل بحال أو طلب في الحال وأخذ بعد الأجل، ولو سكت عنه، بطلت. (وإن ادعى المشتري ثمناً، وبائعه أقل منه بلا قبضه فالقول له) : أي بلا قبض الثمن، فالقول للبائع، (ومع قبضه المشتري) : أي مع قبض الثمن، فالقول للمشتري، (وأخذ في حط الكل بالكل) ، مسألة حط البعض قد مرت في «باب المرابحة» بقوله : والشفيع يأخذ بالأقل في الفصلين. (وفي الشراء بثمن مثلي بمثله، وفي غيره بالقيمة، وفي عقار بعقار أخذ كل بقيمة الآخر) ، وفي ثمن مؤجل بحال أو طلب في الحال وأخذ بعد الأجل، هذا عندنا، وأما عند زفر والشافعي له في قوله القديم، فله أن يأخذه في الحال بالثمن المؤجل، (ولو سكت عنه، بطلت) : أي إن سكت عن الطلب. وصفبر حتى يطلب عند الأجل بطلت شفعته. وفي شراء ذمي بخمر أو خنزير، والشفيع ذمي بمثل الخمر وقيمة الخنزير، والشفيع المسلم بقيمة كل. وفي بناء المشتري وغرسه بالثمن وقيمة البناء والغرس مقلوعين، كما في الغصب، وإن شاء كلف المشتري قلعهما : أي أخذ الشفيع فيما إذا بنى المشتري أو غرس بالثمن وقيمتهما مقلوعين، أو كلف المشتري قلع البناء أو الغرس، والمراد بقيمتهما مقلوعين قيمتهما مستحقي القلع، كما مر في «الغصب»، وعن أبي يوسف له : أنه لا يكلف بالقلع، بل يخير بين أن يأخذ. (قوله: بطلت شفعته... الخ؛ وقد روى ابن أبي مالك عن أبي يوسف له إنه كان يقول أولاً كقولهما، وهو ما ذكر في الكتاب، ثم رجع وقال : له أن يأخذها عند حلول الأجل، وإن لم يطلب في الحال؛ لأن الطلب إنما هو بلا أخذ. وهو في الحال لا يتمكن منه على الوجه الذي يطلبه؛ لأنه إنما يريد الأخذ بعد حلول الأجل أو ثمن مؤجل في الحال لا يتمكن من ذلك، فلا فائدة في طلبه في الحال؛ لسكوته لعدم الفائدة في الطلب لا لإعراضه عن الأخذ. قاله جلبي. Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings on how the pre-emptor (shafi') takes the property (mashfu') in various scenarios. The text begins by establishing a key principle: when there is a dispute between the buyer and seller regarding the actual price paid, and the pre-emptor wants to take the property, the statement of the one who has not yet received the price is trusted. Thus, if the seller has not yet taken the price, his word is taken over the buyer's; if the seller has already received the price, the buyer's word is taken. This is because the pre-emptor is essentially stepping into the buyer's shoes, and the actual price determines his liability. Next, the text addresses the case where the seller reduces (hatt) the entire price for the buyer. In such a case, the pre-emptor takes the property at the full original price, not the reduced amount, because the reduction is considered a separate transaction that does not affect the original sale. Then, the text discusses transactions where the price is a similar item (mithli), such as wheat for wheat, where the pre-emptor pays in kind; if the price is non-mithli (qimiyy), he pays its cash value. If two properties are exchanged, each pre-emptor pays the value of the other property. For a deferred price (thaman mu'ajjal), the pre-emptor has the option to either take the property immediately by paying the full deferred price in cash, or demand the pre-emption immediately but postpone payment until the term. If he remains silent without demanding, his right to pre-emption becomes void. This is the Hanafi position; Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i (in his old view) hold that the pre-emptor can take immediately without demanding, paying the deferred price at its present value. Then, the text turns to cases involving a dhimmi buyer. If a dhimmi buys property in exchange for wine or pigs, the pre-emptor, if also a dhimmi, takes the property by paying the like of wine (i.e., an equivalent amount of wine) and the value of the pig (since pigs are not quantified by like); if the pre-emptor is a Muslim, he must pay the cash value for both because Muslims cannot own or transact in wine or pigs. Finally, the text addresses improvements made by the buyer, such as buildings or plants. The pre-emptor may either pay for these improvements at their value as uprooted (i.e., after removal) and take the property, or force the buyer to remove them at his own expense. This is similar to the rules in usurpation (ghasb). A later narration from Abu Yusuf indicates that he retracted his earlier view and held that the buyer should not be forced to remove improvements; instead, the pre-emptor chooses between taking with compensation or leaving the improvements. Additionally, the commentary notes that a non-Muslim pre-emptor cannot be an apostate, as an apostate has no right of pre-emption, nor can he inherit it. The text also clarifies why wine is treated like pig: for a Muslim, delivery and receipt of wine are impossible, so it falls under the category of valuables (qimiyyat) rather than similar (mithliyat).

Key Terms
مثلي (mithli): A commodity that can be substituted by similar items (like wheat, silver) and is quantified by weight, measure, or count.
قيمة (qimah): The market value of an item that is not mithli, determined at the time of the contract.
ثمن مؤجل (thaman mu'ajjal): A price that is payable at a future date, i.e., credit sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if the pre-emptor remains silent when the sale has a deferred price?
A) His pre-emption right is void
B) He can still take it at the term
C) The demand is automatically made
D) He must pay cash immediately

Key Takeaway
The pre-emptor's payment method matches the price type, but for a deferred price, immediate cash or active demand is required to avoid losing the right.
